The RED PAGE
Wanted—The Best Ten
On the outer frieze of the National Art
Gallery in Sydney there are the names of
some famous artists of the old world whose
works are not to be found inside. Perhaps the
authorities hoped the names would be an in-
spiration to the artists of the new world, or
perhaps they put them there as a guarantee
of good faith. It is certainly better to have
them than the name of the mayor or
governor who did the silver-trowel act on the
foundation stone. But Michael Angelo,
Rubens and the rest did not need the
advertisement.
If the official mind should suddenly
awaken to the fact that there are no names
of authors on the outside of the Mitchell
Library building, the chances are that Homer,
Shakespeare and other eminently safe per-
sonages would get the ad. The place for such
inscriptions, if any are wanted, is outside the
Public Library building. The Mitchell
Library is mainly Australian. It is already
the largest collection of Australiana ; and, in
time to come, will be a great national library.
If any names are placed outside this building
they should be Australian. The pioneers of
literature and art in Australia should receive
honor in their own country.
Will Bulletin readers suggest the names of
ten Australian writers or artists, living or
dead, who are worthy of being commemorated
in such a way—not necessarily men of Aus-
tralian birth, but those whose work was done
in Australia or is Australian in character ? Of
course, the Library trustees are not bound
to accept the suggestion.
The ten names should be placed in order
of merit, and your reasons for the selection
f.ven in a hundred words or thereabouts.
1 Is. will be paid to the first sender of the
list containing the names in the order
which agrees with or is nearest to that
of the first ten in the total vote; 10s. 6d.
to the second. Contributions printed will
be paid for. They should be sent (ad-
dressed “ The Editqr of the ‘ Red Page ’ ”)
so as to reach The Bulletin office before the
10th November next.
The City of Beautiful Night
Long years past, the story goes, a shark
and an alligator met in mortal combat near
where the water now laps the stairs of the
Customs House. Whether Soura, the alliga-
tor, or Baya, the shark, was victor history
telleth not; but through that meeting of the
monsters, Sourabaya, the principal commer-
cial centre of the Dutch East Indies, is said
to have derived its name. Personally, I shall
remember it as the City of Beautiful Night.
' The hot day under which the Dutch villas
show blinding white, and the dirty canal
waters crawl sluggard wise, closes suddenly :
The sun’s rim dips, the Dutch rush out;
With one stride comes the dark.
They rush out in lighted motors, in car-
riages, on bicycles and by the thousand in
hired “ dokkars,” which is the nearest
the Malay pronunciation can get to dog-
carts. So great a variety of wheeled
things I did not know existed. Such
a multiplicity of noises issuing from
the vehicles bespeak many inventions in
phonetics. The well-to-do native popula-
tion, including Chinese and Arabs, make up
a large proportion of the sounding throng
running through the garden streets, by star-
litten canals and under miles-long avenues
of tamarind trees. Possibly from their
equipages come the cruder cadences. Bells,
trumpets, cuckoo-calls, whistles, hoots,
whimpering shrieks and a musical stave from
some well-known air, all used as warnings
for the other fellow to have a care where
he is driving; for all travel at high speed
and the footpath is more often absent than
present. All the one-storeyed white villas
are lighted without on the deep, tiled veran-
dahs, or “ front galleries ” as the Dutch call
them. Not glass, but iron rods are in these
windows, shuttered during the day-time
heat. When a Dutch householder is not
drifting, or his wife shopping, they are taking
the air of the close, electric night, with
their family and guests seated on the
front gallery, under bronze hanging-lamps
against a background of climbing plants ;
or they may be playing Bridge in
the lighted garden. There will come
no breeze to stir the- flame of the
lamps. Or possibly they may be at one
of the open-air restaurants listening to
an Austrian or Hungarian band. Eight-
thirty is the usual dinner hour in Java,
but 9 o’clock is not uncommon. By 10,
after a good dinner, the Dutch have
retired and the streets are deserted save by
native pedestrians going their barefooted
way, and the travelling restaurant carrier
with his plates of native food, little oil lamp
and tiny charcoal stove. Native watchmen
keep ward over the white villas ; the hours,
struck out with clubs on hollowed logs,
resound with a sullen resonance through
the land and the tamarind-belted city, still
gleaming with lamps through all its garden
ways, is very soundly asleep.
There are hundreds of miles of roads made
cloister-like by the shade of gigantic tama-
rinds or kenaries. Under Dutch compul-
sion, fora hundred years the native rulers
have been making roads and planting them
with shade trees. Nature liberally waters the
Garden Island, the natives have con-
served the rains for irrigation, and dug wells,
and the birthrate is amazingly prolific. Four
lines from “ The Light of Asia ” fairly ob-
trude themselves for quotation :
For holy books teach when a man shall plant
Trees for the travellers’ shade, and dig a well
For folk’s comfort, and beget a son.
It shall be good for such after their death.
Office chairs of Dutch statesmen are
already creaking with the weight of thought
their occupants are taking for the day after
to-'morrow : what to do—with the com-
ing surplus population. Javais some 500 miles
long by 50 miles broad, and its population
is approximated at 32,000,000. The Dutch
might withdraw from the island for a year
and leave the cholera, native riders and
internal strife to solve the problem ; but no
one seems to have suggested this solution.
Java is no place for a poor white, and
Dutch surveillance is so strict that no dere-
lict has a chance of entrance. Amongst the
natives there can be no legitimate poverty
because their food is so cheap. A root of
tapioca, for instance, on which an adult
might subsist for a day, costs half a cent.
Five cents go to the coin that equals the
English penny. In the market place at
Sourabaya, an area that corresponds with
our Paddy’s Market, an average of 10,000
guelders are taken every day in cents and
half cents.
Ninety per cent, of the cattle in Java come
from Australia ; the rolls we ate in the hotel
were made from Australian flour, and the
butter, from which we turned aside in haste,
wasan Australian product. Butter throughout
the East is awful. Like Macbeth’s offence,
it smells to Heaven ; but residents, so far as
it is concerned, would seem to lack the
senses of smell and taste. It is scarcely
necessary to mention that the butter does
not arrive in that condition. To quote the old
beau in “ Florodora,” “ it’s the climate ; it’s
the climate ” that’s at fault. The Dutch
understand hotel management from roof to
basement, from soup to nuts—all but the
afternoon-tea faget of the business. At five
in the afternoon your tea, with a tiny saucer
of sugar and a little jug of hot condensed
milk, is put on the table that stands on the
verandah outside every bedroom door. Once
I asked for something to eat with my tea. I
was given a big chunk of bread and two little
mounds of plum jam and the awful butter.
Though I never touched any of these
“ extras, ’ they continued to be 6 o’clock
arrivals for the rest of the time we stayed at
the hotel. The Javanese “boy” who
attends you rarely does any independent
thinking. On one of the Dutch liners I one
morning told the Hassan who looked after
my cabin to let me have a bucket of hot
water at 11. Next morning, at -the
same hour, he also delivered one and
a brilliant smile to me as I sat on
the deck with my friends. But I
believe he was a new “ boy.” The trained
and tried servants at the Sirnpang and
the Hotel des Indes made us .unpatriotic-
ally mad with our Alien Restriction Act.
All the same, no wages could tempt a
Javanese to a colder country than his own.
Those coming here on the Dutch boats rarely
make a second trip. In fairness to our own
servitors, it must he said that one of them
is equal to about two and a half Javanese in
working power. And not until we stayed at
the des Indes, In Batavia, did we see a
Javanese quicken his leisurely movements.
The reason was the manager of that
hotel. He was as a well-bred whirlwind,
yrith eyes for everything and .everyone.
At the Sirnpang, our respective Ilassans
and Mohamecs slept on grass mats, with
a dandy little red or other-hued pillow
apiece, on the verandahs outside our rooms.
They seem to sleep easily anywhere so long
as it’s not on the ground. In the bazaars I
have seen a native, sleeping soundly on a
foot-wide board slanted on a kerosene case.
Two bamboos, somewhat thicker than a man’s
wrist, sloped on an upturned wheelbarrow,
was another native’s couch.
\ Javanese, from coolie to prince, love dis-
play. A “boy”—quite an old boy for a
Javanese—in the service of a Dutch friend
of ours, had been for many years a mandor,
or, as we would say, head waiter. His wage
would be some 30 or 40 guelders a month.
When he had sedulously saved his earnings
during all the years of his service he left his
job and spent everything on a great feast to
his kampong or village. Then he took ser-
vice again as a mere “ boy ” at the (about)
usual rate of 12 gueldersa'month. “Doyou
not regret,” his employer asked him,
“ wasting all \your savings on that foolish
feast and having to begin all over again ? ”
The native’s eyes flashed at remembrance of
the revel, “ Oh, no ; I shall never regret
it,” he replied. “It is now three years since
I gave the feast, and the kampong talks of
it yet. It will be talked of while I lhe.”
The charm of Sourabaya for the stranger
is summed up in the kaleidoscopic life, Dutch
and native, of its commercial streets, its open-
air restaurants, the pageantry made up of irri-
gation and agriculture where rice, sugar-cane
and tapioca are cultivated; and the evergreen-
ness and beauty of its jungle hedges—cocoa-
nut palms, and bananas, and bamboos so thick
that they are utilised for drain pipes and the
carrying of water, and bamboos slender and
feathery, yet so tall that they suggest that
they are sweeping the cobwebs out of the sky.
A. CONOR O’BRIEN.
The Best Sellers
“People read more fiction than anything
else, and women read more than men.
Hence the most popular books are novels
which appeal most strongly to women. A
strong plot, plenty of sentiment, some
humor and a happy ending—that’s what they
like as a steady thing.” This, from a Syd-
ney bookseller, is probably true for Aus-
tralia,
“ The author’s name does not sell a book
nowadays,” said a Bookstall manager of long
experience. “Of course, the success of one
book will bring inquiries for the author’s
next; but if it is not up to the mark the
word is quickly passed round, and down it
goes. Even Kipling’s name did not make
much of a sale for Actions and Reactions.
There is still a big demand for Wells’s Ann
V&ronica, partly because of its emphatic sex
interest ; but there is -very little sale for
Mr. Polly. Some writers keep an even level
and sell all the time, such as Corelli, Caine,
Haggard and Pemberton. But new writers
have every chance of topping the sales if
they supply the stuff that the public wants.
Occasionally an Australian book has a very
good run.”
All the leading bookshops and libraries in
Sydney say that the book most in demand
just now is The Rosary, by Florence Barclay.
The writer, apparently English, is not a
new hand, but is hardly known, and this
book bad no preliminary puffs.
The story is a sentimental one with very
little action—the romance of a plain woman
and a blind man. It begins in this way:
Oh. I. Enter —The Duchess.
The peaceful stillness of an English summer
afternoon brooded over the park and gardens at
Overdone. .. . Beyond—the .wide park ; clumps
of old trees, haunted by shy, brown deer; and,
through the trees, fitful gleams of the river—a
narrow, silver ribbon, winding gracefully in and
out between long grass, buttercups and cow
daisies. The Sundial pointed to four o’clock.
That’s the right atmospheric note to strike at
the beginning. The reader’s soul is wrapped
in peace and respect. Then comes the
Duchess to prepare the way for he? niece,
the heroine—a plain girl (handsome heroines
are American), full of Soul, who is discovered
to have “a voice.” Her song, called “The
Bosary,” which forms the leitmotif of the
story, will give an idea of the mixture of
love and religion which women like :
The hours I spent with .thee, dear heart,
Are as.a string of pearls to me ;
I count them over, ev’ry one apart,
My rosary, rny rosary.
Each hour a pearl, each pearl a prayer,
To still a heart in absence wrung ;
I tell each bead unto the end, and there—
A cross is hung.
O memories that bless and burn ! -
O barren gain and-bitter loss !
I kiss each bead and strive at last to learn
To kiss the cross ... to kiss the cross. .
There was as much unanimity about plac-
ing the next two :
Simon the Jester —Locke.
The Dop Doctoi —Dehan.
The position of Simon shows that the artist
has a chance of popularity if he tells a good
story. Dehan is a new writer, whose con-
struction is clumsy; but he carries the
reader over the crudities by sheer vigor.
“South Africa and India” says one libra-
rian, “are the most popular localities in
fiction just now. Queer things about
strange places always attract readers.”
As to the second three, the lists vary.
George Robertson and Co. say that their
next best sellers are :
4. The Green Mouse —Chambers.
5. The Rajah’s People —Wylie.
6. The Sword Maker —Barr.
At Dymock’s Library these fill the places :
4. A Modern Chronicle —Churchill.
5. Tower of Ivory —Atherton.
6. White Magic —Phillips.
The three authors are American. Says
the manager: “On the whole, American
authors are more popular than English.
In the case of Chambers, readers do
not like flimsy yarns like The Green
Mouse so well as his society novels
—The Danger Mark, The Firing Line,
etc. Yet anything by McCutcheon sells.
Perhaps it is partly due to the Harrison
Fisher illustrations. In the Library, English
books which are “ reading ” very well are
Temple Thurston’s Greatest Wish in the
World and Gity of Beautiful Nonsense. And,
outside of fiction, My Memoirs by Princess
Murat. There are very few inquiries for
Meredith ; none for Hardy.”
The Lockley Library says :
4. The Fortune Hunter —Yance.
5. The Green Mouse —Chambers.
6. Drums of War —Stackpoole.
“Also Gertrude Page’s Rhodesian stories, lan
Hay’s A Man’s Man, and the Australian Mrs.
Gunn’s We of the Never-Never.”
The Sydney Book Club introduces a non-
fiction element:
4. In Lotus Land Japan —Ponting.
5. What's Wrong with the World —Chesterton.
6. The Making of Australia —Foster Fraser.
“Add another novel—Crispe’s Gorry
Thorndylie , *Mrs. John Lane’s According to
Maria, and We of the Never Never.”
Miss Peacock, of the Roycroft Library,
says that after the first three, O. Henry,
Anatole France in English, Foster Fraser,
Oppenheim and Gertrude Page jostle one
another in the ruck, and can’t be placed.
“ The anonymous Letters to My Son will pro-
bably have a vogue like The Englishwoman’s
Love Letters; the demand is just beginning.
Every now and then there is a rush for a
particular book, such as The Woodcarver of
’Lympus , when there isn’t a copy to be had.
Nothing makes people want a book so
badly as being unable to get it. A
tew months after the supplies arrive,
;he demand dies down. A novel that
asts over six months is exceptional. . . .
The best books for us are the good, safe,
steady-going Hocking, Oppenheim, Oxenham
and McCutcheon. I never read them ; but
I love them—they pay for the failures. . . .
Short stories don’t go, as a rule. O. Henry
s fairly popular amongst men ; women don't
care for him ; but then a woman’s sense of
humor is—well—different.”
* » * «r
There is a moral in this for young men
and maidens who are going in for the busi-
ness of fiction writing, which is this:
there are nine and forty ways of writing
popular novels, and forty-eight are bad.
-————fh
The King’s Colonists (Wm. Brooks and
Co.) is a little play for children, of which the
words, music and illustrations are all pro-
vided by Mrs. Helen Ashton. The music
has been lithographed from the author’s
script, and can be read without any trouble.
The sentiment is ultra loyal; but Victor
Trumper and the bunyip are not forgotten ;
and the “Song of the Swagmen ” is rich
with the odor of gum leaves and damper.
A handsome paper-covered volume of
Some Children’s Songs, by Marion Alsop
(words by Dorothy McCrae and designs by
Edith Alsop), comes from George Robertson
and Co. Of the five songs, “ Bubbles ” has
the prettiest setting. All of them are good
for kiddies, and the book should be popular
in schools and nurseries. The printing is
excellent. Unfortunately it was done in
Scotland. Local composers say that Aus-
tralian printers have never succeeded with
music. Perhaps they have not had a fair
chance.
The Sydney Sun did itself proud in welcoming
the Dutch sador3 ; but T'i Beazley exceeded his
poetic license in one line of “The Return to
New Holland'”:
“And Hartog’s name and Tasman’s fame,
And Tasman s waiting bride,
Shall rest enshrined in every mind
Throughout Australia wide.”
’Twas a pleasant story that made Abel Tasman
madly in love with Maria van Diemen, daughter
of the Dutch Governor at Batavia. The old man,
to get rid of the poor suitor, sent him off exploring.
The young man discovered capes and islands, and
named some of them after his true love; and when
he got back the girl was handed over. E. A.
Petherick traced the growth of the legend back a
long way, and gave the facts in that useful publi-
cation of his, The Torch, in 1887. The myth had
been exploded before then, but such things die
hard. At the time Tasman left on his celebrated
voyage in 1642, Maria was the wife of Governor
Van Diemen, and they had no children; Tas-
man was already married and middle-aged.

the “TYPHOID FLY”
DANGEROUS DOMESTIC PEST.
This is our well-known domestic pest—the
common house fly. In addition to being a typhoid
carrier, the fly may carry nearly all the intestinal;
diseases and is the prime agent in the spread ofi
summer dysentery, being in this way unquestion-j
ably responsible for the death of many childrenj
in summer. The fly also possesses importance as
a disseminator of the bacilli of tuberculosis, thei
danger of human infection from tubercular flvi
specks being by the ingestion of the specks
on food.
It is a startling thought that a person who
drinks a cup of tea or a glass of milk into which a|
fly has fallen may take into his system 1,250,000
bacilli of typhoid, dysentery or tuberculosis. i
Summing up the position, the typhoid or house;
fly is a general or common carrier of pathogenic 1
bacteria. It may carry typhoid fever, Asiatic;
cholera, dysentery, cholera morbus and other
intestinal diseases. It may carry the bacilli
of tuberculosis and certain eye diseases. It
is everywhere present, and it is disposed
of with comparative ease by the use of
INSECTIBANE.
INSECTIBANE is sold everywhere, but it
is necessary to be careful to' observe the spelling
of the name, as no other insect powder possesses
the qualities which render INSECTIBANE so
peculiarly efficacious.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Victim.
A home vignette, as glows the fire—
A wearied man made well by dinner,
A deep arm-chair, his heart’s desire,
And softly in there creeps a sinner !
A soft gown round her warm limbs falling,
A milky throat, by firelight pink,
Two haunting eyes for love are calling 1
A red mouth where a man might drink !
She steals to him—’tis time for coaxing ;
A dress is due, perhaps a bonnet.
Her sweetest smile (the one for hoaxing)
Her face doth wear with blushes on it !
Around his neck a white arm’s creeping,
Upon his knee a thief has perched ;
Two dancing eyes in his are peeping
(You’d ne’er find sweeter if you searched).
He does not move (his glance is fleeting),
He is quite stern (he knows her caper);
You’d never guess his heart is beating ;
He just sits tight and—reads the paper !
n.s.w.
HELEN JEROME.
A Lyric.
As some doubt seems to exist in Australasia as to tlie
precise qualities of a lyric, how is this for “ feeling ex-
pressed through temperament?”
Wondermath deep as night
Shot with long wisps of light,
Moontrails of fishy white
Caught in the gleam,
Blistered and blinded star,
Thrice wrapped in things that are
Swung from the nebulous car
Of Cynthia’s dream !
Not as the wise suppose,
Tilting a whiff tious nose,
Grasping the horny rose —
Inwrought decay—
But, as becomes the great,
Hoisting a lambent crate
Where the dumb millions wait,
Bending to pray.
Then, with a lofty hand
Scourging the milky sand,
Truer to Hate’s command
Than to the gall,
Something for each to bring,
Few, few are left to sing
Where the swart starbeams wring
Nothing at all.
Death and the doom are dumb,
Dark from the Drask they come,
Cleft with the voiceless hum
Of stark despair ;
Culled from Cheropian wells,
Gathered in heavens and hells,
Bound as with vapturous kells,
Wafts of her hair.
So shall Penlinden light.
Deep o’er the haft of night,
Trembling from height to height,
Shimmer to death,
Scattering her silver train,
Soul-stung with withering pain,
Fall in a dying rain,
Scant of lorn breath.
Ah, me ! The rose is dead !
Ah, me ! The prayer is said !
Death with her stars is red
Never again ;
Not for a viverous rag,.
Not for a doubting hag, •
Smite we the nether jag,
All, all in vain !
MAO. .J,AND.
D. M. W.
My Income.
My income is a little less
Than is enough, to meet my dues.
And always has been, though I’ve had
A wide diversity of screws.
Yet I am prudent, and I search
My ends in manner most discreet
To make the two ends meet—or try :
But there are other things than meat.
And, mindful of an ancient saw,
My way, per boot, to town I’ll wend
To save a copper, and by chance
Will spend a shilling with a friend.
I am not quite prepared to state
Just what it is, or where my pelf
Comes —when it does come—from, because
I really do not know myself;
But when my luck was poor and lean
I’ve borrowed half a sov. from Brown,
A trinket pawned for fifteen bob,
And sold the ticket for a crown.
And this, with something borrowed back
From what I lent to Smith, and then
Some sundries added on have made
My income for the week two-ten.
I do not know if I should count
Quite all the things I mentioh with
My income ; 'p’r’aps I should exclude
The sum I borrowed back from Smith.
But what’s the odds ! Ido not care ;
But one thing causes me the blues :
My income is a little less
Than is enough to meet my Jews.
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1910.
PiUOK OLI.
A PROBLEM IN CLOTHES.
The Minister for Customs has been endeavoring to arrive at a conclusion as to what is a “dressed” and what an “undressed” doll. Mr. Tudor
confessed yesterday that the problem had beaten him.— Daily Paper.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tim Bullktin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Rusiness letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no ease be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager, To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor. Bullktin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojiicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1910.
THE FALSE PROPHETS.
Some ten years ago the Commonwealth of Aus-
tralia started its career amid prognostications of
woe from many quarters. The howl of the False
Prophets was in the public ear, and the half-mast
flag of the False Prophets flew before the public
eye, arid the hired raven of the False Prophets
sat on a blighted tree and said—for the animal
had a cold —“ Deverbore ! ” It was alleged, in
many wild and hairy quarters, that the year 1901
was the end of things. Freetrade—the Freetrade
that New jSouth Wales never really possessed
and wouldn’t have had at a gift—was going
to be lost. Human freedom was going to
be lost. The right of the States to work
out their own destiny was going to be lost.
Everything was about to be lost except grass;
that substance was to be found—growing abun-
dantly in the city streets. It is interesting to
look back on those drear prognostications and
consider how things have really panned out.
And then it is interesting to consider the False
Prophets —those who hired the catarrhal raven
which said “ Deverbore” —for they are prophesy-
ing to-day as confidently and bumptiously as ever,
and wear the same old infallible expression in the
left eye. State Premier Wade, of N.S.W.,
and his political push —also the Sydney
Telegraph and Evening News, and the
Upper House variety of Tory, and the Yass-
berra Federal capital site gang—are just as
full of knowledge about how to save the country
as if the logic of events hadn’t long since revealed
them as the greatest aggregate ass that ever
climbed it tree to escape from the Deluge, and
• built a mare’s nest amid the higher branches. And
they are just as sorry to-day for the ignorance of
everybody else as they were before their pro-
phecies burst some years ago.
New South Wales was the home of the False
Prophets of the anti-Federal days, and it was in
the streets of its capital that the cow-feed was to be
richest when the grass of desolation had time to
grow ; therefore a special and melancholy interest
attaches to the story of its subsequent progress.
The State was 113 years old when Federation be-
gan, and it, is now aged nearly 123 years. In 1901 it
had 1,379,527 people, so in the days of separatism
and low tariffs it acquired population at an average
rate of 12,209 per annum. Nine years later the
population was estimated at 1,655,330, an increase
of 2/5,803; so the growth in the Federal and Pro-
tectionist period averaged well over 30,000 per
annum. Yet the first period covered the time of
the gold boom, the silver and coal finds, the cheap
and easy land grants, and the introduction of
150,000 assisted immigrants; while the second
period was merely one of a fairly decent tariff
and hard work. The increase last year
(40,435) was more than double the figure (14,937)
during the last year (1901) of the old system and
the old tariff, for it wasn’t till the very end of
1901 that the new tariff came into force. The in-
crease by excess of births over deaths, which was
1626 per 100,000 people in 1900 and 1598 in 1901,
was 1719 last year. The State lost 1518 people by
excess of departures over arrivals in 1900, and
6917 in 1901. In 1908 it gained 5350, and in 1909,
12,506 by excess of arrivals over departures.
* * * *
The False Prophets always arise in disorder
about this stage of the argument, and say
that the vast improvement in the aspect of affairs
is solely owing to the transcendent genius of
Mr. Gregory Wade, who is now clamoring at
the top of his voice for a renewal of his job as
State Premier. But three things which Gregory
Wade can’t control are manufactures, com-
merce and shipping. They depend mostly on the
tariff, and the Australian Parliament makes the
tariff; also, it invariably makes the sort of tariff
that Wade disapproves of. The first 113 years—
the 113 un-Federal years—of N.S.W. history saw
60,779 people employed in N.S.W. factories ; the
next nine years raised the figure to 92,181. The
wages paid to factory operatives were £4,974,216
in 1900, and £7,674,664 in 1909. The value of
goods produced in 1900 was under £24,000,000;
in 1909 it was over £40,000,000. In the matter of
employment, one year of the new order of things
was equal, in the matter of progress, to more than
seven of the old ; and in the matter of output, one
year under Federation and Protection was worth
nearly as much as eight of the ancient system
which the Sydney Telegraph and most of the
Wade following declared to bo the whole gar-
ment of political righteousness, the one sure road
to Paradise, and likewise the only pebble on the
beach. And in the same nine years of Federation
N.S.W. commerce increased from £55,725,587 per
annum—the figure it had attained after 113
years of the ancient busted superstitions— to
£79,495,861 ; and shipping (entered and cleared)
from 7,870,503 tons to 11,559,460 tons. Taking
one consideration with another, the growth in the
nine years of the new era compares amazingly
well with the snail-like progress of the 113 years
that went before.
* * # *
When the evidences of general prosperity
become too strong toberefuted.it is the custom
of the False Prophets of Foreign-trade to fall
back in good order upon the alleged Increased
Cost of Living. Owing to the more or less
apocryphal upheaval in the cost of living, .and
the sky-scraping price of food, the workman’s
sovereign is variously stated to be now worth
only 15s. 6d. or 17s. 7|d., or 19s. Id., or any old
sum down to 13s. 3d. which strikes the fertile
imagination of the writer. But the workman
doesn’t seem to have the least idea that
anything terrible has happened to him, He
pays his way as of old, and puts the surplus
in the savings bank just as he used to do, and
every year the surplus grows bigger. In 1900 these
banks of the working classes had 282,643 depositors,
and the said depositors had £10,901,382 of deposits,
or £3B 11s. sd. each. In 1909 there were 460,251
depositors, with £20,150,574 of deposits, or
£43 15s. 7d. each. And, during the same period,
the deposits in the N.S.W. banks of issue rushed
up from £32,233,591 to £46,124,997, and the cash
held in the banks of issue from £5,933,076 to
£10,521,262 (three months later, by March
31, 1910, the figure was £11,919,838). The
net earnings of the State’s railways and
tramways rose from £1,394,052 in 1900 to
£2,075,626 . in 1909. The people, despite the
frantic borrowings of the Wade Government, are
so far shaking themselves clear of their depend-
ence on Bull-Cohen, the money-lender, that the
amount of the State debt which is locally owned
has risen in nine years from £13,000,000 to
£23,000,000. The State revenue has climbed up
from under £10,000,000 to close upon £14,000,000.
Even the agricultural industry is moving, but it
is less dependent on the Commonwealth and more
on the State Government than are manufactures
and commerce; so, as might be expected, it
struggles along much more slowly. The area
under crop in 1900 was 2,439,639 acres. By
1909 it had crawled along to 2,713,971 acres. By
March, 1910, it was estimated that it had made a
plunge and got up to 3,174,690 acres. The
total expansion of less than 700,000 acres is fairly
good ; but, compared with the great uprising of
manufactures, commerce and banking—the mat-
ters more directly under Federal influence—it is
mean and almost contemptible.
4r * * *
There are many other instances which might be
cited, but the list tends to grow too long. The
moral of it all is that N.S.W. has boomed under
Federation as it never boomed before. It has
made such progress that one year of the new
order of things has been worth anything from two
to a dozen of the old. It has gained more by the
union of Australia than any other three States
put together. The beneficent tariff, which its
own wooden-headed legislators and unspeakably
ignorant metropolitan daily press refused to
supply, was conferred on it by the votes of
Victorian, South Australian and Queensland
politicians, and since then it has never
looked back. - The debt which it owes to
these States for dragging it out of the old bog and
mudhole of revenue-tariffism and semi- Freetrade,
and making it the great affluent community it is,
can hardly be estimated. It might be a vastly
more affluent community but for the insane con-
gestion policy, which keeps three-fourths of its
good ports closed against commerce, and makes
them mere bad debts and haunts of casual fisher-
men ; but that is a matter for which the Common-
. wealth isn’t responsible. Meanwhile, the States
which supplied N.S.W with the blessing of its
new tariff have their reward in the bumptious
and arrogant patronage of the False Prophets-,
who are still going strong. The old importing
press of Sydney still saves its face by preaching
Freetrade and denouncing Protection, being
assured that there is no longer any fear of losing
the thing it denounces or getting the horrible
alleged blessing which it says it is craving for.
And the old parochial frauds who prophesied,
nine years ago, that, because of Federation, there
would presently be excellent pasture in George-
street, Sydney, smite their chests with the
hollow sound that the gorilla makes in the
African forest, and profess that the prosperity is
all their work, and want to be re-elected by large
majorities on the strength of it.
* * *
The party which is now demanding that it
should be again entrusted with the management
of the Cornstalk State is essentially the party of
the old False Prophets. It is the crowd which
couldn’t see far enough ahead to realise that all
this prosperity was coming. It is the multi-
tude with one blind eye and one glass
ditto. By doing everything that this gang
declared to be absurd and ruinous, the
Australian Parliament has filled N.S.W.
with such affluence as it had never before
experienced. But their utter failure has in
no way dispirited the Bogus Prophets. They
still pose as the men who know every-
thing and the only men who know any-
thing. They understand exactly the evil
consequences of a progressive land-tax, a
Federal note issue, a national bank, a unifi-
cation and reconstruction policy, a Federal capi-
tal anywhere save at Yassberra, Labor domination
in general, and Government by anybody save
themselves. They see these evils ahead with the
same prescient eye which, ten years ago, looked
into the dim future and saw grass in the streets.
Next month they will appeal to the community
with all their old confidence to re-elect them for
the salvation of the State. And it is quite pos-
sible that the community will re-elect them.
Nothing is more unreliable than a community.
A FINANCIAL CUL-DE-SAC.
The Federal Treasurer has to crawl through a
tight place during this second half of 1910, and
some anxiety is felt as to the probable graceful-
ness of his crawl. From the beginning of last
July, and for 10 years thereafter, the Common-
wealth is to pay to the State Treasurers 255. per
inhabitant, or estimated inhabitant, per annum.
At the same time, it is pledged by the Braddon
clause, which doesn’t expire until the end of the
year, to pay over until December 31 three-fourths
of the tariff revenue, and that is a vast amount
more than 255. per head. Therefore, to make
an average of 255. for the current finan-
cial, year, it will probably have to pay
about 20s. or 225. Gd. per head during
the first six months, and ss. or 2s. Gd. during the
remainder of the year. In other words, the affair
is lopsided, and the head of the liability is wholly
disproportionate to its tail. There is a necessity
to raise a lot of immediate money ; in other words,
there is urgent need for six months’ accommoda-
tion from Uncle or from somebody. One way out
of the difficulty would be to offer the State
Treasurers the Federal lOU with interest, and
redeem it about next March. This idea ought to
please the State Treasurers ; they are so used to
paying interest, and so unaccustomed to receiving
it, that the novelty of the proceeding should be to
them as a white elephant, or a new spring bonnet,
or the recovery of the Lost Drink, or anything
else that is flabbergasting and unlooked for.
Another expedient would be to borrow temporarily
from the banks or the trust funds. Prime Minister
Fisher’s unfortunate inspiration is to take the
money—estimated at £1,500,000 —out of the
Federal paper money fund, and pay no interest
on it. So many unlucky things have happened of
late to the Federal paper money:scheme, and it
has been so mangled and altered and turned
inside out and upside down, that it may be held
that one more episode doesn’t matter. Yet this
episode seems to matter a good deal.
* » * *
As the Government scheme stands to-day, the
proposed Australian notes have no definite basis
whatsoever. There is no Bank of the Nation to
put them in circulation. There is no obligation
laid on the private banks, as in Canada, to hold
40 per cent, of their cash reserves in Federal notes,
and thus provide a permanent and reliable market
for the Federal paper. There is also no obligation
laid on the private banks, as in Canada, to pay
cheques up to £2O in small Government notes if de-
manded. The original propositoni was that the Aus-
tralian private banks should be required, if called
upon, to pay up to £25 in Federal notes of 10s.,
£l, £5, £lO or £2O. It was pointed out, however,
that this would necessitate the holding of £ll5
in notes against a possible £25 liability, in
order. to comply with the law, and that
the position was next to impossible; whereupon
Prime Minister Fisher, instead of making pay-
ment compulsory in 10s. and £1 notes only, aban-
doned the whole. clause, and closed up the
one remaining reliable market for the pro-
posed paper issue. This left the Federal
note a mere homeless or outcast piece of
paper. It will be legal tender if anybody offers
it, but no one is bound to offer it or have it to offer.
Anybody may demand it in payment of a lawful
debt, but the person from whom it is demanded
may simply say that he hasn’t got it, never had it,
would hate to be seen dead with it, and wouldn’t
touch it with a long pole. The banks may take
£500,000 worth or £10,000,000 worth of it, just
as they feel inclined. Having got the amount,
whatever it may be, they may put the Federal
paper into circulation, or they may keep it on
hand, or they may suddenly, and just at the most
awkward moment for the Government, return the
whole lot and demand back the gold they paid
for it. The idea of a Federal note issue was a
splendid one, but there is no idea so splendid that
it can’t be muddled into misery and general
decay if the wrong man gets hold of it.
* * * *
It is difficult to see that anything of great
importance can safely be done with the
money received in exchange for arr issue
of paper money which stands on so pre-
carious a footing. If any of the ordinary
precautions for keeping paper in circulation were
adopted, a gold reserve of 25 .per cent, would be
more than ample. With no precautions whatso-
ever, it is hard to say what figure might or might
not be sufficient. The best course would certainly
be to hold a fairly large gold reserve ; to invest
the balance, whatever it might be, in saleable
State securities, which could be realised or pawned
in case of a sudden emergency ; and to similarly
invest the interest derived from these securities,
so that the fund would be always growing
stronger and more impervious. And about the
worst possible course is the one proposed.
According to this method, it seems that a large
proportion of the money received in exchange
for notes—if there is any money, or any
appreciable amount of money, so received
is to be temporarily borrowed by the Govern-
ment. No interest is to be paid upon the loan, so
the transaction won’t strengthen the reserve.
The Treasurer will begin, at a very early date, to
acquire the habit of regarding the notes reserve as
a,handy fund to draw upon gratis when there is a
temporary famine in the cash-box, and no one
knows where that habit will lead to. Apparently
Parliament will shut up at the usual date, leaving
no saleable securities to even nominally fill the
void created by the withdrawal of £1,500,000, and
leaving nobody with any special authority to
meet an emergency, should such a thing arise.
And if the banks should suddenly grow dead tired
of the Federal notes early in the recess, and demand
their gold back just at the worst possible time, the
results will apparently be gri«at astonishment
and chaos.
It appears to have been the misfortune of Prime
Minister Fisher to take the advice of all his most
virulent opponents in this matter. The assump-
tion is that they called upon him, and spoke
kindly and even respectfully to him, and put
their refulgent plug-hats in a row on his office
table, and showed him figures and calculations,
and thus gently elbowed him along the road they
wanted him to take. And he took that road
surely enough. A Government note issue which
depends for its very existence on the goodwill and
forbearance of the private banks ought to be satis-
factory to all the affable and plug-hatted sup-
porters of Private Enterprise who could possibly
call on any Prime Minister.
THE PROFOUND ADMIRATION OF
JOSEPH COOK.
They had just managed to land Yass-Canberra safely
on the right side. . . . But he advised his listeners,
whenever they heard a man mouthing about “high
national grounds,” to look closely into what he was
doing, and they would find that the high national view
was from his own particular back .yard. —Joseph Cook
(Sydney Telegraph, 20/9 /’ 10).
One of the things I admire about the Wade. Govern-
ment is its striking a blow at centralisation. We ought
to have in New South Wales three or four seaports and
hig inland cities. A system of decentralisation, now for
the first time being taken up in this State, will further
add to the prosperity of the country. —Joseph Cook
(Sydney Telegraph, 21/9I'10).
Having received in the above-mentioned flow
of language the indirect but very clear assur-
ance of Joseph Cook, M.H.R., who ought to have
some acquaintance with the subject, that his old
friend, Premier Wade, of N.S.W., is a poli-
tical fraud and confidence man as regards his
professed decentralisation policy, it is time for
the Cornstalk people to consider what they will
do about the matter on the fast-approaching elec
tion day. Premier Wade professes to aim at rt
versing the narrow, paltry, ill-judged congestion
and concentration policy of his predecessors. On
high national grounds he talks about a desire to
open the Closed Ports of the State, and to rescue
the rich northern and southern coastal districts
from their long-continued desolation. And Cook,
who knows Wade, and who also, presumably,
knows himself, declares that people who propose
to do things on high national grounds are frauds,
hypocrites and delusions. Thereby he professes
to knock the prop of reliability from underneath
the one big idea of which Wade was even dimly
suspected, and unless Wade abjures Cook as a
man of mean ideals with whom no high-minded
politician can associate, the situation is made clear
to the electors and all doubt is removed.
# * # *
And if Cook’s warning, that Wade’s suddenly
acquired national ideals are not to be trusted, is
insufficient in itself, there are other warnings.
Here, for instance, is an extract from a recent
leading article in the Sydney Telegraph— an ex-
tract which can’t be put before the public too often,
printed in a paper which is Wade’s earnest sup-
porter and admirer and practically his press organ
and official mouthpiece:—
To say that our railway system has been deliberately
planned in the interests of Sydney, and that the interior
has been persistently sacrificed, is to quite ignore the
restraints of accuracy. . . . No doubt the policy of open-
ing up new seaports adopted by the Wade Government
is a sound one, and we hope to see COCKSPUR lines
running into the hinterland of all our harbors in due
time. But even then tret will mostly be employed
BRINGING PRODUCE FOR SHIPMENT BY COASTAL STEAMER TO
Sydney, where it will be either consumed or sent over-
sea in the big ocean liners. . . . This talk about “delibe-
rately inflating Sydney ” at the expense of the country is
crude nonsense.
That is a very clear declaration of the fraudulent
and bogus character of the boasted Wade de-
centralisation policy, and Wade has not thought
it worth while to repudiate it. And, in addition,
here is Wade on his own alleged decentralisation
policy—not Wade talking to a country audience,
and fooling it with misty platitudes about possible
good times ahead, but Wade discoursing to the
people of a rich Sydney suburb, where the local
mercantile class has its habitation, and assuring
them that the old congestion policy is to stand,
and is, if possible, to be made more congested
than it was before :
At no distant date the whole of the wharfage accom-
modation on the southern side of the harbor will be re-
quired for the export of the produce of the southern por-
tion of this State, which must come to Sydney, our one
deep sea port. It will therefore be necessary to make
similar provision on the north side of the harbor at
Ball’s Head for the rapidly-increasing produce from the
northern part of the State.
* * * *
So the situation is something like this: (1)
Joseph Cook greatly admires the lofty patriotism
of his friend Wade, who has developed a yearning
to “strike a blow at centralisation ” and to supply
N.S.W. with three or four real seaports, and
to create big inland cities. (2) The same
Cook says that people who profess to
have great national aspirations (like Wade’s)
are quite unreliable, and no value should be
attached to their protestations. (3) The Tele-
graph, which ought to know, declares that there
are to be no large, new seaports at all, and no
new coastal cities, and no big railway connections
with the neglected deep-water harbors —only some
possible little cockspur lines and a pottering
transhipment trade with. Sydney by coastal
steamer, just the same, apparently, as the trade
which already potters at Ulladulla and Merim-
bula and Bellinger and such-like places. (4) Wade
himself, after discoursing many platitudes about
opening up the closed deep-water harbors, declares
that there are really no such places to open.
Thus the only person who gives any definite pro-
mise of better things is Cook, who is lost in pro-
found reverence for his friend Wade. And what
standing Cook has in the matter doesn’t appear.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Passing of Tait.
Thomas Tait has handed in his resignation as
chief of the Victorian Railways, and it is under-
stood that a locally-trained man will get his billet.
He will certainly do so unless the Murray Govern-
ment is quite incapable of profiting by experience.
Australia has imported a number of railway
managers at different times, and, with one excep-
tion, they have ranged from very ordinary men to
dismal failures. Even Eddy, the solitary shining
light of the crowd, wasn’t by any means the
phenomenal light that is commonly supposed. He
did better than Goodchap, his predecessor; but,


then, he was given powers and opportunities whic
GoODCHAP never possessed. He made great mi
provements ; but he was given a million pounds o
loan money with which to do mere current repairs
of a kind which Goodchap before him and Oliver
after him were expected to effect out of revenue.
There has always been a tendency to give the
freshly-imported Railway Commissioner oppor-
tunities which were denied to the locally-traine
man. Thus, because Oliver couldn’t get on as a
mere nominal chief of the N.S.W. railways with
two snarling colleagues in equal authority, it vvas
decided to make the holder of his position an
autocrat; but instead of Oliver being allowed to
show what he could do under the improved cir-
cumstances, the place was given to Johnson— and
the less said about the glorious regime o. .John-
son the better. He and Tait and .Speight
are three men who will be long remem-
bered on the railroads of Australia; and
along with them will be remembered nn-
ported Judges like Bootuby, of S.A., and
Booth, of Westralia ; imported harbor experts
like Coode; imported military geniuses like
Hutton ; and all manner of other importations.
The resignation of Thomas Tait came at a time
when it had begun to be felt that an inquiry into
his management couldn’t be postponed any longer.
During his term of office the list of disasters on
the Victorian lines grew in such a fashion as to be
a public scandal and a public terror. The blame
was always laid on subordinate officers who
had “neglected the regulations,” but no matter
how eloquent the explanation about the careless-
ness of subordinates might be, the dead people
remained dead, and their surviving relatives
remained angry. At last, those people who hadn’t
yet been killed came to the conclusion that
there were only two possible explanations
to fit the case: Either (1) T ait’s system was very
bad, or (2) Tait’s luck in selecting incompetent
employees was something dreadful. They began
to remember, too, that Tait got his professional
training in a slap dash country which has the worst
record on earth for railway wreckage ; and that
didn’t increase their confidence to any notable
extent when the train lurched more than usual, or
the driver blew his whistle like the Devil and
crowded on the brakes. Probably Tait and
Johnson will be the last, or very nearly the last,
of the race of imported railway managers. No-
body in his senses would desire to keep the big
positions on the railways exclusively for Aus-
tralians by birth ; but there is going to be a
demand for men of Australian training—men of
whom something more is known than that
they have a quart of written testimonials and
a complete ignorance of local conditions. When
a G-overnment looks abroad for a perfect stranger
to fill an important post, it has to depend wholly
on his excellent testimonials. He may have got
them because lie deserved them. He may have
get them because his old employers wanted to
get rid of him without giving him the crude and
naked sack; and often tjie testimonial that is
given to the man one wants to get rid of is a lot
more brilliant than that given to the man one
would be very sorry to lose. He may have
acquired them by making himself a nuisance to
influential people ; or eminent fellow-churchmen
may have pulled strings on his behalf quite as
much out of regard for his piety as out of faith in
his professional ability. There are many possi-
bilities in the case. Australia has met a number
of these possibilities, and in most cases it hasn’t
liked them. Yet they were hard to get rid of.
A man already in the service can be made acting-
Commissioner for a spell, and sent back to his
old job if he fails; but the imported pig-in-a-
poke must have a long engagement signed before
he leaves his home in Smashville, U.S.A., or
•wherever it is, and starts on the first stage of his
journey Australia-wards.

A Tattered. Old Female.
Half its time the Tory press of N.S.W. can’t
quite recollect whether it represents a heavy
borrowing party or a strongly i conomical party;
and it fails to remember whether it wants to
curse the Labor people for helping Waddell and
O’Sullivan to borrow too much, or to denounce
them as a dangerous lot vvho, if they get into
power, won’t borrow nearly enough. The Telk-
gkaph was in the latter stage of its mental
trouble last Monday, and denounced Labor in
this fashion as an institution which, if it got into
power, would lag on the road to the pawnshop
and make insufficient speed unto Cohen : ■
At the end of June last there were 3643 miles of
Government railway open for trallio in New South Wales.
Of this, 1519 miles failed to pay interest and work) ngr ex-
penses, not to speak of the I per cent, for a sinking fund
required by the Labor Party’s platform. qSo far from that,
they entaiied the Department in an expense of £304,893
for the 12 months. Under the Labor Party’s plan not
oiie of those railways—and there; are ,27 of them —could
have been built. . . . Why, the whole State rail way system
does not pay 1 per cent, over and above working
expenses and the interest bill. On an average, the
railway loans cost about £3 12s. , 6d. per cent.
Last year the system earned £4 11s. 7d. per cent,
—or lid. per cent, less than the Labor party re-
quires new lines to earn before they will consent to
construct them. There had been railways in New South
Wales for 18 years before the earnings were sufficient to
pay interest at to-day’s loan rates. And it was not until
25 years had elapsed that the profits reached the 1 per
cent, sinking fund level. Under the Labor scheme, New
South Wales could never have had a railway at all.
And then one is tempted to look back into the
story of these old, disastrous days, when Toryism
was supreme in N.S.W. and the big loans and the
railways were just commencing. N.S.W. was a
wonderful place in the old days. The people
paid no : land-tax, income-tax or death
duties, hardly any stamp duties, no tobacco
duty,'no beer excise, hardly any local rates—in
fact, they regarded themselves as a special people
who couldn’t be expected • to hand , out taxes
-as other communities did. They lived on
Customs taxes (not heavy ones), on land sales, on
loans, on faith in Providence, and on a good deal
of rurn. Up to 1855 the money spent on railways
only amounted to £515,000; by 1860 it was
£1,422,000; by 1870, £5,566,000. All through
these early years the land revenue—the income or
GoD-given endowment which came to the country
without any taxation or any labor—would have
paid for all the railway building easily, if the
lazy old Tories of the back-block period
hadn't wanted the money to save them from
the necessity of paying taxes ; and by 1870
there would have also been £IOO,OOO a
year ■(the figure reached £300,000 a year by
1875) of revenue from the unencumbered lines
with which to build more railways. Every line
that N.S.W. now possesses could have been built
without borrowing a single penny, and yet the
taxes to-day might have been only half what they
are, and whoever says anything to the contrary
isra.liar. The trouble was that, instead of levying
a few. small imposts in 1855, in order to leave the
land revenue available for railway building, the
„ money was borrowed, then and the taxes were
levied afterwards to pay the interest bill, which
■ goeth on for ever. If a public work is built out of
revenue, the money has only to be paid once;
.whereas, if it is built, out of loans. tl)e interest
bill happens every year until the Day of Judg-
ment, and until the work has been paid for
100 times over —but, unfortunately, the silly
old pioneers couldn’t see it. The past can t
be undone, but the brightest feature in the Labor
nartv’s rather weak finance is that it threatens to
do a shade better in the future. It isn t possible
• to confine railway building to lines that will pay
full interest on the loan money and 1 per cent,
sinking' fund as well. But it is possible to charge
a portion of the cost of ill-paying lines to revenue
and nav iust so much out of loans that the line will
meet its interest bill and its 1 per cent, sinking
fund charge. Also, it is quite possible to pay 1 per
cent, sinking fund on the whole public debt out
of revenue. N.S.W. isn’t so dog-poor that it
is unable to build even a yard of line without
fleeing to the pawnbrokery for the money ;
thanks to fairly good management m Com-
monwealth affairs, even Wade hasn t contrived
to drag it down quite so low as that. Phe wors
and muddiest aspersion thrown at the marble
brow of the Ma State is this constant wail ot
aggregate Toryism, that she must cadge for a crust
in the gutters of Lombardy all her days because
she is a tattered old female, with no visible sup-
port of her own and no prospect of ever having any.
The Keward of Apostacy.
Now that the Yassberra party has to some ex-
tent got its second wind, and dragged its feet out
of the bog of astonishment in which it sank upon
hearing of its own success, it is wondering audibly
how the thing happened, and what the Fisher
Government meant by it. That Ministry cer-
tainly did things that were calculated to set the
angels wondering. A year or two ago the great
majority of its members were ardent supporters
of Dalgety as the best capital site in the running,
yet, in the end, it was their votes and their clam-
orous support which secured the selection of
Yassberra. By their own showing they re-
garded Dalgety as the best choice in the
interests of the country, and by their own
showing they ended by voting against what they
considered the interests of the country. And
they hadn’t even the mercenary excuse that then-
billets depended on their vote; they began by
weakly giving up their convictions in favor of
Dalgety to please a pro-Yassberra Parliament,
and they ended by shoving the site they didn’t
believe in down the throat of another Parliament
which, in the main, didn’t believe in it either.
In fact, it believed so little that all the influence
of the Fisher Government only managed to make
the Senate vote a tie or dead heat. Not one of
the converted or perverted M misters had the
courage to say that he Voti for Yassberra
because he recognised it as u.ie spot which
howled for recognition in the public in-
terest. ‘Prime-Minister Fisher said vaguely
that he regarded the capital site as only a
small matter, after all, which was something akin
to Henry IV. ’s famous remark about another
capital site, that 1 “it was worth a mass”; but
that only made his opponents the more flabber-
gasted, for the spectacle of a Labor Ministry
needlessly abandoning its obvious convictions and
trampling with all the derision of a Bruce Smith
on its own cherished referendum principle, all for
the sake of a very small matter and a thing that
really didn’t signify much, was quite beyond
theni. Now, having dug themselves ou,s of-the
pit of their own amazement,i the Yassberra-ites
have formulated the idea that the deed vvas done
as a rather clumsy bribe to the people of N.S.W.
to induce them to vote for the State Labor party
at next month’s election. As Sydney Telegraph
recently put it in a leading article headed, “ What
the Capital means to Labor ”
The State election is only three weeks off, and the
Federal Parliament will have to show unusual expedition
to get the Bill passed through both Houses in time for
the proclamation to be made before polling-day. And
unless that can be done, and the whole matter be as
“definitely settled” as Mr. Hughes now erroneously
declares it to be, Labor must expect the people of New
South Wales to regard its good faith as unproved. Sup-
pose, for instance, that the necessary steps for the final
acquisition of the capital territory have not been taken
by election day, what guarantee have we that they will
be taken at all'? The Federal Labor party is divided on
the question, and the majority of its members have only
voted for Yass-Canberra because the Government cracked
its whip as loudly as the Caucus would allow—a course
which was palpably advisable in view of the impending
elections in New South Wales and the hopelessness of a
“harmonious” Labor Government emerging therefrom
if Melbourne influence against justice being done was
yielded to. After the elections, however. Labor will
have slipped the leash of political necessity. If Mr.
McGowen obtains office, there will be no need to force
the capital question ; and if he is confirmed in the position
of leader of the Opposition, there will be the less inclina-
tion to do anything for a recalcitrant State which has
refused to put Labor in office. The subject will be wide
open again, and the chances of Labor’s recent endorse-
ment of Yass-Canberra repeating itself will be very poor.
... If the proclamation is not out before polling day, the
capital, instead of being settled, will really be an open
question. And if the Fisher Government does not attend
to that formality within the time, its Labor colleagues
in this State, etc., etc.
It is a muddy sort of a reward that the Fisher
Government gets —this assumption that it went
back on its convictions in order to help its friend
McGowen to a State billet; but any reward that
is likely to accrue in such a case is almost bound
to be muddy.
A Lost Opportunity.
The Mayor of Annandale erupted as follows at a
meeting addressed by C. G. Wade last week :
I came here to-night for two reasons. (Cheers.) One
was that I have never heard Mr. Wade speak before, like
many of you, perhaps—(applause)—and the other was to
hear him grapple with those vile lie3 —(frantic cheering)
—and the slanders that have been going about the
country for the last two or three months. (Cheers.) I
want to hear him throw them back at those who utter
them to choke them. (Applause.) He is quite able for
the lot of them. (Cheers.) I want him to get them by
the neck. (Cheers.)
It was a golden opportunity for this suburban
alderman's hero to pulverise the alleged
“slanders." Yet beyond the usual unsatisfying
generalisations about “diabolical calumnies ” and
“shameless untruths,” Wade didn’t pulverise a
thing. He side-stepped details as though they
were death-adders. His mayoral admirer gave
him the chance of his lifetime to stat„e definitely
that the Coercion Act didn’t abrogate trial by
jury, and hadn’t been condemned as shockingly
arbitrary by the most Conservative journals in
the Conservative land of J. Bull. The chance
was allowed to slide. Wade might have paralysed
his Labor opponents by announcing, once and for
all, that he never remitted half the fine imposed
on McKlB, the big North Sydney baker, without
reference to the Judge who tried the case. The
Labor people say Wade did remit half McKye’s
fine; also, that McKye was a second offender;
also, that Judge Heydon denounced McKye’s
offence as “particularly contumacious”; also,
that the same Judge was astounded at Wade’s
interference with that well-earned penalty. At
Annandale Wade discoursed in a large fashion
about “dead cats” and thelike, but he didn’t come
down to bedrock and deny a single one of the
foregoing and other things alleged against him.
Within a few feet of him there was a worshipper
who would have given anything to hear him get
“by the neck” those persons who had charged
his Justice Department with sending out police-
men to sound the jury-men empanelled to try
Peter Bowling. Yet no one was throttled.
The subject was left severely alone. The wire-
netting steal (perpetrated while C. G. Wade was
Attorney-General) wasn’t mentioned ; nor was
Barren jack and its 25 miles of soon-to-be
irrigated, yet still unresumed, land—property from
whicheventhe “Liberal” press,now recognises that
private owners are going to make a vast profit at
the public expense. In a word, despite the en-
couraging presence of a large crowd of people of
the same way of thinking as his chairman—folks
who simply yearned to hear him “ grapple with
the vile lies of his opponents and throw them
back at those who utter them to choke them ”
Wade preserved the murkiest silence. There is
only one inference to be d rawn: he confined himself
to wild, inconclusive yelps about “calumny” and
“ injustice ” because he dared not get down to de-
tails, the charges being true. If only for the credit
of its own intelligence, it is up to “Liberalism”
to drop the “ calumny ” cry till the person who is
said to be calumniated butts in and definitely
controverts some of the so-called calumnies. At
present, for all the passionate demands of his sup-
porters that he shall speak and choke his. de-
tractors, Wade continues as embarrassedly silent
as a malefactor apprehended with the goods about
him.

The Strange Vagaries of “Justice.”
Three police-court cases, reported recently in
different parts of Australia, indicate what a wild
gamble the law is liable to be. At the Fitzroy
(Vic.) court, the following evidence was given:—
Amelia Shanahan stated that after she retired to bed
last night her husband pulled her on the floor and kicked
her about the hips. She had been kicked and punched
by her husband earlier in the evening-. Defendant earned
good wages, but spent all his money in hotels, and she
had to work to support herself.
Defendant: You were drunk yesterday.
Witness: I was not. You gave me 75., but it cost me
ss. 6d. to get* your coat out of pawn.
Constable D. Brown gave evidence that when he
arrested defendant he found Mrs. Shanahan in a state of
collapse.
There were absolutely no mitigating circum-
stances—and the Bench handed the guilty party
the blighting penalty of a 40-shilling fine. At
Wagga (N.S.W.), about the same time, Frederick
Cheney pleaded guilty to cruelty to horses on
August 22 and 23. According to the local paper :
Sub-Inspector Musgravb stated that Cheney had three
horses in a waggon, and the wheels of the latter became
bogged. As the horses would not pull it out, he left
them from sundown on the 22nd ult. until the next
morning. On both days, it was alleged, he procured
a stick and an axe and beat the horses.
Constable Brownlee stated that he visited the place
and examined the horses. One in particular seemed to
have been very severely illtreated, its ribs and haunches
beinv covered with blood, the hair matted together and
the body covered with bruises.
Two witnesses stated that the horses were kept m the
roadway from 7.30 p.m. on the 22nd until noon the
next day. Mrs.- Rynehart said that in the evening
accused was beating them with a stick. Mrs. Dunkley
stated that he was hitting the horses with a stick, and
on one of them applied the back of an axe.
After listening to which, the magistrate remarked
severely that “if it had been proved that Cheney
had used the sharp edge of the axe he would
have sent him to gaol for three months,” and
then imposed a fined of £2. The reverse
side of the picture was presented at the
Sydney Water Police Court last week when
Frank Snaide was charged with having no
visible means of support, and in that condition
“ loafing about Potts Point.” He was sentenced
to six months’ hard labor ! Yet, beyond the fact
that he was dirty, that he dwelt with a number
of dogs on a vacant allotment and that the Potts
Pointers didn’t approve of him, the police had no
overt reproach to level at Snaide. In the case of
Shanahan, of Victoria, it is probably due to the
luck of owning a wife with a stout constitu-
tion that that appalling ruffian hadn’t to stand
his trial on a charge of murder. The Law’s treat-
ment of him amounts practically to a magisterial
license to others of similar tastes to go and
do likewise. What sort of a deterrent is
the possibility of a 40s. fine to a really en-
thusiastic wife-beater, anyhow? In regard to
the case of Fred Cheney and those who
adjudicated on it, words fail and comment collapses
on the floor. Here the Bench lays it down that
so long as a man or brute, in charge of horses,
refrains from hacking them with the sharp side
of an axe, he will be let off with a fine. Questions
that surge up in this connection are—-(1) If men
don’t deserve gaol who beat and kick women
into “a state of collapse,” and who torture defence-
less animals for hours at a time, when do they
deserve it? And (2) if wife-beaters and horse-
torturers merit only trifling fines for their most
blood-thirsty efforts, what is a fair punishment
for a citizen who camps with some dogs on a
vacant allotment? Should he get six months’
hard labor, or a purse of sovereigns?

Something More Important Than Leg-Irons.
The shindy as to who ironed Peter Bowling,
and why and wherefore; and whether he was
manacled according to the regulations or
the cold hatred of Wade (who refers to him as
“ a criminal ”), or the fear of the gaol-overseers
that he was a desperate bloke who might kick the
Black Maria to smithereens to spite the Govern-
ment, threatens to last up to the day of
election to the eclipse of several other mat-
ters of more vital importance. One of those
matters is the Coercion Act, which was blud-
geoned through the N. S. Wales Assembly last
December at one sitting, and which is the most
befitting monument to stand as the headstone at
the grave of the Wade combination. By the pro-
visions of that Act any officer of police, from a
sergeant upwards, who has reasonable grounds to
believe that any building is being used fora meet-
ing for the purpose of instigating or aiding in a
lockout or strike, may whistle up a battering ram
and bust the place up to satisfy his suspicions.
And in the event of finding two or three persons in-
side, he can lug them along the pavement until he
reaches a lockup, where they will be deposited
until opportunity occurs to try them. If Wade’s
law is satisfied that the citizens who were found
sitting round the dining-room fire when the police
broke in with an axe were even instigating a
strike, then the said citizens may be sentenced to
12 months’ hard struggle in Wade’s gaol. Simi-
larly with two persons “assembled ” to aid or
encourage a strike (calling out “’Ooray” might
put a strike in fresh heart, and the inser-
tion of ten bob into the hat for the hungry
women and children of the strikers would be real
bosker aid) —12 months’ hard 1 And whether the
provisions of that Act are, or are not, “without
parallel in the history of Great Britain and Aus-
tralia,” as McGowen declared, the method of
passing them was infamous. The following is no
summary of the proceedings, but the actual record
after the second reading had been stopped by the
application of the “gag,” moved by Lonsdale,
and carried by 42 votes to 32 :

And this same bludgeoning Wood, when a point
of order was taken against Wade during the
second reading, to prevent him dealing with
matters calculated to prejudice the trial of men
arrested in connection with the Newcastle
trouble, had the hide to say to the Speaker
You being invited to give a ruling, I submit—and, if I
may say so, I do it with a due regard to the position of
the Chair, and as a warning to the Chair— that the Chair
is not here to deprive any individual member of the
House, whether he sits on the Government side or on the
Opposition side, of those rights vested in him as a mem-
ber of the House. An hon. member is entitled to
discuss anything that may crop up in relation
to its bearing on the people of the country and the
doings of the House. ... So long as the Premier or any
other hon. member confines his remarks to the legitimate
issue before the House—that is, the passing of a measure
to deal with the industrial conditions that exist outside
—just so long must every hon. member have th'e fullcst,
freest and most complete right to express hiS' opinions,
here to-night.
Having expounded himself in such democratic lan-
guage, he sat down, metaphorically spat on his
hands, took a firm grip of the sandbag and “ stif-
fened out ” the House. The men who helped him
through with this refined method of Responsible
Government were Arthur, Ball, Barton,
Briner, Broughton, W. Brown, Clarke,
Collins, Davidson, Donaldson, Downes,
Fallick, Fitzpatrick, Gilbert, Graham (Jimmy),
B. Hall, Latimer, Henley, Hindmarsh, Hogue,
Hunt, James, Lee, Levy. Lonsdale, Mahony,
McCoy, McFarlane, Millard, McLaurin,
Moore, Morton, Moxham, Nobbs, Oakes,
Onslow, Parkes, Perry, Robson, Thomas and
Waddell. Wade left the chamber on the
ground that he was sick, which is probably true—
it was enough to make a glue-factory sick.
White Ants.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most of the Labor members who voted for the Yass-
Canberra site were influenced by purely parochial in-
terests. — Political Item,.
Says Mac the Splitter to me, says he,
“ There’s many a fair an’ sightly tree,
There’s many a tall, young ash I ve seen,
Straight in the barrel an’ seemin’ clean ;
But you find when she’s down an’ you take a cut
She’s rotten with ants from head to butt.
An’ ants is the Devil, you take it from me,”
Says Mac the Splitter, “an’ worse,” says he.
Says Mac the Splitter to me, one day :
“ I’m a fair good judge o’ the trees that pay ;
At failin’ orsplittin’ I’m hard to beat. '
But ants ! They worry a cove a treat;
An’ there’s scarce a tree in this belt, I guess,
Ain’t got some ants in her, more or less.
But you’ll find me pickin’ the soundest tree,”
Says Mac the Splitter. “My oath !” says he.
Says Mao the Splitter : “ Now listen here ;
Them ants they guv me a strange idear ;
An’, talkin’ o’ this ’ere politics game,
I judge them Parties an’ trees the same.
There’s none of ’em free from a fault or knot,
An’ some has the borers, an’ some the rot.
An’ them State Insects, it seems to me,”
Says Mac the Splitter, “is ants” says he.
Says Mao the Splitter : “ I dunno much
Of these political moves an’ such ;
But I ain’t such a darn poor judge as some,
An’ I didn’t look twice at the Fusion gum.
It was rot, dry rot, as a fool could note ;
An’ I says, ‘ That' tree don’t get my vote.’
So I looks around for a sounder tree,”
Says Mac the Splitter. “ That’s what,” says he.
Says Mac the Splitter : “ I cruised around ;
The Labor tree looked clean an’ sound ;
So I took a chip o’ the policee.
Same as I’d chip a palin’ tree ;
I carted it home an’ tried the wood,
An’ it seemed all straight in the grain an’ good.
Thinks I, ‘ This ’ere is a boshter tree,”’
Says Mac the Splitter, “ bar ants,” says he.
Says Mac the Splitter : “ You never can tell
Till you open her up; an’ ants is Hell !
An’ these State Insects—spare me days !
Have all the worst of the wliite-ant ways.
They work inside of her, out o’ sight.
You chip an’ sound her an’ vote her right;
But when you open that straight, sound tree,”
Says Mac the Splitter—“ Gorstrooth !” says he.
Says Mac the Splitter: “ I call it dirt!
I feel all out, an’ me pride is hurt.
Fer there ain’t a bloke that can judge a tree
Ip the whole darn forest along with me.
I’ll swear that tree I chipped was good,
An’ the chip was straight, clean, honest wood ;
But, I’ve found white ants in me Labor Tree”
Says Mac the Splitter. “ Damn ants ! ” says he.

The “S. M. Herald’s” Testimonial to Labor.
Last Tuesday was a memorable occasion in the
annals of the N.S. W. Labor Party. On that day
the S. M. Herald arose honestly (though cantan-
kerously) on its two feet, and did the Square
Thing or Quadrangular Substance. To all intents
and purposes it took back all its previous remarks.
In times gone by, it was the fashion of the Tory
press to wail that the Labor party represented a
class only ; that it was a mere horny-handed con-
glomeration ; that it was a pick-and-shovel body
with a pick-andAihovel mind, trying to run the
country on pick-and-shovel principles in the pick-
and-shovel interest, and with a complete disregard
of anything and everything that wasn’t pick-and-
shovel. It was constantly and strenuously pointed
out that there wasn’t in the Labor party one
financier who could be trusted with control of the
Treasury or one lawyer who could hold the
Attorney-General’s job—not even a very poor
lawyer. And now—well, let the S. M. Herald
speak for itself :
One of the most striking features of the political Labor
movement—and the one to which the average unionist
seems to be persistently blind—is the manner in which,
year by year, the genuine laborer is being increasingly
shouldered aside, and the movement itself used for the
advancement of outsiders. Thefirst Parliamentary Labor
party was brought intd existence as a direct outcome of
the big 1890 strike in which the working men suffered so
severely', and the members of it were practically all mem-
bers of the class it was sought to represent. With the
growth of union organisation it became obvious that to
fight an election from the Labor side meant far less
trouble and expense than if fought from the other side.
The undrilled forces of Liberalism called for strenuous
efforts if they were to be properly marshalled, whereas as
time went on the Labor ranks marched to order with
more and more machine-like precision. This state of
affairs inevitably drew the eyes of political self-seekers
towards the Labor movement. Election after election
saw ah increasing number of outsiders worming their way
into the Labor camp, and fewer and fewer genuine
workers on the hustings. Thus it comes about that, as
against the list of laborers, miners, engineers, plasterers,
bricklayers, able-bodied seamen; battery hands, wharf-
laborers, boiler-makers, shearers, etc., who made up the
first Labor party in 1891, we have attached to the names
Of candidates standing in the Labor interest to-day
the following occupations : —Barrister-at-law, solicitor,
grazier, manufacturer, clergyman, managing director,
land agent, patent attorney', auctioneer, newspaper pro-
prietor, contractor, commission agent, librarian, master
coachbuilder, master tanner, chemist, factory manager,
commercial traveller, teacher, clerk, accountant.
These are a few of the occupations figuring in the list ;
but surely they are sufficient to cause the genuine
worker to ask himself whether all the Labor candidates
are burning with a zealous passion for the uplifting of
the masses—or with just such a passion for the uplifting
of themselves.
The curious point is that the S.M. Herald doesn’t
seem any better satisfied than it was before. It
used to complain that the Labor party only re-
presented a class. Now it complains even more
bitterly that the Labor party is beginning to
assimilate men of all classes.
