A Lawyer and a Loophole.
J. Drysdalk Brown, Victorian Attorney-General,
says he is going to prosecute, under the Crimes
Act, certain railway employees whose disregard
of certain profusely disregarded regulations is
alleged to have produced the Richmond accident.
BroWn’s intention was no sooner published than
it-was condemned as useless, and worse than use-
less. Among others, the Shop Assistants’ Federa-
tion, which represents a large section of the
public -interested in the injured passengers, pro-
tested against the Government’s decision to
criminally prosecute men whom no jury, it de-
clared, would convict, thus putting the country
to great expense and getting nothing in return.
The mover of this resolution, himself an old rail-
way man, declared further that J. Drysdalk
Brown’s talk of prosecution was likely to “ cover
up the cheeseparing policy which is the primary
cause of the enormous number of accidents that
have occurred under the Commissioners’ re-
gime.” Rightly or wrongly, the Victorian
public has thus settled the question in its
own mind ; wherefore the astonishing move
of J. Drysdalk Brown challenges the indignation
of 20 times as many citizens as dropped un-
favorable comments on the same Attorney-
General’s recent costly prosecution of Robert
Harper and others, in connection with a low-down
blackmailing conspiracy. In that case the public,
knowing nothing save what it read, was easily
led to suppose that J. Drysdalk Brown had a
fund of secret information to draw upon, and
would be able to justify his costly proceedings.
And even when he failed utterly to do anything
of the sort, the fact that the victim was a wealthy
man and a much-abused employer, ensured him a
certain amount of loud approval from persons and
organisations whose prejudices paralysed their
judgment. But, in his dealing with the Richmond
case, he has no such force of prejudice behind
him, and no uninformed public, hungry for
“revelations,” in front of him. He stands ex-
posed to all-round criticism as an Attorney-
General who proposes to scatter public money
upon a few lawyers in what is held to be
an attempt to visit the sins of the rail-
way administration upon the heads of sundry
employees, who may or may not be blamable, but
are certainly not the prime sinners. Everybody
knows—even the whitewashing Board of Inquiry
stated the fact—that the smash at Richmond
could not possibly have occurred under an up-to-date
system of safeguarding the public. Also, everybody
knows that many passengers were killed or terribly
injured, not as a result of the collision merely,-but
owing to the fault of the Department in running
a 40-year-old carriage in conjunction with two
younger and stouter carriages, which smashed it to
matchwood between them. With circumstances
of that nature well known, what good can be
achieved by prosecuting these employees ? And
hf»w is Attorney-General Brown going to restore
confidence in the Railway Department by com-
mitting the Government to a course in which the
public so promptly expresses its utter want of
confidence ?

From the Old Soldier :
The railway breaks-of-gauge are bad, of course, and
would hamper defence work ; but, for all that, I don’t
believe the yarn that 30,000 mounted troops would need
63 days to go by rail from Melbourne to Brisbane under
present conditions. They could pretty nearly march the
distance by road in the time. Soldiers with arms and
accoutrements require more room than ordinary pas-
sengers. A compartment that would hold 10 of the
one lot would be crowded with eight of the others. One
hundred and fifty men and horses are a load for a train of
34 carriages and trucks. Therefore 30,000 would need
SBK) trains. How many trains a day could pass along the
line? The Russians say they find it dangerous to run
more than 10 on a single track. At any rate, it would
take 20 days to get the 30,000 away from Melbourne.
Even if there were no break-of-gauge, men and horses
could not-be run straight through from Melbourne to
Brisbane—l3oo/ miles. Neither men nor horses could
stand it; it would mean keeping them cooped
up without food or i'est for two days to 2J days
at least. Without an3'l break-of-gauge, then, from the
time the first of the 200 trains left Melbourne till the last
arrived in Brisbane, at leas-rt 22Jdays would elapse ; andif
(say) two-hours in every eight were allowed, for rest and
refreshment, the -time would still be extended by only'
another half day for each,cargo. But even that wouldn’t
bump out the total periodmuch beyond the half of the
allegad 63 days. f

Why does a piece like “Our Miss Gibbs,”
which has been warmly acclaimed by English
critics, and is billed as “the greatest musical
success of our generation,” appear such negligible
stuff when considered in this country ? The music
is good enough in its own familiar fashion. The
producer at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) has done his
work nobly. The stage picturesVire beautiful; so
are the stage ladies and their dresses. Some of
the lyrics are astonishingly smooth and clever.
The principals know their business from A to Z.
And yet the result is unsatisfying. To the writer
it seems that “ Our Miss Gibbs ” is too racy of the
London soil to stand transplantation to these parts,
where the topical hits and gags of the tiny area
which is bounded by the metropolitan cab radius
are Greek to9opercent, of theatre-goers. Thegentle
satire latent in the character of Hughie Pierre-
point, gentleman crook, is lost on us to a great
extent. Australian theatres haven’t been teem-
ing for the past two years with noble-
hearted amateur cracksmen. We have never
seen the original of Mr. Amalfy ; few
of as would know his name if we heard it. We
remember dimly to have read of Dorando in con-
nection with a Marathon race, but our Mara-
thons don’t finish in the haunts of fashion; and
so, when Timothy Gibbs puts on a grotesque run-
ning costume and flees through the White City,
his feat is only regarded as another manifestation
of dementia on the part of “Cryptos” or his
collaborator, James T. Tanner, constructor.
* * * *
Any Australian production of this playis
bound to labor under another severe handicap.
Hughie, the dominant figure in it, is, in the West
End of London, a perfectly understandable—one
might almost say credible—person. In Sydney
he is neither. Certainly, even in London, a
Pierrepoint would hesitate to wear purple evening
clothes, a heavily-frogged frock coat, and so On.
But he might safely talk and “ rag ” in the ex-
uberant Kirby manner, and not be oppressed with
fears of the asylum. _ George Grossmith, jun., a
genius in his own strictly-limited line, contributed
this type—the lineal descendant of the mid-Vic-
torian “ masher ” —to English society some years
ago. Not only is every gilded young Londoner
from White’s or the Bachelors in sympathy with
Pierrepoint (if only for the reason that, in him,
each One of them glimpses some part of his own
better, brighter self), but he also represents the
glass of fashion and mould of form for vast num-
bers of bank-clerks, shop assistants and others
doOmed to remain apart from the pageantry of
life. All Hughie’s quaint sayings and allusions and
queer turns of phrase make swift connection with
Londoners, for to them they are dear old friends.
Here his chaff falls flat, not because it isn’t an
excellent article in its line, but because, despite
spirited efforts to give it a local flavor by season-
ing it with references to Manly, Paris House and
Coogee, it is in a different language from ours.
* * * *
Where the characters handle phases and
emotions with which the audience is acquainted,
they meet with the customary response. The
love-story of sweet Mary Gibbs, whom a need-
lessly offensive Duchess seeks to separate
from her adorer. Lord Eynsford, is altern-
ately heart-rending and sentimentally pleas-
ing. The knockabout comedy of her relative
Timothy provokes loud and continuous laughter.
The subsidiary affair of the heart between Hughie
and Lady Elizabeth Thanet ends in a manner
gratifying to all right-thinkers. An interpolated
turn by Fred Leslie and Miss Ivy Schilling—part
dance and part muscular display brings
down the house. And then there is the
newcomer, Miss Blanche Browne. She has
all the hall-marks of the London Gaiety—
the gleaming and multifarious teeth, the grace,
the finish, the tiny, cultivated voice, the perfect
clothes, the true poise, the tendency to under-
play. She is a charming acquisition to a cast
whose remaining members, together with their
methods, are well and favorably known to
students of this sheet.
There are, of course, other notable things
in “ The Chance of a. Lifetime ” besides its dar-
ing and picturesque, but painfully inefficient,
villain. There are starting-gates, a weighing-
room, live horses and other paraphernalia of the
racecourse. There is a typical Ascot Enclosure
crowd of faultlessly-groomed male and female
smart-setters. There is a likely-looking Gold
Cup, which is filched from its stand in the Saddl-
ing Paddock by a calamitous-looking person in
burglar’s clothes, deposited in Captain Clinch’s
London flat, and subsequently removed there-
from by Mrs. Edgar, a virtuous female
whom the Captain has several times stren-

The J. C. Williamson Grand Opera Co.
began a short season at Sydney Royal, on Satur-
day, with a creditable production of “Carmen.”
There was a week-end rush of new shows, so
Bizet’s fiery opera didn’t get all the attention it
deserved ; but the people who did go were enthusi-
astic—which is more comforting to the principals
than to the manag'ement. Bel Sorel was the
Carmen, and Reginald Roberts the infatuated Don
Jose, who throws up his steady job as a soldier,
and his tunic with the 300 brass buttons, to keep
in with the. tobacco-factory lady. In this
instance, Don Jose had good and sufficient
reasons for losing his head along with his
heart and job, for Bel Sorel’s Carmen is
seductive enough to lure a Field-Marshal from the
War Office. Roberts made a complete success of
the part of the deluded brigadier. He sang
throughout with great spirit, and acted power-
fully right up to the gruesome ending outside the
Sydney Cricket Ground, where a regiment of
armed men are busy torturing a bull to death. It
is when he gets a chance at grand opera or a Gil-
bert and Sullivan r6le that Roberts is at his best ;
in the shallow musical comedies he usually does
not shine as much as.performers who, if they were
dumped into a part which required strong treat-
ment, would be .so lost that an adver-
tisement in the Missing Friends column
couldn’t find them. Arthur Crane sang the
music of the conquering Escamillo admirably ;
but his acting lacked fire. He looked haughty
enough to have slain a charging bull with one
glance; but bis pursuit of Carmen was almost
perfunctory. One of the finest things of the pro-
duction was Rosina Buckmann’s Michaela. Her
pure, flexible voice and clear enunciation made
her numbers a delierht, and she played with in-
tense sympathy. Rufus Ferguson and Edward
Wynn, as Dancairo and Reniendado, the smug-
glers, and Mabel Batchelor and Florence Quin, as
Frasquita and Mercedes, earned the word that
cheers. This (Thursday) evening “Carmen” will
be repeated, with Georgina Delmar and Frederick
Blarney as the principals. On Friday, Bel Sorel
and Roberts will agaiu take charge; and on
Saturday Amy Castles will appear for the first
time as Santuzza in “ Cavalleria Rusticana,” with
Roberts as Turiddu ; • and, later on, as Nedda in
“ I Pagliacci,” with Blarney as Canio.

Carreno’s quaternion of concerts came to
an end on Saturday night, when the great player,
with whom none take the liberty of criticism, in-
terpreted Chopin, Beethoven, Schumann, Tschai-
kowsky and Rubenstein in the higher flights of
some of those great Masters. The season ended,
Carrefio has gone to the mountains for a fort-
night’s recuperation.,,, A victorious wrestle with
the old and modern Masters generally calls for a
holiday afterwards. But on Tuesday, Thursday
and Saturday, October 11th, 13th and 15th, she
will make her positively last appearances in
Sydney. Therq is Benno Scherek’s word on it.
Afterwards Scherelc. will tour the Mistress of the
Ivories through South Africa.

“The Little Breadwinner,” which was
produced at Sydney Palace on Saturday, was billed
as “ the most charming and delightful of modern
child plays ” ; and after that warning the congre-
gation attended at its own risk. “The Littk
Bread winner” is not so much a play as a sermon.
Observe the plot: William Dorrington, lord by
accident and wowser by inclination, adopts
Richard Lawrence, who (a) is Gay but not
Wicked ; ( b ) falls in love with the aged wowser’s
granddaughter; (c) attends racecourses ; (d) knows
a bookie; ( e ) owes him a few quid; (/) is
accused by a serving Mary Ann in the house of
that offence which invariably causes the hero to
throw his fair forehead roofward and cry, “My
Gawd, it’s a wicked lie ! ” ; and (g) is booted out
of the ancestral home or monastery. Margaret
Daventry, the granddaughter, goes on the wallaby
with him. She marries him because he has no
money and is out of a job. Joseph Prior, confi-
dential secretary and private hypocrite, is the
cause of all the trouble —having pulled the leg of
old Judkins concerning Richard Lawrences
carrying-on, with the object of eventually eating
the ancestral cake. The hero and heroine, having
no more brains than the average followers of those
occupations, promptly starve when there is
nothing doing but work. As a matter of
fact, the lady goes stone blind looking for
a square meal. Fortunately, they have a
child who is able to take in washing and go out
Singing (in a manner of speaking). They are thus
enabled to live in comfort with Charles Prior
(brother of Joe), who, although a wealthy bookie,
threw his bag away to follow his pore, stone-broke
friend and help eat the hard-working child out
of house and home. After several years old Dor-
rington relents, the serving Marianne admits
that her diabolical accusation was a bluff insti-
gated by the confidential hypocrite, the said o.h. is
seruffed like a dog by a village constable with a
lop-sided nose, and everybody says “Good enough
for the Cow,” and goes home. And all is peace.
* * * *
Charles Brown, as the venerable snuffle-
buster whose intelligence is as thick and
easy-working as the dust-coat of a tortoise,
plays the part as naturally as if he had
spent his life in a Methodist Retreat; but
the rest of the people do their work by
rote and without deviation from the grand old
traditions of Drury Lane. The biggest share of
the audience’s appreciation is served out to
Queenie Williams, who appears in the name-
part, but it is mostly prompted by the maudlin
sentimentality which finds an outlet in nose-sniffs
and heart-bursting applause. Nevertheless, the
young lady deserves an honest word of praise, and
is offered it herewith. “The Little Bread-
winner” will continue to earn a crust until further
notice.
** * *
, Grade Grahame, the lady who boomed in
the days of Anderson’s “Wonderland,” has come
back to live up to the Tivoli programme’s
description—“ a box of mischief.” Miss Grahame
still owns the same old infectious smile ; but her
songs mostly require to be assisted with knowing
nods and winks and an occasional giggle l .
Dressed in academic robes, she looks through a
small telescope at different members of the audi-
ence, affects to regard them as stars, and invites
them to twinkle. Between the verses, she kneels
at the footlights and invites someone in the stalls
to “ hold her hand till the tram goes by.” (Roars
of laughter.) The audience is half the time
craning its neck to see what stallite she is
addressing. This sort of thing uses up the time
which other soubrettes fill in with dancing.
Morris Cronin’s humorous juggling remains the
large hunk of the cake of entertainment. Tom
Dawson’s songs make another fair-sized slice.
Barnes and West. Olga Grey, the Quealys, and
the Australian Gold Diggers’quartette supply
therest. The Newman modelaeroplanedemonstr;i-
tion is absorbing, and makes the onlooker hunger
to see a full-sized machine at work.

Nothing much can be said about the
Sydney Picture Shows this week, as the Lyceum
and West’s Glaciarium are due for a change of
programme at a time when the printer begins to
take charge of things, and the mere writer becomes
a person of no consequence. Nevertheless, one
cannot refrain from breathing a word of praise over
the splendid series that Spencer’s people secured
of the Children’s Demonstration at the Sydney
Cricket Ground. The whole scheme was excel-
lent. It is rumored that Spencer will, on his re-
turn from Europe, start a theatre where plays will
be staged and cinematographed, and Australians
will have an opportunity of seeing Australian
mummers in Australian scenes on films made
from start to finish in Australia. It is to be
hoped it’s a true bill. At the Glaciarium
the summer rush has commenced, and duiing
the few hot nights that have transpired,
the merry management has tickled the taste of
the gazers with some pictures in which there is an
unlimited water supply—the sort of supply which,
could Prime Minister Fisher capture that Djin
from the Brass Bottle, he would let loose in the
wastes of Yassberra.
The keenest visioned politician, when his
eyes are bandaged by parochial interests, can see
no further through a stone wall than the mentally
blind. But the thoughtful who clarify their
visions with inspiring glances at the amber light
of D.C.L., the Whisky of the Wise, never have
to grope in intellectual gloom. D.C.L. flashes
the cheerful sunshine of wisdom into the darkest
of mental fogs.*
“THE CHANCE OF A LIFETIME.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sydney Cri. achieved, last week, that show
Where real, live thoroughbreds pervade the boards,
And stage-folk through the weirdest antics go
’Mid spielers, bookies, stable-boys and lords.
The piece abounds in marvels—that’s a cinch—
Yet all are tame compared with Captain Clinch.
Clinch is a man whose blank fatuity
And cold, considered wickedness combined
Must fill psychologists with morbid glee.
He is a type whom students of the mind
Pursue, as Lancelot did the Holy Grail,
And in their quest (what wonder ?) mostly fail.
Clinch, in his role.of sharper, makes a bid
For Trixie—as he doesn’t get the mare,
He “ lays ” her with her owner and a Yid
(A worthy soul) in thousands. Why the pair
Are mad enough to book a single bet
With one like Clinch has got me puzzled yet.
Some blackmail follows ; after which the Cap.
Essays to burgle Trixie from her stall.
He’s promptly foiled. What odds! This slight mishap
Disturbs the gallant fellow not at all.
His lexicon ignores the word “ success,”
And yet he does : not machinate the'less.
His asinine attempt to “ square ” the lad
Who rides the Gold Cup fav’rite fails, of course ;
As does his plot—audacious ; in fact, mad—
To have the jockey “ outed ” on the horse.
You’d think the Captain’s game, by now, was up?
Not so. He hires a pal to steal the Cup.
Alas! The Cap.’s innate incompetence
Benders him wholly powerless to hold
The goblet which another’s skill and sense
Have won. Once more 'mid hoots and jeers he’s
bowled »
Right out. He hooks it—to the last a crass,
Crude malefactor and a tragic ass.
N.s.w.
D.

In the present revival of “The Sign of
the Cross,” as was mentioned last week, Mel-
bourne will pay its last respects to Julius Knight’s
manly limbs, which, by the way, have now
arrived at full maturity. The wily old melo-
drama drew a crowded audience last Saturday, so
its fortnight will doubtless be prosperous. Judged
by the rules of comparison with rival attractions,
“ The Sign ” deserves to prosper. This is not the
best or second-best all-round performance of
“The Sign” that Melbourne has seen, but it has
points of particular merit. Cyril Mackay is a
most forcible Licinius, whilst Winter Hall makes
Tigellinus a dignified, subtle, matter-of-fact per-
son. Miss Gleeson is an admirable Stephanus,
and Miss Nellie Calvin, as the irresponsible
Dacia, would be as charming as she is natural but
for her tripk of gasping her lines. On the other
hand, Sydney Stirling is a weak and colorless
Nero, Miss Wilson is an over-demonstrative
Berenice, and Miss Warwick is a merely tall and
beautiful Mercia. The indications of early Chris-
tian hysteria are lacking in her voice, and her
acting is tame.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the first
week of “ The Whip” left the management with
nothing more to desire in the way of business.
Minus its two principal “ sensation ” scenes, this
would be a sufficiently big melodrama, though not
as strong in interest as, say, “ The White Heather.”
With the amazing railway accident and the re-
markable horse race added to its less unreason-
able attractions, “The Whip” is, seemingly,
a “bonza.” Tumultuous applause greets the
achievement of the “express” engine, which
collides with the empty horse-box and shat-
ters it at one fell tap. The audience is
too excited to notice that the onrushing train
comes to a full stop when it has assaulted the
fragile vehicle in the rear. And, inasmuch as the
true purpose of melodrama is to paralyse the pub-
lic’s critical faculty, the stage-manager—Wybert
Stamford—may be complimented on the Great
Railway Smash. It divides first prize for popu-
larity with Henry Claude, the hoarse-voiced boy
at the Waxworks. A minor mechanical effect in
“ The Whip ” is the motor accident in the first act.
This has been giving the manager an anxious time.
At the dress-rehearsal of the drama a live man
of ordinary rank impersonated the noble earl
who gets hurled from the motor; but the luckless
counterfeit happened to break his leg in the
catastrophe. After this disheartening experi-
ence the realism of a live man was withdrawn in
favor of a small dummy earl, to whom the acci-
dent didn’t matter. But the dummy, being light
and uncontrollable, used to get hurled too far out
of the motor-car, and sometimes he struck the
beautiful summer sky on the back cloth, and biffed
so violently against a fleecy cloud that sawdust
flew out of him. Therefore, in the interests of
realism, the scandalous effigy was taken off the
job, and another real man, with a talent for falling
soft, is now rising to the occasion.
** * *
“ The Winning Ticket,” at the King’s, is
now in its third week and thusfar the rough-edged
Australian horse drama with its mostly plebeian
characters —the only “titled” person being the
wandering villain who is a French Count —pro-
mises to stick to the girths of the gilt-edged
favorite from Drury Lane. Everybody, in a way
of speaking, must see “ The Whip,” and to have
seen Williamson’s show is to have a terrible crav-
ing to see Anderson’s show. Even the most
exalted scorners of Australian manufacture
are yielding to the temptation. An old
Spanish proverb says, “First the man takes
a drink. Then the drink takes a drink.
Then the drink takes the man.” But The
Bulletin trusts that man will stop at the
second drink of hippodrama, and not allow it to
get him fairly down. The railway train-cwm-
water-jump scene in “The Winning Ticket ” has
been elaborated of late, and nowanights Red-
grave, the wicked Count, shifts the swing bridge
in the first place, which is a reasonable thing to
do, in order that Beckett may shift it back into
position before the tram arrives. The horrors of
this situation should be less hurriedly piled up,
however. The tempo is too rapid, Anderson
will be having a death in the audience, if he
doesn’t mind.
** * *
“Sergeant Brue,” the quaint farcical
comedy interrupted by musical items, is putting
in one week at Melbourne Royal. The company now
gathered around Cromwell, as Crookie Scrubbs,
is widely different from the former co. Brue, in
the hands of Percy Clifton, is a more possible and
less amusing policeman than the departed Brett
made him. Miss Gilliam has less voice than her
predecessor; also Shortland, as Mr. Crank, tends
to feebleness of expression. But Crookie amounts
to more than half the absurdity, and Cromwell is
as raucously droll as heretofore. On Saturday
comes the new comic opera—“ Tom Jones” —with
a new prima donna in the part of Sophia Western.
Edward German’s music is said to be fine, and the
book of the opera runs presumably on the lines of
Buchanan’s adaptation from the original story,
which Brough and Boucicault staged 20 years ago.
Dolores was welcomed, on Saturday night,
in the Melbourne Town Hall by what was obvi-
ously a room-full of Dolores admirers. The
event suggests that other fine artists (Carreiio,
for example) may be encouraged to regard Mel-
bourne as a good city to come back to. Dolores
appears to be classed as a comet due once
every four years; and, as comets lack the
monotony of fixed stars, her advent, with
lustre undimmed, is an occasion that justifies
the warm hand of welcome. On Saturday night
there was a clear atmosphere, and the display
attained unusual brilliance, and covered no less
than 18 song flashes. They ranged from the
florid music of Verdi’s Rigoletto, through two
Schumann songs, to the simple melody of McCoy,
whoever he may be. When, in the McCoy song,
Dolores warbled: “If I were a clover, and you
were a bee, would you seek me?” the audience
hadn’t to think twice for the right answer. The
industry displayed in bringing to light many
songs of other times opens up a new charm in
concert singing. Four songs on Saturday s pro-
gramme dated from 1700 to 1827. A McDowell song
was a reminder that Clarence Newell, the Cali-
fornian pianist, who came out with Dolores
some years ago, introduced the music of
that American composer to Australia. By the
way, Dolores always brings an American pianist.
Newell is remembered as a youthful and unaf-
fected performer, with an unprofessional scorn for
the matted skull-thatch. Franz Boyd Wells was
of the same type, and now we have Selwyn
Shrimplin, who is nowhere near Paderewski as
regards hair. All three, however, play accom-
paniments that ripple like echoes of the Dolores
song notes.
#* * *
The latest comer to speak of at the Opera
House is Larry Lewis, who brings no air of
novelty with him. Barring an increase in avoir-
dupois, he is the same gentle Larry as of old, with
the same outstanding jacket. Also, he remarks
with undiminished veracity that his song is silly.
Powell and Vesey are giving a new sketch in
bright style, the Charlenes make good their claim
to be “piquant musical experts,” Bluett is still
scouting, and Meymott continues to want his
tooth drawn so that he can put it on the mantel-
pieqe and watch it ache.

The toe-ball semi-final between Carlton
and South Melbourne is being reproduced at
Melbourne Olympia. The picture is curiously
interesting, for it shows the football face in
various weird and woeful expressions. Still more
attractive is the film of the Australian destroyer
Yarra flinging the sea into corrugations of foam.
The mawkish bit is supplied by “Convict 796,”
which urges the virtuesof hymns as moralspecifics.
In this case it is the strains of “Lead, Kindly
Light ” that melt the malefactor’s ’ard ’eart.

“ The Bandit’s Wife,” which nightly
hastens o’er the sheet at Melbourne Athenaeum,
is probably founded on a yarn by the late O.
Henry. Anyhow, the tragedy of the lady who is
shot by her lover in mistake for her husband is
reminiscent of a story by the brilliant American.
There is a suspicion of real comedy about another
item, “ The Midnight Cupid,” which is a tale of an
imposition marching to the tune of Love’s Young
Dream. A further newcomer is “Purgation,” a
fable of a young man’s sins and his final redemp-
tion, highly adulterated with the saccharine stuff
which most picture-women absorb with a glutton-
ous pleasure.
** * *
Three new efforts in Australian dramatic
literature will be staged at the pretty little Turn
Verein Hall on Wednesday night next week.
Louis Esson, William Moore and Miss Prichard
are the trio of dramatists, and outside of their
interesting contributions to the bill there will be
recitations and things—including coffee. No
seats will be reserved on this Bohemian occasion.
The price will be 3s. 6d. to any part of the hall,
which accommodates 700 people. An intellectual
atmosphere ordinarily prevails at Turn Verein
gatherings, but in this instance the air will be
several degrees over-proof, and by persons in
need of divine afflatus the opportunity should not
be missed.
* # *
The English Opera has ended a striking
and, on the whole, successful season at Adelaide
Royal, with Amy Castles making a plucky effort
to look like the consumptive heroine of “La
Boheme.” The group of men played up to her
finely. Now “ The Third Floor Back ” has come
in. Adelaide reckons itself just the city to ap-
preciate drama with an evangelical tinge, and the
inevitable controversy has begun already. On
first impressions, the weakness of the play as a
play is the unfailing way in which one bird after
another comes down as the Stranger shoots. It
saysmuch for the acting that a feeling of monotony
can be avoided. The Tivoli has the ten Kremos.
One wonders how many more of the seemingly
endless family have been spoiled in the making—
fractured or dislocated for life while working up
to the present pitch of daring and success. The
Yeamans-Titus lady has come in; she still does
baby impersonations, though she is getting quite
a big girl now. Mendel has wisely moved on, just as
he was tending to become monotonous. He might
be better stage-managed, and one hopes he will
soon have made money enough to devote himself
to his art. It was quite pathetic to see him listen-
ing to Carreiio, and a man who can on a sudden
demand improvise on “ Alas, Those Chimes ! ” in
the unmistakable manner of Wagner—as he did
one night last week —deserves a better fate than
having to play two tunes at once while he bluffs
at singing a third. Concerts are everywhere,
notably two at which the massed forces of the
State schools crowded the Exhibition with a de-
lighted audience of parents and others. The Elder
Conservatorium backed up this venture in. the
most sporting way by lending its orchestra.
MUSINGS ON MELBOURNE’S MELODRAMAS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Nero reigned in mighty Rome there were no motor-
cars,
No railway trains, or jockey boys, or whisky and cigars ,
No Paris fashions ruled the dames and cramped their
walking gait;
Men’s garments then were not the same as now are up-
to-date ;
And sinners sinned without disguise, and left the public
park
To Christians, who assembled there, in secret, after dark.
In those good old dramatic days of Pagan unbelief,
The wicked triumphed in the end, and Virtue came to
grief.
So, having gazed upon “The Whip,” and seen the Bridge
that Swings—
The one is at Her Majesty’s, the other at the King’s-
Seen one gee win the Guineas, and another win the Cup,
This bard with sporting drama has been latterly fed up ;
And he beams on bare-legged Marcus in the Wilson
Barrett play,
And he nods to Tigellinus in a friendly sort of way.
For the gory times of Nero, when the motor-car was not,
And the scheming of the villains didn’t constitute the
plot,
To him seem more artistic, in their garb and moral tone,
Than these days of racing dramas which include a
telephone.
It is good to lose the present in a dream of long a-gone,
When the lions ate the Wowsers, and the bad men held
their own.
VICTORIA.
O.K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“The Whip,” from a racing man’s point of view, has
one virtue. Other sporting melodramas have been pre-
posterously impossible in their betting transactions.
The stone-broke hero almost invariably made a. fortune
by laying odds on , or at least even money on, the. gee-gee
tnat the villain meant to “ nobble.” Every reference to the
market odds was an insult to a punter’s understanding,
But in “ The Whip ” the people talk reasonably. The
sporty girl tells her best earl, in confidence, that the
dark horse in her noble grandpa’s stable has won its trial
by 20 lengths, and suggests that he shall back it with a
leviathan bookmaker who happens to be drunk and reck-
less just then. A very wealthy bookmaker,doing business
in a drunken, vainglorious state, is not a feasible proposi-
tion, but he comes within the bounds of dramatic license.
In this case the “ plunging” earl catches Joe Kelly fairly
on the bound and asks him to lay the odds in thousands,
to which Joe responds with an offer to oblige his lordship
three times over. Then he quotes his prices—level £3OOO
against the favorite or £15,000 to £3OOO against the second
favorite. Having named another horse or two without
drawing the wager, he is challenged to quote his price
against the field. He offers 20 to 1 and the punter names
the dark horse, The Whip, and pots him for the full
amount —£60,000 to £3OOO. Whereupon, Kelly naturally
surmises that he has been taunted into a trap, throws his
betting-book down and curses himself for a blanky fool.
Subsequently, the wicked Captain, having also got the
“ tip,” tries to back The Whip with the furious Joe afore-
said, who tells him of the big bet he has laid. Then the
Captain acquaints Joe with the full strength of that noble
animal’s trial, and the plot to stop it from winning is
quite naturally evolved.
“Busk”: What a poor view Australia has had of
Matheson Lang ! Consider the great parts he has acted,
such as Hamlet, Romeo, Othello, Joseph Surface, Mal-
volio, David Garrick, etc. In “ The Merry Wives of
Windsor” he has been Ford and even Falstaff. His
Petruchio would be agreeable after Asche’s and Kolker’s.
Surely the old-time starring plan was best for drawing
out a man’s powers. Brook or Sullivan, on tour, would
change the bill every night; and such was the practice
of Lang in touring the West Indies and South America.
What things are “ The Third Floor Back ” and “ Pete ” to
draw forth the great powers demanded by the legitimate !
Why not give the opportunity of cojppaping Lang’s Ham-
let with H. B. Irving’s? A pretty man with no talent
might give as much satisfaction in walking through "The
Third Floor Back.” Bother the scenery! Give us two
boards and a passion, as Dumas said.
“ Stargazer ” : Miss Marie Illington, who plays the
Hon. Mrs. Beamish in “ The Whip ” at Melb. Majesty’s,
told a reporter on the day of her arrival: “ When lam
not acting, I am dressing. I took a prize offered by a
London paper for the best-dressed woman.” After that, I
was prepared to find Miss Marie Illington a mere stick
—a clothes-prop, so to speak—instead of which the lady
proved to be a remarkably good, quiet comedienne, a
smooth actress, mellowed by long experience, with
nothing objectionable about her but her clothes. She
wore the intolerable hobble skirt, more hobbled than
usual, restricting her stage stride so absurdly that
throughout the show the audience was watching for the
apparently inevitable headlong dive into ttie orchestra.
That ever-impending catastrophe quite smothered the
interest in the drama’s terrific railway smash. I hope
Miss Illington will presently forget she once took a first
prize for frocks. I never saw an actress so worn by her
clothes.

less”: We got a
hint, the other
day, as to where
some of these
juvetiiled ancers
come from, and
also as to the
way they come.
It was at th ■
Glebe (Sydney)
police court, where a woman was prosecuted for not
sending her little daughter to school. The lady
haughtily explained that she was having the infant in-
structed in the piano, violin, Shaksptare and step-
dancing ; and, as she found she couldn’t crowd in arith-
metic, grammar and the other frills, she just dropped

“S.S.: “The Whip” is an all-English melodrama. It
dots not at any moment acknowledge the existence of
the Commonwealth of Australia. And yet, on the first
night, Ambrose Manning, comedian, was permitted to
“localise” by dragging Willie Judkins into certain comic
reflections on Madame Tussaud’s waxworks. Ordinarily,
you have only to say “ Judkins ”in aMelbourne theatre to
provoke a roar of laughter ; but the incongruity of Man-
ning’s jape struck even a first-night audience, and pro-
voked a solemn and oppressive silence akin to that of the
tomb. As first President of the Association for Ignoring
Judkins, I wish to put on record the above gratifying
circumstance.
“ 0.K.” : Matheson Lang, before leaving Melbourne for
the West, produced himself in a verra Scotch character —
he “ entertained at lunch a few of his friends among
Scottish residents of Melbourne, including the Prime
Minister.” Lang hasn’t Asche’s good opinion of Aus-
tralians as critics. But Lang can hardly complain of the
start he got in Sydney and Melbourne. The trumpets
blew a hurricane at the approach of himself and his wife,
and if he had been a really strong dramatic actor, with
high-class plays and a striking leading lady, he would have
gone for a bit above his value anyhow. But the plays were
a novel “morality” comedy, in which he tried to
force the effect, and a mawkish melodrama that had no
novelty whatever. The Sydney mistake of opening the
season with “Pete,” and laying too much stress upon the
hlessed baby, was not repeated in Melbourne, where
“The Passing of the Third Floor Back ” got first chance.
Had Lang’s “Stranger” not been so suggestive of a
Divine Being in Holy Orders, and had Rose Musgrove.for
example, taken the part of the Slavey, there might have
been an eight weeks’ run for the morality comedy. It
has at. least one exceedingly spicy line in it.
“R. 8.”: The cabled terms on which Lina Cavallieri,
opera-bouffe prima-donna, of Paris, agreed to marry
Robert Winthrop Chanler are interesting as a sample of
the lady’s gall. Robert transferred to her all his personal
estate, worth a million dollars or so, also 20,000 dollars a
year for hairpin money. Lina’s interview with a Parisian
journalist was calmly and unconsciously funny. She said
she had had to make many sacrifices in marrying Mr.
Chanler and thus rewarding his devotion. He knew, she
said, of her stormy past, and appreciated the fact that
she had given up all her lovers. Robert Winthrop sat
there during the interview and beamed. Lina was a
Roman flower girl, and her great beauty—beauty of
feature only, for she has the mind of a phonograph and
the soul of a doll—attracted many counts. (There
are thousands of counts in Italy, because if a count has
12 sons they are all counts, and if each of these sons has
12 sons, the original count becomes a gross of counts in
two generations.) A marquis fought a count for her,
and stabbed him with his bodkin, which was just as
well, for a marquis is not so numerous in Italy as a count.
Lina’s voice production was paid for by some abundant
count or marquis, and in its way her thin mezzo is a
triumph of cultivation, for it was created from scales and
a voice that was originally only good to eat with. And
now Robert Winthrop succeeds ; and his succession will
probably be a failure. The lady who talks of self-abne-
gation with a cloistral air, because she has given up many
counts to marry one American, is too cool a proposition
for matrimony. She is a cold storage.
J. Edward Sykes, the Sydney musician with the moun-
tainous button-hole, is to have a benefit on December 16.
Sykes, besides being one of the soundest musicians in
Australia, has the sympathy that helps to make the few
really good accompanists that any country owns; and
Dolores wants to show her appreciation of J. Edward in
that capacity by warbling at the benefit. And when
Dolores wants to warble, a committee doesn’t need much
pressing to let her have her way.
Edwin Geach’s Dramatic Qo. had a week’s rest at
Winton, in Western Queensland, recently. A motor
lorry, carrying the luggage and scenery from Longreach,
broke down on the way, and when it was repaired rain
set in, and the buzz-cart had to wait for some days till
the black soil had dried up. Meanwhile, the villain
forgot his wickedness, and the heroine neglected her
che-ild, and the comic relief behaved like a ratibnal
being, and the manager made unprintable remarks, while
the ticket office was an aching void.
THE “STOCK” ACTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His name is Monty Banagher.
You meet him at the whisky joint.
When no good fellow leads him there
He loiters sadly on the Point,
With one eye roaming near and far,
And one fixed firmly on the bar.
He wears a suit of rusty black,
A wide-rimmed hat of rakish shape,
A cape-like cloak, thrown grandly back,
And boots from which his feet escape.
Sometimes he gets a shave on tick.
At sniping lunch he’s neat and quick.
His hair is long, his brow severe.
He is an actor, well I know,
For oft, in confidence and beer,
The gentleman has told me so.
“ I am,” exclaims the stately mime,
“ The great stock actor of me time.”
It is quite true, although his friends
Of Monty often make a mock ;
The great “ stock ” actor’s talent ends
Where it begins—portraying stock.
In fact, doe 3 he, at Christmas time,
Enact the cow in pantomime !

SOCIETY
DANGER!
“ Gripes! Guess I've struck an armored cruiser.”
The most unconsciously-humorous man in
Melbourne is a converted psalm-smiter who lately
referred to Sam Mauger as a “wowser.” The
unconscious humorist, it seems, was formerly a
devoted Calathumpian with only one failure. He
used to buy a Melbourne Gup ticket in Tatt.’s
every year. A few years ago his ticket drew first
prize, and with £6OOO coming to him he promptly
proposed to give £5OO to the church in which he
was a shining light. But the other elders would
have none of it. They railed against his sinful
gamble and bade him return his prize. He sug-
gested that they would like the whole lot for the
church. They threw up their hands and said :
“Send it all back.” Finding that he couldn’t
save his soul without relinquishing the £6OOO, he
retired from his pew and became an inveterate
foe to wowserism in all its bearings. This shows
that even Calathumpian principles are amenable
to financial argument. •
REPARTEE.
H E: Providence neglected to endow woman with a sense of the ridiculous .”
S HE: “ Naturally—knowing she would have to live with man.”
Following the example of the brave
Maorilander who cured himself of nervous dys-
pepsia by fasting for 52 days, a courageous dairy
farmer of Goulburn (N.S.W.) lately threw down
the plate of battle and defied his waistcoat for
42 days. Just to remind it that it was not ex-
pected to retire from business altogether, he
allowed it three cups of coffee daily, with a little
milk and sugar. For the first six days, Waist-
coat called him a Socialist, and threatened to
leave the country; but on the seventh day it
recognised it had a hero to deal with, and sub-
mitted so gracefully that five foodless and almost
driukless weeks went by in peace and harmony.
At the end of the test, the waistcoat-conqueror,
who had kept on doing his work as usual, found
he had lost 51b. and his indigestion. He now
only approaches the dining-table once every 24
hours ; also, he has sworn off intoxicating meat,
and wouldn’t step across the road to have a chop
with Judkins if that great reformer asked him
on his knees. Fearful possibilities now loom up
through the political fog. Within a year or so,
wild-eyed and inaccurate zealots will probably be
denouncing the Cursed Steak, and telling spell-
bound audiences how they witnessed the horri-
ble spectacle of 39 meat-gorged infants reeling
out of one of the most brilliantly-lighted but-
chers’ shops in Sydney.
* * * *
“0.K.”: If Australia’s defence system
is to be preserved from superfluous frill and
finery, it needs a clause in the Act to prohibit
officahs from masquerading in scarlet and gilt
buttons at places of public enter-
tainment. At Her Majesty’s
(Melb.)the other night a hundred
or so militaires were gathered in
dazzling array in the dress circle,
and, presumably, other thin red
lines of superior warriors were in
the stalls. The lieutenants, cap-
tains and majahs were not all so
glaring as Solomon in all his
glory. Some of them had blue
or buff jackets, but gilt buttons
were common to all, save two of
three (generals and commanders-
in-chief, perhaps) who wore
medals on their black coats to
prevent their being mistaken for
beastly civilians. Why this
popinjay demonstration ? Had
the gallant fellows heard that
“ The Whip ” —the melodrama
they were patronising—makes a
display of hunting costumes, and
were they bent on showing the
world that officahs in uniform
are fit to adorn the stage ?
The effect, really, was most distracting. One
couldn’t fix one’s gaze upon the far-off scarlet
supers whilst these fine fellows were asserting
their splendid presence in the foreground. Why
did they do it—and do they do it frequently ?
* * * *
The incredible has happened, and a land
shindykit has been formed in Sydney which
doesn’t aim at making a profit. It happened this
way. Archbishop Wright has refused to live
at Bishops-court, Randwick, alleging that it is
too far from the city, so churchmen have decided to
purchase the old home of T. S. Mort, at Darling
Point. This, however, could not be done until
Bishops-court had been melted down into cash.
So a syndicate was formed to purchase the pro-
perty, with the object of allowing the Arch-
bishop to get the manger he wants without having
to pawn his crook and the collection plate.
At latest advices, the 9-ton yawl Pandora,
in which Captains Blyth and Arapakis propose
sailing round the globe, was in Maoriland waters,
and had already been given a taste of the dis-
respect which old-man Neptune has for freak
craft in that vicinity. Bad luck has befallen
nearly every one of them after reaching the
country where the single crazy thing one can be
sure to meet afloat is a loan. Voss, in the Tilikum,
was about the only man that dodged the
curse of the Maoriland sea. The Kia Ora,
which was constructed at Auckland
for the round-the-world trip, was
heaved ashore on the East Coast after
a heated argument with a gale, and
Buckeridge and Isaac (the crew) were
chased right up the beach by D.
Jones. That cured Isaac; but Bucker-
idge patched up his craft, collected
another crew of one, and sailed into
the Sea of Mystery. One day, long
afterwards, the Kia Ora crept slowly
into Waitemata harbor, with the soli-
tary crew just sufficiently alive to
tell how Buckeridge had injured
himself at sea, and had died and been
buried there. The next attempt was
made by Felix Tanner, who built
a miniature Ark on the plans laid
down by the Original Shipbuilder. He
sailed out of New Plymouth ; but was
rescued soon afterwards and presented
to the ■ Society that deals with the
Apparently Drowned. Felix gave up
constructing Arks, and took a hand at
constructing a gallows; but he had bad
luck at that, too. He put a patent
frame on the market; but, owing to lack
of enterprise, no hangman would buy it.
There is a lot of conservatism about
hangmen, who are among the most con-
sistent supporters the Right-Thinking
party and the Property Defence League
possess.
The mysterious disappearance of a gentle-
man named Stoltz, occupied the attention of the
Bankruptcy Court in Sydney recently, and re-
vealed a case lull of difficulties. Stoltz, sometime
in June, went out in a boat and never came back.
The day he went away was unusually fine, hardly
a ripple on the water. The boat in which he
paddled out from Woolloomooloo Bay was subse-
quently found afloat off Wollongong, on the
south coast, 48 miles from where Stoltz started
his journey. In it were found Stoltz’s macintosh,
pipe and tobacco pouch, but no sign of Stoltz.
rpiT i
The people who were anxious to see hull and the
color of his cash presumed that he had Jett the
State and was staying away to avoid the un-
pleasantness of parting up. This supposition was
given some color by the fact that, shortly before
he went boating, he had several trunks painted up,
renovated and removed from his chambers. An
other witness swore that Stoltz had told him he
intended shortly to go to Germany, and had not
added any information as to how long he intended
to stay. Nobody brought out any suggestion as
to how Stoltz had got out of the boat and escaped,
and, as the man had loft his macintosh, pipe and
pouch behind him, there seems to be more than a
possibility that Stoltz has not bolted from L.S
creditor friends, but retired to his long home.
Even if he were going to Germany, he would
probably have taken his pipe and pouch with
him. A man would want to smoke on the way to
Germany, while on the track to Abraham's
bosom smoking is severely discouraged.
Complete disappearance alive is a fine art in
which but few people attain any particular pro-
ficiency. When the Devonshire-street (Sydney)
Cemetery was removed, there were several cases
in which there was more than a suggestion that
the gentleman whose name was on the tombstone
had not been buried there. In one instance a fine
leaden coffin, in a vault, had been plundered for
the value of the lead, and the gentleman who had
done the burglary had left a note to the effect
that, when he broke in, he could find no trace of
the original inhabitant, and that, in his humble
opinion, “ the bloke had done a bunk and never
been buried at all.” Another coffin, which was m
a good state of preservation, was found to be
unusually heavy, and was smashed by being
bumped on the floor. Then it was discovered
to have been occupied by a bag of sand which
turned the scale at 12 stone, the weight of
a well-set-up man. The removers had doubts
about the genuineness of that funeral. Another
disappearance worried the police some time back.
The man had worked in an office for many years.
One afternoon a lady floated in and threw a
smile. The man—a Scotsman —bounded up,
hugged the lady three times, reached his hat and
blew out. He never came back, and when they
came to sort things out it was discovered that he
had forgotten to leave about £lBO of the office s
cash. Of course, it was quite inadvertent, as the
girl was pretty enough to make any man forget a
trifle like that. But where he went to, or who
the lady was, the police never found out. It was
a most complete and artistic disappearance.

From The Bulletin’s Victorian railway-
accident reporter :
The line between Clunes and Talbot is one of the
safest to travel on in the State. This isn’t because they
don’t have accidents there, but because they have them
so often that the wreckage now knows exactly how to
pile itself so as to escape doing murder or serious bodily
harm. No one ever suffers more than a scratch, although
trains get broken up wholesale. A while ago, a whole
outfit fell over a bridge into a canyon filled with rushing
Judkins ale, and no passenger even got tickled, although
the smash was so complete that farmers miles down the
creek found carriage doors and windows mixed up with
their crops next day. Since then trains have been leaving
the rails through force of habit about eight times a
month, and the fact isn’t even thought worth mentioning.
On Monday of last week a frolicsome “ mixed ” turned
itself and a quarter of a mile of permanent way
into hash inside half a minute, but delivered the
goods (in the shape of about 40 passengers) so
safely that the latter thought it another of Tait’s little
jokes, and dug each other in the ribs and wondered what
the great man would be up to next. I went out to have a
look at the wreck. As a wreck, it was worth going to

PRO PATRIA EDERE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is sweet and glorious to dine for one’s country.—
Horace (amended).
George Reid, at luncheon, in a stirring speech, proposed
“ Tlie Royal Navy.”— Commonkind of cable nowadays.
“ George Reid, at luncheon, in a strring speech,
Proposed the Royal Navy.”
Brethren, your hearing do I now beseech
The while I sing- a song your hearts to reach—
A Song of Grub and Gravy !
A man like a puncheon
Went out to a luncheon,
He'd the gift o’ the gab, and effront’ry ;
There was no one more able,
At bar or at table,
To feed, or be fee’d, for his country.
lie could joke like Joe Miller ;
His smile was a “killer” ;
Ilis buttonhole bore an azalea ;
He was gay, debonair,
And he sat in his chair
Determined to dine for Australia.
At a sup and a swoop
He disposed of the soup,
And a couple of glasses of Chablis ;
Hors d'am tires came after—
The smiles turned to laughter,
And talk became brisk and more bubbly.
A sole a la Russe
Vanished next, without fuss ;
Then there followed a fillet of turbot,
With oysters and soy ;
And he noted with joy
That the next course was lamb, a la Serb, hot.
A salmi of pheasant
He tried, was so pleasant
He tackled another big plateful !...
“ This is beautiful claret,”
He said—“ Do not spare it,
D.-ar Duke!” “Ya-as,” the Duke drawled;
“ de-late-ful!”
Our hero, so knightly,
By this, was grown sprightly,
All marvelled to note his abandon.
He would cry, “ Hence vvith grief 1
Bring me sirloin of beef,
And a magnum of Moet and Chandon ! ”
With cream and plum tart,
And asperges d la carte
(In England ’tis best in September),
Heroic G. Reid
Closed a notable feed,
With vintage port—dry as an ember.
With coffee and brandy,
And cigarettes handy,
And trophies of pears, pines and peaches,
He sat in his chair
Smiling, gay, debonair.
Prepared for the toasts and the speeches.
Australia, advance !
Gaze upon the expanse
Of shirt-front the kindly Fates lend you
To buckler your coasts ;
Then, drink deep in your toasts—
The Man Who Will Dine to Defend You!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

309-311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
Sydney has been filled with a grey haze ;
for every Dutch man-o’-warsman goes round town
with a cigar in his face, and thus knocks flat the
stage idea that every Van Dam has a vast pipe
curling down to his chin. But this is the Dutch
East Indian fleet that is in Australian waters;
and in the Dutch Indies cigar-tobacco costs less
than cabbage, and is a heap more plentiful. Java
and Borneo grow the bulk of the “filling’ ; and
Sumatra supplies the wrapping leaf. At Dobo,
on the way north, the Australian gets his first
taste of cheap tobacco. If he wants stuff for his
pipe he will probably have to buy English brands
and pay for them ; but a pocketful of cigarettes
can be had for a guelder and a half (2s. 6d.). He
will have to wait till he reaches Java to get cigars
thrown at him ; once there, he can buy them for
anything down to three cents, which is something
a shade over a ha’penny. But the Dutchman in
the East Indies doesn’t buy by ones and twos.
He gets in a thousand at a time, and the place
where he doesn’t smoke them hasn’t yet been
invented.
“The Religious Editor”: To be thor-
oughly efficient, a verger needs to be a swift and
silent dog-ejector. Wandering dogs are
always sneaking into church, and the
congregation has never yet been col-
lected that can listen attentively and
retain a reverent demeanor whilst
an over-awed cur is being hunted
and prodded through the sacred
edifice. The best dog-ejecting
verger I have ever seen at work
is employed at the church of the
Rev. Asbestos Mac Fireproof,
opposite my last place of abode.
He is a tall, grim, deliberate
man, with long arms and hands
like the claws of a grab dredge,
and he can gather up the most
degraded beast by the scruff of
its neck and bear it down the
aisle as easily as the ordinary
churchwarden carries a pile of
hymnbooks. It is hisinvariable
custom to take his handful of
wandering sin to the porch,
and, placing it on the top step
like a football, give it one hearty
kick to impress on whatever ser-
ves it for a mind that it is
not to enter there again. The
sin lets out a satisfying lost-
soul yelp, and, with its bruised
tail between its legs, flies like a
hairy arrow down the street.
Once a dog has been kicked out
of the church of the Rev. A.
Mac Fireproof, it never returns.
I have seen that verger, in his
solemn, professional way, suc-
cessfully kick out all sizes in
dogs, except the mastiff. I am
now engaged trying to win the
confidence and esteem of a vagrant
brute, about as big as a panther,
with the deliberate intention of getting
it to follow me in some night, when the
place is full, just to see if the champion will be
equal to the job. If you read, later on, of a
verger being eaten alive in church, whilst the
choir was singing “ O Let Us Be Joyful! ” you
will know that the dog won.

The Council of Melb. University invites
applications from qualified gentlemen for the new
position of Professor of English (language and
literature), and specifies that photographs of
applicants must accompany applications. The
Rev. E. Sugden opposed this. “It looks like
a beauty competition,” he said ; but he was over-
ruled. After all, it may not be a beautiful Pro-
fessor that the council is after ; they may desire
to have the new Professor known as “ Plain
English.”

There’s much gnashing of buck-fceeth and
wailing of “Whaffor?” in the China camps
around Fremantle (W.A.), and the Chow-grown
lettuce is for the moment a drug in the market.
Health-Officer Dr. Paget, with the Mayor, Town
Clerk and a councillor as a bodyguard, marched
into the vegetable area, and great is the smell of
the doctor’s report. Water is obtained by making
pot-holes two or three feet deep, and many of
these “ wells ” were discovered by the seekers
after light. The doctor said that they were
nothing less than fever-beds, swarming with
typhoid germs—and in these the Celestials rinse
their vegetables. Said the doctor : “That water
no good, John.’’ Said the Chow : WAter all
li’ ” —and grabbed a handful of the slime and
swallowed it. “All li’—you tly.” Ihe com-
mittee turned blue. Another Pong said he washed
his vegetables in a barrel, and pointed it out. Ihe
“ water ” also was thick, and the doctor opined it
was davs old. Said the doctor : “ Been there too
long. Too dirt}', John. Let it out.” Said the
Chow : “ Not dirty—l pullum out plug at bottom
each morning and fillum up again. You see me let
it out.” The heathen in his blindness couldn’t find
a plug. He smiled blandly and asked the visitors
to have some fruit.

G. B. Nicholls, one of the three apostles
who said he saw a flock of small boys reeling
drunk from “ one of the most brilliantly lighted
hotels in Sydney,” and whose present special job
is to picture (1) the horrors of Derrink, and (2)
the blessings of No License in Maori Lind for the
encouragement of N. S. Wales, contributing
articles to the Otago Daily Times (M.L.). Jins
is from one of the latest: —
One dav was spent in Woolloomooloo, where over 5000
people are packed as closely as herrings in a space 400yds.
lon«- and 200 wide. Here children are to be seen pla.y-
iixAn the narrow street right alongside the public-house
door, about which men and women in all stages of degra-
dation, and all alike ready to pour out tirades of hlthv
language upon the children’s heads if they are annoyed
in the slightest degree. As usually they are very easily
annoyed, the tirades are frequent. .. . The crowd of
vicious, weedy-looking, filthy-nnnded and filthy-tongued
gaol-birds that faced us when we held our open-air meet-
ing in this district is the natural and inevitable outcome
of'life under such conditions. Of this crowd of several
hundred, some 50 or 60 were fighting drunk.
A dark shadow of doubt fell on Nicholls when he
refused to specify the “ brilliantly-lighted hotel ;
and the question now arises, “If Nicholls gives
Maoriland such outrageous guff as the foregoing
about Australia, what reliance can be placed on
what he tells Australia about the state of things
in Maoriland?”

“ Sr’s ” dreadful tale of Show-time :
Father is an M.P., and when ma’s country relations
came to town she proudly explained that father cou d
show them everything, including Tait’s favorite wreck-
ages the Zoo, Pentridge and the draughts in Parliament
House. After covering that list, a desire was expressed
to have a look over a warship then in the bay. Accord-
ingly, father wrote to the Defence authorities asking
permission for himself and party to inspect the vessel,
and received an answer stating that the desired permis-
sion was granted, and that the ship’s officers were being
notified. On the day of the visit father was called away
to attend to a doubtful supporter, and we decided to
take the department’s letter and go without him. On
arrival at the pier a steam launch was waiting.
Uncle presented the letter, and aunt and the
kids were courteously handed aboard. As we ranged
alongside the vessel a bugle blared, and, climbing up the
«-an<?way, we found half the crew mustered in drill order.
A gold-braided agent met us, looked at the Defence de-
partment’s introduction, then at aunt and the kids, and
finally at the masthead flag. Meanwhile uncle and all of
us inspected the squad. Uncle seemed vexed when they
wouldn’t shake hands with him, and refused even to
answer when he asked, “Ow’d some ev yous chaps like
er job harvestin’?” After being shown over the ship, we
were offered biscuits and wine. Uncle encored the wine,
and having wiped his whiskers loudly, started to explain
the accident of our visit. In the middle of the explana-
tion an astonished officer showed me the departments
letter which stated that “ a Parliamentary party ’ would
attend, and the ship’s officers had made arrangements
for the reception of about a dozen large-sized M s.P. We
left that officer three points off his mental course, ana
when we slipped down the gangway there wasn t even a
bos’n’s whistle to say good-bye. The dead finish was at
the boat-staging ashore, where aunt offered a tip to the
lieutenant or major-domo in charge of the launch. I
could see him stamping on his hat in the stern-sheets all
the way back to the ship.

“The Achinese are very good fighters, sats the

’Twixt our Mrs. Billo and their Mrs. Achin
There is little to choose when the ructions begin.
For though A.’s in the Indies and B. may be found
In a dusty back street when the “demon” calls round ,
Or Dutchmen come marching wild husbands to tame,
A.’s methods and B.’s are exactly the same.
’Twixt a stronghold well-guarded, a front door well
barred,
A big fight in the forest, a “ scrap ” in the yard,
A crumpled-up Dutchman, a badly-mauled “ D,
The difference, if any, is one of degree.
The Indian jungles, the hack lanes of ’Loo,
Are just teeming with hearts that are faithful and true ;
Therefore, down goes the Dutchman, and down goes the
“ D f
Demonstrating that A is the equal of B.
NEW HOLLAND.
HENDRIE VAN MURPHY.
WHEN HOPES RAN HIGH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When hopes ran high the world was young ;
We thought that we would never die,
And glorious songs were proudly sung
In those grand days when hopes ran high.
When hopes ran high the world was true ;
We thought that friends could never lie.
There have been bitter truths for you,
And me, since days when hopes ran high.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

| A repetition of the Joke played upon
[the Customs officers by the Chinese
crew of the E. and A. liner Empire on
her previous visit to Melbourne took
place yesterday. Observing the launch
in their wake, the Chinese crew of the
Empire, amid much evident mirth, cast
[overboard a number of parcels, and glee-
fully watched the Customs launch stop to
recover them. WhiletheCustomsofficials
felt certain that the parcels, as on the
previous occasion, contained nothing but
rice-scrapings, they decided not to take
any chances. The parcels were accord-'
ingly taken from the water and ex-
amined for traces of contraband ; but, as
was suspected, they contained nothing
but dirty rice.

THE LEAKAGE.

“Tiger Snake” : As a belated contribu-
•tion to the native-bear endurance controversy, I
have to record the koala’s capability in another
direction. Bear was never intended by Nature to
do long-distance travelling ; nevertheless, one
will be met occasionally negotiating the big plains.
I met one, away back in 1884, between Conda-
mine Plains and St. Ronans. There was no
timber for five miles either side. The following
day, returning by the same track, I saw the bear
again. He had established himself on a windmill,
only a short distance from where I first discovered
him. He was comfortably perched on the ribs of
■the wheel and was revolving gaily, and seemingly
taking pleasure in the motion. On July 16, 1904,
I was on the same road once more. Imagine my
astonishment, on nearing the same old mill, to
-see, to all outward appearance, the same old
bear, in the same old place, still flying around
with the wheel He was not, however, enjoying
the sensation so much as he had done 20 years
previously, for, at every turn of the wheel, he
gave a plaintive cry which suggested that his
nervous system had been undermined through
scarcity of food and 20 years’ strenubusness in
■holding his position. That was six years ago.
Probably he is there yet.

“Awood”: In Sydney, I was told, I
would find a camp of real Australian black brother
at La Perouse. Visions of roasted ’possum and
’guana or a corroboree made me part with th e
tram fare to that place. Before going out I tried
to buy a book to tell me something about the
black man. I struck one entitled “ Boswell’s Life
of Johnson,” but it did not seem to meet the case at
all; Boswell was no sport. So I went out un-
armed. The first brother I met was middle-aged,
much bewhiskered, and carried a dilapidated
bunch of wild flowers. He looked tame, so I
made signs that I wished to hold converse with
him. He came at me with: “Good-day, matey,
Wanter buy a buneher flowers fer yer tart?” I
was counted out. When I came to, and wouldn’t
buy the flowers, he produced a boomerang that
looked as if it hah ■ -’ri made at a sawmill, and in-
vited me to have a throw. I had much pleasure
in accepting, and told him to look out it didn’t
hit him when it came back. I threw it about
20yds. and managed to hit the ground. The
boomerang broke in two. Black brother looked
concerned, and intimated that “two bob” would
square it. 1 gave him the “ two bob ” and caught
the next tram back to town. I related my ex-
perience to a friend, and, strange to say, he had
had a similar experience. His boomerang also
broke and cost him two bob. Now I wonder
what black brother sticks it together with.
“Leo Lear”: Who’s missing from the
Abo. list? One of the gang has turned up in
British East Africa, where he has been
shooting tall game. In a published
account of his adventures he casually
remarks that, after firing a shot into a
patch of scrub, 15,000 tail of animal
rose from the interior of the locality
and passed him at a gallop—gnu, topi,
kongoni, zebra, wart-hog, giraffe, jim-
jams and other beasts of prey “in an
unbroken column, solid as a charging
squadron.” The hunter also pirated a
well-known Australian character, and
let him loose in East Africa, under the
name of dingonec, thus giving color
to my suspicion as to his (the hunter’s)
land of origin. The dingonec is de-
scribed as “ a cross between a sea-ser-
pent, a leopard and a whale; 15ft.
long; head as big as that of a lioness,
with two long white fangs; back
scaled like an armadillo; marked like
a leopard ; a broad fin-tail; and feet as
wide as a hippo’s, but clawed like a
reptile’s.” The name of the writer is
given as E. B. Bronson, but the flavor of bush
hospitality about his statements makes me sus-
picious.

“ W.C.M.”: The imported rabbit and fox
curses are expensive enough in a direct sense,
while the bill which they run up indirectly is
almost equally serious. Consider, for instance,
the invoice for increased insect pests. The
western Riverina, Lower Darling, western Dar-
ling and all the districts out west (N.8.W.) are
invaded by caterpillars, which are turning a pros-
pective good season into a mighty poor one. So
far as I have seen, there are very few insect-eat-
ing birds, the natural enemies of these caterpillars,
to be found out here now. The poison laid for
the rabbit has helped to kill off the insectivorous
fowl. Then the fox has been a great enemy,
especially to the small ground-larks' and what is
known as the saltbush canary, eating the eggs
and the young birds. The wild ducks are rapidly
decreasing from similar causes. The only effective
method of dealing with the rabbit is closer settle-
ment, hurried on by a heavy land-tax. Plough-
ing and digging out the burrows is really the
only method to destroy bunny effectively. The
fox can be easily dealt with, as yet, by shooting
or trapping ; but he is on the increase, especially
as most of the Pastures Protection Boards have,
with great lack of wisdom, removed the bonus
placed on the scalp of the fox, on the plea that
he is effective in destroying the rabbit. This
bug-kill-bug policy merely leads to the survival of
the worst bug.
“ Werrimee ” : For indolent habits and
downright laziness the Outback hut-keeper ranks
second only to a Domain dosser. He has no
lights to trim, no sheep to mind, only a hut to
look after—and that is about the quietest and
least troublesome thing a man can be set to look
after. It is such a tame, silent, monotonous
charge that only a lazy man would take it in
hand. The salary is
not munificent, though
quite commensurate
with the labor in-
volved. Thehutstands
in a neighborhood
where it is occasion-
ally necessary; it may
be connected with an
expensive shearing
shed, or it may be a
dummy selection, or
one taken over .by a
squatter. Anyhow, if
travellers pass that
way, there is some
danger in leaving it
without a caretaker.
Of course, there may
be cattle or horses
or sheep in the pad-
dock adjoining, but
these require no at-
tention. The hut-
keeper may walk
round the fence once
a week if he feels in
want of exercise. The
only time he has to
put on a little steam
is when the boss
calls, which isn’t very
often, unless the hut is
on the town road. The
caller wants lunch,
and his horses have
to be taken out and
put in again when
they have had a spell.
This unwonted exer-
tion exhausts the
caretaker for an hour
or two, and he
stretches out on his
bunk and smokes,
feeling the satisfac-
tion of a man who
has done his duty.
He spends much
time on that bunk. On hot days and hot nights
he drags the mattress out on to the verandah, or
on the grass behind the shaded part of the build-
ing. When he thus sleeps outside he gets up
early, for the morning sun burns him out. He
has a collection of old books, and keeps up a read-
ing supply by swapping with travellers. He has
cards, too, which while away an hour or
two when a player drops in. Apart from the
books, his existence is about on a par with
that of the aboriginal, only the aboriginal has to
bestir himself a lot more in pursuit of a nimble
dinner.

The dangerous end of a mule is supposed
to be at the rear, but a stockman of the Mutooroo
Pastoral Co. (IS.A.) is in hospital at Adelaide
through taking that for granted.' The die
was roping in grabbed him just above the ankle,
and held on with a bulldog grip, until beaten off.
Curator A. S. Le Soeuf, of Sydney Zoo,
lately put the case for another Australian :
The opossum is a valuable fur-producing animal, which
in suitable country should be given the same care and
attention as is bestowed on sheep and cattle. The pre-
sent method of dealing with the animal is most haphazard
and wasteful, to say the least of it. Take a typical case
which actually happened in N.S.W. the other day. A
station-owner, with about 3000 opossums on hisproperty,
sold the right to catch them to trappers for £5OO, and
thought be had made a good bargain. The trappers
made a clean sweep, and the industry is done in that
district for a decade. But if the owner had given
a little attention to the animals, and left about
1500 females and 500 males for a breeding stock, he
would have had 1500 young annually, and when
these could take care of themselves he could
kill off the same number of mature specimens, which
would be worth at the present prices (say 7s. 6d. each)
£562 108. The expense of catching would take a station
hand three months, say £24 for wages. Traps, etc.,
would cost an equal amount; so, after deducting railway
freights and brokerage, there should be about £5OO per
annum for his trouble. Yet he was willing to sell the
producers of this income for the same amount in cash in
one year.
Opossums mature in 12 months, and will breed the
second year. They range in color from black to grey,
and from dark brown to rusty red and sometimes white.
They will breed true to color, and any desired race
can be maintained by selection. The animals feed on
almost anything, but eucalyptus is the main food. They
are easily trapped. Moreover, they sleep in holes in trees
during the day, and if small boxes are placed in the
boughs they will use these, and can be examined or taken
at any time required. Of course, on ordinary properties
there would be no occasion to fence them in. They do
not wander unless the food supply gives out.
Heavy forest-land useless for ordinary stock, and
country too poor or too damp for grass to grow, would,
if planted, give a good return from opossums ; and from
timber, too, if the more valuable trees are selected. The
number that could be carried per acre would depend
entirely on the food supply, and would \ary in every dis-
trict, but would probably be from 25 to 50.

“ Snell ” : The solemn formalities attend-
ing the interment of a distant relation were
rudely interrupted by shrill screams from another
part of the burying-ground. The screams were
maintained, but were diversified by yells, the
sound of blows and other news of battle. Pre-
sently a wild, dishevelled female came charging
over a series of eminently respectable graves,
with her mangled hat in her hand, and her
golden hairpads a-nangingdown her back, closely
pursued by an exasperated sister armed with a
man’s umbrella and a just quarrel.. The pursuer
twice overhauled her victim with the brolly
before the assailed dashed into the thick of our
funeral, clamoring up to O for police protection

ON ONE MAN PLAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
S’elp me,
It might have been wuss,
It might ha’ been better
(But I ain no fretter).
Here I am, old ,
Almost clean bowled
. (No good to fuss);
Blime, I’m cold,
Though the sun’s shinin’.
I’m a queer cuss.
No use repinin’—
Kinder missed the 'bus,
Spent all me gold
Soon as I got it.
Lent it or spent it
(S’pose I’ve made a muss)
One o’ the fool chaps,
P’raps.
Rich chaps—when ill or
Tired o’ the tramp
(I’m poor as Lazarus) —
Die on a piller
Soothed by a nuss;
No use to grumble,
No use to funk ;
My bed is humble,
Grass for me bunk.
Good enough for us
Tramps when we’re drunk,
Or when we’re trampin’
(No use to fuss),
Good enough, why, blime,
For the last campin’,
Grass an’ the blue sky ;
Who wants a nuss,
Makin’ him swaller stuff
When he wants ter lie still T
... Washin’ him, like enough.
I’m a lucky chap,
With me grass an’ sky ;
No roof or walls,
No folks crowdin’ by,
Whisperin’an’soft footfalls;
VV indin’ up affairs,
Farewells an’ fuss,
Sobbiu’s and prayers—
I’m a lucky chap.
Yet... I don’t know why—
’Cause I’m weak, mayhap—
Blowed if I don’t feel
I’ve somehow missed the ’bus,
Goin’ out alone
On me very own,
Givin’ th’ crows a meal,
Leavin’ but bleachin’ bone
Under the dazzlin’ sky.
I’m a poof old cuss
Lavin’ here to die,
Thirsty, hot an’ cold
... old
cuss—
Goin’ out alone
On me very own.

“ E.S.S.” on wet sheep :
At the beginning of the season there was a good deal
of talk about an invention that was going to settle all
arguments re wet sheep. Whether it settled them or
not, I can’t say ; there has been a deep silence since it
was taken forth to demonstrate its efficacy. I suspect
it has got mixed up with the prickly pear exterminators
and other doubtful company. There should be good
money in a reliable tester, though it might seem a matter
of no importance to non-sheep men. Every shed in the
country, one might say, has had its squabbles over the
wetness or non-wetness of sheep. No shearer cares to
handle wet sheep—except near the cut-out, when he
wants to finish and get to another shed. It is often an
excellent excuse for a “ camp ” in the hut, when the work
has been hard, or broken weather has caused an
outbreak of that epidemic known as tired feel-
ing. If there has been a light shower the previous
day or night, the men go to work in the morning with
speculations as to the condition of the wool. One or two
sheep are shorn, and the verdict is “ wet.” The manager
feels the sheep in the pens, and votes “ dry,” and there’s
a wrangle. The dampness or otherwise of wool is not
easy to judge on a sheep’s back or when it is freshly cut.
A moist atmosphere gives a clammy, wettish feeling to
yolky wool. In one shed the classer took a piece of wool
from an alleged wet fleece and a piece from the bins,
shorn the day before, and asked the rep. which was the
wet one. He tested both, and picked the one that
came from the bins. At another shed, when a
shower of rain had dampened things a little during
the night, the manager, having a shrewd idea as
to the temper of his team, hurried down early
in the morning and let out the sheep that had been
penned overnight, filling their places with damp sheep
from the yard. The men shore them till breakfast time
w'ithout protest. The ppns were cutout in the second
run ; then the dry sheep were put in. Each shore one,
and each man proclaimed his fleece wet. One
asserted that his particular hogget was sopping, and he’d
be pick-axed if he was going to continue on sheep like
that, to get rheumatism and other abominable com-
plaints for the sake of a few bob. The sheep were
turned out, and they remained out for two days. There
was no dampness about them when they came back.
*•***■
At a recent meeting of the Brisbane
Charity Organisation Society the question of
beggars cropped up, and Governor Macgregor,
who presided, told about one he knew in the
South Sea Islands. He first dropped across him
in a hill fort during a mountain war. The nigger
was badly wounded in the knee, and to save his
life Macgregor amputated the injured leg. The
patient recovered, and, being the only one-legged
person thereabouts, was regarded as something of
a curiosity. He took to cadging for a living, and
did pretty well at the business, but eventually got
tired of it and wrote this letter to Mac : “ Dear
Sir, —It is through you that I am now alive. I
was dying and you saved me by removing my leg.
You afterwards kept me alive. You are there-
fore my father, and it is your duty to provide for
me.” Naturally, being a Scotchman, Mac. saw
the joke. And naturally, for the same reason,
Wun Leg didn’t see the annuity.

“C.K.Y.”: Am on the Hawkesbury, 30
miles from Sydney, almost in the suburbs, yet
have wallabies, ’possums, platypus, dingoes, lyre-
birds, bandicoots, besides assorted snakes, all in
the landscape. Now the foxes are here in droves.
Our local Hunt Club, that chased the red herring
for years, went bung just as the genuine article
came along. The local fox is twice the size of the
British beast, and often more yellow than red ;
also he has a whole orchestra of diabolical cries
for night serenadings—cries that I never heard
made by his Damp Country relative. The usual
dog will not face the local fox, and the unusual dog
wishes he had left the business alone. We have
also a Pastures Protection Board that—that draws
its salary.

“ Machete ” : The Papuan of the Mamba
River, who is a true Papuan, believes firmly in
devils, and associates them with the strange
spiked implements he uses in his elaborate cere-
monial dances and plays. Also, the charms the
Papuan cuts from wood are invariably spiked.
In fact, the artless Papuan Pagan couples the
Devil with spiked tail, horns and toasting-fork,
much as his more civilized Christian brother
does. Why this association of spikes and Sheol ?
And has it any connection with the Prickly
Curse ?
