“Tindog”: The Nor’-Wesfc pearling
grounds are infested by a queer fish with a
sucker on-the back of its head. Sometimes a dozen
will adhere to a lugger, which isn’t good for the
lugger’s progress. The beast is a great scavenger,
and will let go and dart out every time you throw
anything overboard. The only time these fish
entirely leave the boat is when it comes to Broome
and gets high and dry on the beach. As the tide
is ebbing away, you will see them drop off and
wriggle through a few inches of water hack to the
sea. Their suction power is about equal to that
of a dredge or the blackmailing adventuress in the
melodrama. A land animal of similar capacity
could suck trees out of the ground.
“ Barcoo ” : Re the big Cambridge Downs
(Q.) shearing tallies (B. 8/9/’10). They wouldn’t
have looked so big to professional barbers if it
had been added that they were put up with
broad-gauge combs. Fifteen men with a portable
plant on the Flinders, which is in the same dis-
trict as Cambridge, averaged 197 per day for a
week, and used the ordinary combs. This was in
1903, and the same crowd did the same in 1904,
and, all things considered, I reckon that’s far the
better job of the two.

“ Casino ” : “ Vigilans’s ” par. (B. 1/9/10)
is interesting, but very wide of the mark in
several places. Pigmy geese are not
found solely in the Northern Territory.
I have shot plenty all through Queens-
land, and one pair as far south as the
Richmond River (N.S.W.). The state-
ment that the whistler is the only duck
that perches on trees is also wrong in
fact. Every bush boy knows that at
nesting time, or in times of flood, black
ducks are frequently seen ’lighting on
trees—the so-called wood duck is a goose,
and is not on in this act. To change
the subject violently: Has the fre-
quently-expressed opinion that ducks
carry their young out of trees in their
bills got any evidence behind it? On
several occasions I have seen ducks
taking their freshly-hatched broods
across country to swamps and lagoons,
but always on the “hoof.” Once a
black boy and myself were boiling our
quart-pot about midday at the foot of a
big gum, when we were suddenly assailed
by a shower of six or eight small duck-
lings, which came down one at a time
from a hollow limb quite 40ft. above,
and instantly dived out of sight in the
long grass. The nigger’s explanation
was that at dusk the mother would
collect her family and take them over-
land to the river, which was about a
mile distant. Brown brother pointed
out that the motion of the infants’
stumpy wings caused a sort of spiral
descent which broke the fall. I once
saw a musk duck swimming along with
a young one perched on her back, but I
think the musk family rarely exceeds one. At
any rate, I have never seen the old musk lady
with more.

“ Itan ” : Which Australian tree gives the
coolest shade? My bank note goes on the More-
ton Bay fig. In an inland N. S. Wales town
where I wielded the editorial crowbar for some
time, there was growing at the back of the pre-
mises a big fig, the branches of which spread
themselves across the roof. Extensions to the
building necessitated the uprooting of the oldest
arboreal inhabitant, and the result was an increase
in the summer temperature of 10 to 15 degrees.
ALONG THE CONDAMINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cattle low, the breezes blovv
And flirt the leaves among,
And stir the fronds which fringe the ponds
Where festooned vines are hung.
The grey gum-boles, like aureoles,
Glow in the autumn shine ;
On Fancy’s track I travel back
Along the Condamine.
When sunsets flame, a grey-haired dame,
Her dim eyes filled with pain,
In pictured poise, and deaf to noise,
Looks out across the plain,
Where shadows loom and gathering gloom
Obscures the herds of kine ;
And long her gaze peers through the haze
That shrouds the Condamine.
A garden gate at evening late
In fancy I can see.
Where scented musk pervades the dusk,
A maiden waits for me.
I left her there when skies were fair,
The world with hope a-shine.
She’s waiting still upon the hill
Above the Condamine.
A mother’s eyes, with glad surprise.
Will glow ere weeks are gone ;
A maiden true shall doff the rue
And orange blossoms don.
My mind goes hack on Fancy’s track,
And soon, O hearts o’ mine !
Again I’ll ride, whate’er betide,
Along the Condamine.
QUEENSLAND.
AUSTIN DODD.

PERSONAL

“ JUD"; OR THE ARTESIAN BORE.
When he leaves Victoria, Tait will not go
hack to railway work. He is going to “take it
easy.” He wasn’t exactly a poor man when he
came to Australia, and Hans Irvine showed him
the way into numerous profitable mining specs.
He will presumably retire to Montreal; his father
was Chief Justice there.
Robert Stout, M.L.’s Chief Justice,
becomes more and more wowserish in his old age
(he is now 66). He has for years been a sup-
porter of No License and No Tobacco, which
didn’t matter much so long as the support was
confined to mothers’ meetings or the Rational
Association, where Stout has occasionally preached
and detailed with much apparentdistress thedrawn
faces and stunted growth of the cigarette-smoking
youth or “the nicotine-stained fingers of bar-
risters ” who appeared before him. But unfortu-
nately he has started to pour out his fads into the
pot of justice. A few days ago, at Auckland, a
young man, found guilty of perjury, came before
Stout for sentence; and, after being told that
“ he had doubtless fallen entirely through drink,”
he was released on two years’ probation on the
following terms: (1) During the first 12 months
he is not to leave his house after 8 o’clock at
night without the probation officer’s consent; (2)
he must not during the two years visit an hotel;
(3) he must not drink intoxicating liquors ; (4) he
must not smoke; (5) he must consent to the issue
of a prohibition order. It’s a good job that Stout,
0. J., is not a vegetarian and a Mormon, or the
bewildered probationer might have been ordered
toliveon peanuts and marry 17 widows.
When Australia first began to dig out
some of its zinc the whole lot of it went to Ger-
many and Belgium. Then a big English crowd
that was interested in the stuff decided to start
the intricate and highly technical zinc-smelting
business in England, and German engineers and
chemists were got
across to build and
run the works.
Apparently, such
a mess was made
of things that, at
the end of three
years, the loss on
the business had
been £28,030. Just
before that third
year ended the co.
(which happens to
have ex-Governor
Kintore for chair
man) looked round
for an Australian
engineer-metallur-
gist, and shipped
off J. C. Moulden,
an Adelaide young
man, then working
for the Sulphide
Corporation. This
past year the co.
shows a trading
profit for the first
time; and the
directors—includ-
ing Kintore and
“Better Not”
Wa 11 ingt o n—
“place on record
their high appre-
ciation of the work
done by ” the Aus-
tralian young man,
“which has resulted in bringing the affairs of the
co. into a much more promising condition.” After
all, it seems, the Australian can carry out a much
more intricate job than the manufacture of a
clothes peg. Still, Heaven only knows why the
Australian metallurgist should have to go to
England to take the Australian dirt out of Aus-
tralian metal.
Alison G. O. Pain, the Perth (W.A.)
journalist who died the other day, was a relative
of Barry Pain, the novelist; and it was through
the latter that he was dragged into the turmoil of
the Inky Way.
“He was a strong and fearless man,” says
Premier Murray of Kailway Commissioner Tait,
who has thrown up his job.
“ He was a strong and fearless man ”
That epitaph, we all agree,
Fits him as well as any can ;
But kindly add : “ Unhappily,
His trains were not as strong as he.”

More played-out Australians!
Michael Hindmarsh, founder of a well-known
N.S.W. family, married Cely Sophia Rutter, a
native of the Ma State and a member of a very
early family, in 1824. Michael died at 67, and
Cely at 62. They had 14 children, of whom two
died in infancy, and need not be counted. One
died at 68, and the rest are still alive and busy.
The youngest is 58, and the grand total of ages
(including the son who gave up at 68) is 860 years.
I don’t know how many descendants there are ;
but the old lady I know best has 48 grandchildren
and nine great-grandchildren.
Cheerful story of the early days in Tas-
mania, recalled by Henry Button, of Launceston
Examinf.u, in his new book of reminiscences. A
Hobart lady had, as cook, a female convict who
was very clever, but resented all interference with
her duties. One time the mistress, trying to
exert authority, was met by a platform with just
two planks in it. (1) Cook wouldn’t have any-
body trapesing into her kitchen. (2) The trouble
she had been transported for was killing a man.
Audited and found correct; and missis thereafter
stayed in the parlor—with the door locked.
Two persons have been condemned to
death in Sydney within a week ; and the Sheriff’s
Department begins to take a new interest in life.
Mr. Mayburv, the Sheriff, likes a department
with some go in it. He gave up several jobs be-
cause they did not seem to lead to anything
definite. He was, nearly 40 years ago, a public-
school teacher. But there was no finality in
dealing with boys. They came back again and
again with the same offences on their head. So
he obtained an appointment in the Post Office.
But that was no better. After a time he applied
for a petty sessions clerkship, and was sent to
Bathurst. But there was no finality there either;
the same parties were always coming back. The
Sheriff’s Department is really the only one
that can call itself final. When certain pre-
liminaries are completed the Sheriff can step for-
ward and say, “Go,” and the party never comes
back. Mayburv, therefore, by aprocessof natural
selection became Under-Sheriff in 1882 and Sheriff
in 1896.
Oxford University nowadays offers a special
degree to the eye-mender. At the examination
held lately there were 14 candidates and nine
passes, and of the nine two were Australians—-
Newman, late of Casterton (Vic.), and Waddy,
of N.S.W.
AT THE SYDNEY ZOO.—WHEN THE
JAPANESE COME.
“ Never mind, Jacko! We'll soon be free. Togo
will be here in another week.”
The cable brings news of the death of
Andrew Rowan, one of Melbourne’s best-known
men of a generation back. He came originally
from Down, in Ireland, and set up in Melbourne
as a dealer in station supplies, with a branch in
Sydney. Then he himself “ squatted ” in Queens-
liind. He was one of the original directors of the
Melbourne Tram Co., and had a seat on the Board
of Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. and other large,
Fat enterprises.
Michael O’Loghlen, eldest son of Victoria’s
ancient baronet, has been appointed a Deputy
Lieutenant of the County Clare, Ireland, where
the family has a small property. The first
baronet, grandfather of the present, who is the
fourth, was the first Roman Catholic Judge
appointed in Ireland after the Reformation. He
was a friend of O’Connell, and has a marble statue
to his memory in the hall of the Law Courts in
Dublin.
Before Bishop Dawes, who died the other
day, took to shepherding he was a civil engineer,
and superintended the erection of a bridge over
the Thames at Fogtown. He was the first
Anglican Gaiters consecrated in Australia. Be-
fore that, there was a notion that the garments
couldn’t be put on properly except in Bull’s
country ; but, when Brisbane wanted a coadjutor-
bishop, the local authorities decided to chance it,
and had the job done in Sydney. They didn’t
even send to England for a man, for Dawes had
been shepherding in South Brisbane for years.
No evil results followed from either desperate
experiment.
A scheme is on foot to shift the monument
of Col. Collins, the founder of Hobart, from the
disused graveyard, where it now stands, to the
Parliamentary Reserve over the way. The pre-
sent monument was not erected until 1838, in the
time of Sir John Franklin. A small wooden
church, the first in the settlement, used to stand
on the spot, and Collins was buried beneath it.
When the church went, the vault underneath was
forgotten for years, until some small boys, playing
in thechurchyard, foundit. Then Franklin whipped
up enough money to get the place marked with a
graven image. Collins’s funeral, by the way, was
a gorgeous business. Everyone had new clothes
for the occasion, and everything was distributed
from the general store. The lengthy list of
articles applied for suggests that the colonists must
have previously borrowed summer fashions from
the aborigines. There was trouble when the
time came for the British Government to foot the
bill. But it swallowed its scruples and did it.
“Deeby”: Ex-Premier Joe Carruthers,
who gives up these latter days to money-making,
and doesn’t worry much about either law or
politics, is the shrewdest land-speculator I know.
No matter where he buys land, there, sooner or
later (but mostly sooner), a railway line is built.
It is now even proposed to construct one through
the mostly-emprv big estates from Cooma to the
neighborhood of Oarruthers’s property on the road
to Kosciusko. I don’t know whether he will be
able to guess the McGowen Government’s inten-
tions as well; but if, by some horrible accident,
the Wade Government should continue in office,
my tip is to buy land as near as possible to wher-
ever Carruthers may elect to make his next
purchase.
Died, in London, “ Chief” Jim Shaw, one-
time Mayor of Coolgardie. and later Mayor of
Adelaide. One of the ideal men of an early rush,
he was generous as sunlight, kindly as spring,
his one offence being that bad language which is
only skin deep. As Mayor of Adelaide he gave a
ball at the Town Hall, and everybody said it was
a great success. After the dancers had gone, Jim
Shaw went to the Napoleon for a drink ; and said
a friend to him : “ How did the ball go, ‘Chief’? ’
“ I’ll tell you,” says Jim. “About 2 o’clock this
mornin’. Lady came to me an’ slaps me on
the back, an’, says she : ‘Jim, s’help me , this
is the best hop I was ever at in me life.’
So there you are.” Then those who knew “the
Chief ” understood that the lady had said : “ Mr.
Shaw, this is a charming dance.”
Ex-Speaker Alfred Cowley thus addressed
the Brisbane Rechabites at their annual meeting
last week :
Our late beloved monarch, King Edward, owned the
whole of one village, and in that area the sale of intoxi-
cating liquors was absolutely forbidden. (Loud applause.)
If Alfred and his Rechabites will turn to the
Melbourne Age (Sept. 10) they will find another
little bit of royal information :
The King of England is master of many industries. It
is not generally known that he is owner of a distillery in
which is manufactured the very finest irhisky. This dis-
tillery is situated on the Balmoral estate, and is known as
Lochnagar, and its products are reserved exclusively for
the use of members of the Royal family and their respec-
tive households.
Now Bung can have his turn. It’s a handy sort
of monarch that can be a shining example to the
people on both sides of the beer-pump.
STATE RIGHTS.
American newspapers denounce ex-President Roose-
velt for the part he is taking in State politics. It is sug-
gested that he should confine himself to national ques-
tions. — Cable.
UNCLE SAM: “ Dang it, Teddy, look at Aus-
tralia's awful plight and give up the habit.”
Although Premier John Verran, of South
Aus., prides himself on his bluntness of speech,
the papers won’t have it untrimmed. “Peake,”
he shouted angrily, banging a clenched fist on the
table, thereby causing the Chairman and the
water-bottle to jump simultaneously, “he’s a rat.
You take it from me; a sewer rat, that’s what he
is, when you come to his politics.” And this is
how a daily put it: “Mr. Verran referred to Mr.
Peake as a chameleon, his political skin always
harmonising completely with his environment.”
THE CARPENTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Carpenters of Providence are hustling to and fro ;
I hear the sound of hammering—it’s time for Wade to go !
The coffin-shop is echoing—whilst busy hammers beat
The corpse is slowly stiffening beneath the winding sheet.
The Carpenters have measured it, this cold cadaver who
Will shortly rest beneath the ground with all the buried
crew.
The hearse is new-caparisoned, the plumes are nodding
slow ;
The Carpenters have passed the word and the remains
must go.
The hammers beat in harmonj'—it somehow seems to
me
That Mister Wade’s not popular, as the remains should
be!
The State is all a coffin-shop that seems to shout
“ Hooray !
It’s Mister Wade we’re burying—we’re planting him to-
day I
A dismal nark was Gregory, an angry cove and sour !
But now he’s drifting tomb-ward ; we will plant him in
an hour.
We’ve waited for him very long—we thought he’d never
die ;
But now he’s cold as Potiphar, and so our hammers fly ! ”
The Carpenters of Providence, ivho planted Alf and Joe,
They're busy noiv with Gregory—it’s time for him to go !
The Public Undertakery is boxing Mister Wade —
Hark hoiv each dead Conservative goes whizzing down the
grade!
Each Tory croivd that perishes in caskets large and neat
Is placed by merry Carpenters, and cheerful hammers
beat!
It is the pleased elector-folk, who hustle to-and-fro ;
They’re planting Wade the bickersome—dashed glad to
see him go !
victoria.

This bank has its own way of doing business. It has only
three directors, of whom one is managing-director ; and
it has no auditor. In the latter respect, it now stands
alone, so far as The Bulletin can remember,
among the banks of the Commonwealth. The West-
ralian Bank used to belong to the same strange
persuasion ; but it joined the Conformists a few
years ago, leaving the Commercial of Tasmania to
argue out the point alone with its shareholders. No
doubt a 12 per cent, dividend is a sweet, seductive song
for any management to sing ; but it wouldn’t necessarily
follow that the tune would be changed even if there
were an auditor. And, anyhow, there are others beside
the shareholders to consider. For every £1 that the
latter have in the bank the public (depositors, etc.) have
£4 65.; and the people with the £4 6s. more or less at
stake deserve whatever protection an independent audit
can give them.
The bank, in spite of its strange old ways (it is
the second-oldest bank in Australasia), has some staunch
friends, and a lot of Tasmanian business passes to it auto-
matically—partly, no doubt, because “ the Barclays,” the
chief rulers of the concern, are reckoned to have a
strong pull in the small island. So, though it hasn’t the
use of any tremendous amount of outside money in pro-
portion to its own, its profits show up very well.
Reckoned on the basis of capital and reserves, recent
figures work out at this :
Feb., 1907 .. £9 14 10 p.c. Feb., 1909 ..£8 19 3 p.c.
Aug., 1907 .. 11 7 3 „ Aug., 1909 .. 9 3 9 „
Feb., 1908 .. 10 8 3 Feb., 1910 .. 818 8 ~
Aug., 1908.. 9 2 1 „ Aug., 1910.. 819 10 ~
No doubt, the return is helped by the hidden reserve,
represented partly by written-down values of property.
Though the bank has put a good many thousands lately
into premises—including big offices at Launceston —its
premises account stands at only £51,124. Its cash posi-
tion is never very strong, however. Lately it has
been strengthened—possibly in view of the fact
that the bank will have to pay out gold for its own notes
and buy Commonwealth notes with more of the same
handy sort of mineral; yet even now, in spite of those
pending transactions, it has only 32 per cent, of the
public’s money in liquid assets. No douht much of t his
gold will come back to the bank, for neither the people
who get gold for the old notes nor the Commonwealth
Government which gets gold for the new notes will eat the
sovereigns. Still, there will be a temporary disarrange-
ment ; and so far as liquid assets are concerned the Com-
mercial of Tasmania is in no stronger position than it
ought to be.
In the past 12 months the chief items have
moved like this :
Aug., ’O9. Aug., ’lO.
Liabilities: —
Notes and Bills £60,715 £65,627 Inc. £4,912
Deposits .. 1,507,534 1,529,138 Inc. 21,604
Liquid Assets.. 479,601 514,054 Inc. 34,453
Advances .. 1,424,330 1,414,756 Dec. 9,574
Premises .. 43,746 51,124 Inc. 7,378
That shows a slight increase in deposits and a slight de-
crease in advances, with the consequent increase in liquid
assets mentioned above. But how slowly the figures do
mount 1 Ten years ago the deposits were £1,456,702,
or only £72,436 less than they are now. Con-
sidering the remarkably fine seasons of the
past few years, and the remarkably fine prices
for most of the things Tasmania has to sell, the
snail-like pace of the growth of deposits is a shocking re-
flection either on the Government of Tasmania or on this
bank. And this paper doesn’t think the reflection is on
the bank, except insofar as it is responsible for the
Government of the State.

If this co.’s annual report had come out about a fortnight
earlier, some people might have stopped to think it over
again before they rushed along with their cheques for
another new Australian insurance co. which wais lately
put on the market. And, though they might still have
dropped in with their cheques at the brokers’, others
might have thought still harder before they rushed the
shares up to a fairly stiff premium. It is all very well to
consider the dividends paid by existing Australian fire or
fire-and-marine insurance cos. In most cases they no
doubt are very enticing. But while this kind of co.
usually does splendidly after it has got to manhood,
there is a big proportion that can’t get through the
troubles of infancy. In 1885 there were no less than 30
Australasian cos. doing this kind of business ; and of the
30, no less than 19 have disappeared—the Ade-
laide, Australian Alliance, Australian General, Aus-
tralian Mercantile Union, Colonial (M.L.), Equitable
Fire, Equitable of M.L., Indemnity, Industrial
Mutual Fire, Mercantile of S. Aus., Metropolitan
Mutual, National of Australasia, Pacific, South
Australian, Southern, Sydney Lloyd’s, Sydney Marine,
United Australian, Union of M.L. .In most cases
they have, of course, been absorbed; that is to
say, shareholders have got something for their busi-
ness, much or little—mostly little. And at odd times
since 1885 other offices have been started and have
dropped out. Investors can’t therefore regard a fire-and-
marine proposition as a dead-sure thing. Much money is
made in it—and much is lost. And a good deal more is
lost than should be, because the public will not realise
that the results of the first year are no true standard for
judging future losses, wherefore shares are frequently
rushed to extravagant prices. In this Commonwealth
case it will be observed that the so-called profits for the
first year were nearly twice as great as they have been in
any subsequent year. The fact, alluded to at the time,
was that while a’full year’s premium had been received in
most cases, only a few months’ risks had been carried.
The co. s present misfortune, resulting in a loss
of £B3B on the actual business of underwriting for the
year, appears to be mainly due to marine insurance.
The rates on this class of risk —and especially on ocean
cargoes—have for some time past been so ridiculously
low that the more prudent offices have been cutting down
their “lines ” ; and rather late in the day this co. follows
suit. The next list shows how the year compares with
those that have gone before : x
Net Ex- Loss Expense

to cover the risks for the remainder of the terms for
which premiums have been accepted. If so, there are no
real reserves at all. This is r po way to attract insurers.

Somewhere about the waist-line of the foregoing table a
remark appears about written-down capital ; and from
that the wise will know that the profits shown on sales
during the past three years are really only surpluses
realised in excess of written-down values. That is to
say, the losses have not been quite as great as it had
been figured that they would be. Still the be§t usually
go first, and the surpluses have been so small that it looks
very much as though the writing down in 1903 was not
severe enough. The published accounts don’t show all
the details; but these have been got together chiefly

The gradual shrinkage in the profit on sales is so bitterly
suggestive of a loss on some future sales that £19,156 is
now put by out of the reserves toform a contingent fund
to meet the likely deficits. The society might go on
nursing the properties, and holding the desirable subur-
ban block in idleness until the hungry hunter could be
persuaded to pay the price demanded; but, as the
directors put it, there is an “urgency, in view of the
approaching land-tax, of realising the remaining pro-
perties as promptly as possible.” Fortunately the society
hasn’t got a great deal of presumably-unproductive
vacant land left; the total property item, £116,976, is
made up this way:—House property, £112,458; vacant
land, £4518. In 1905 the figures were—Houses, £282,295 ;
land, £52,206. Altogether, then, it has been a very vigor-
ous five years of clearing out.
In 1893, when the boom trouble arrived, the
co.’s deposit and debenture liability amounted to
£435,536. The first arrangement made with the creditors
was the usual piece of extravagant folly : depositors were
to get 6 per cent, and debenture-holders 5 per cent.
Subsequently the latter had to put up with as little as 2J
per cent. A good deal of swapping was also done—pro-
perty for deposits or debentures. Thus and in other ways
the old debt had been reduced to £175,577 by 1904. Since
then further satisfactory progress has been made :

By next year the new advances should considerably

business on building-society terms; but no doubt most
of it has been due to the sale of properties ; and when it
has no more properties to sell, that class of business may
be a good deal harder to get. For the old-fashioned
building society without frills has lost, much of its old
popularity since the Starr-Bowkett idea came along and
caught the eye with its embroideries.

That is the best that can be done with it in a single
table. The position is like this. In 1902, when the
last arrangement was made with the creditors, the
society undertook to pay 3 per cent, as a regular
thing, and an extra 2 per cent, when the profits ran
that far. The table shows the payments actually made
during each term. For the past year a full 5 per cent,
has been paid for 12 months; and apparently the full
payment for the second half of the year also been
made. On this latter point, however, things are not put
as plainly as usual. The society has been having a re-
valuation, and the first result is also in the table.
The next step will be to clear up the mess by writing
down capital. Nominally the latter is at present
£193,210, in shares fully paid to £5. This it is
proposed to write down to £96,605, in £2 10s. shares.
As the present deficit is £76,605, this writing down of
capital will give the society a reserve or contingent fund
of £20,000, ostensibly in case the assets don’t realise even
the reduced valuation. The £43,952 shown as written off
is made up as follows : . (
Old New Written
Valuation. Valuation. Off.
Society’s premises £78,000 £50,000 £28,000

Land (vacant) .. 18,635 9,235 9,400
Leaseholds .. 2,253 500 1,753
Total .. .. £113,637 £69,685 £43,952
The properties in hand, apart from the office premises,
are thus written down to £19,685, which is less than the
amount of the contingent fund, so the marking down of
capital is sufficient to write these assets off entirely.
There isn’t, therefore, anything half-hearted about the
proposed capital reduction. If the other assets, includ-
ing office premises, are worth their book value, there will
be £116,605 of assets in excess of liabilities, even though
the capital is only £96,605 ; that is to say, within a few
pence of £3 per share. The other assets are mainly
balances due on time-payment sales and on mortgages,
£105,903 in all.
Then, as to the future. Since the society has'
been- paying 5 per cent, to the old creditors it hasn’t
been able to show a profit. But, owing to the very rapid
realisation of securities, so much of the old debt has been
paid off that the co. has lately been able to arrange to
pav off the balance, borrowing the money at something
less than 5 per cent, to do it—possibly at 3 or 3£ per cent.
This is how the matter stands: —
1896. 1909. 1910.
Australian deposits, etc. .. £169,416 £32,180)
British ditto .. .. 307,689 78,136/
Mortgages, etc. .. .. 30,000 Nil 56,270
Total debt .. ~ £507,105 £110,316 £58,131
If the society can get a clear 4 per cent, on its assets, and
has to pay 3J per cent, on its debts, it will show 7 £ 5050 a
year profit, equal to a shade over 5 per cent, on its capi-
tal. No doubt 4 per cent, is a very small net return ;
but on the recent records it seems doubtful whether the
society can go much beyond it. Last year the revenue
was £9354 ; deduct £2500 -for exes, and upkeep, and
£6854 is left, which is barely 4 per cent, on even the
reduced book value of the assets. Of course, as pro-
perties are realised more profitable investments may be
got; but the prospects of very profitable new building-
society business are not brilliant. And, anyway, most of
the co.’s capital is in office premises, which may be a gilt-
edged security at the written-down value, but certainly
don’t give a glittering return—especially when the
management exes, of a co. have to be paid out of the
revenue.

Thus, when the society started it had £1 18s. out at risk
for every £1 of its own capital; it has now £2 10s. for
every £l. It ought to be piling up reserves to meet the
extra losses which are bound to follow the extra risks ;
but the piling-up is not at all rapid. The society might
well have been content to push up its dividend more
slowly. Anyhow, as the div. has got to 7 percent., it ought
to stop there for a few years to give the reserves time to
grow. If they are not wanted to wipe out losses, they won’t
eat themselves. Though the deposits and other liabilities
have moved along brjskly, they are still well protected ;
and depositors, etc., have the further satisfaction of
knowing that under the rules it is forbidden to go into
debt to the extent of more than twice the amount of the
paid capital.

This little co. tries to fit them with insurance of all
shapes and sizes. It covers the risks of death, accident
and disease ; it will bury you if you die, and provide
medicine and a little to go on with if you are sick. It is
dear insurance, of course ; as shown above, the expenses
last year ran to a trifle more than the claims, reckoning
the 5 per cent, dividend in with the exes. In proportion to
total income, the outgoings other than claims have been
as follows for the past eight years :
190* .. £52 17 5 per cent. 1907 .. £46 9 9 per cent.
1904 .. 4916 6 ~ „ 1908 .. 45 9 8 „
1905 .. 50 14 9 ~ ~ 1909 .a. 44 18 4 ~ ~

A steady reduction in the rate is thus shown since 1905.

much higher than the figures shown above, because the
Ordinary business would naturally bring the average
down a trifle. Even Ordinary business, though, may
mean a £25 policy in the case of this office. The
one thing that can be said in favor of business
on such terms is that it is better than nothing,
and if this kind of co. didn’t get it, it probably wouldn’t
be got at all. So far as most of the benefits are con-
cerned, the insurer would no doubt do better with an
ordinary friendly society “lodge.” But the “lodge”
doesn’t go into the little back streets and hunt up mem-
bers. This co. does ; and so it has its uses. Its financial
position is quite sound, as far as one can judge : it has
£10,104 in Government stock, and practically all the rest
of its funds and its capital (£4794) in mortgages and loans
on policies, chiefly the former.

Feb., 1910.. 4483 1179 5 „ „ 3253 294 2932
Aug., 1910.. 5796 1250 5 „ ~ 3500 464 3514
The figures in the last line are approximate. It has been
assumed that £2OO will be appropriated for directors
fees, tax, etc. ; and the total amount of the dividend and
the bonus has been guessed at. It isn’t a satis-
factory way to present things; but it can’t be
helped, because of the peculiar manner in which
the accounts are submitted. The fact is, the
society was started on a brotherly-love basis, over 40
years ago, and it gets old methods with difficulty.
Until a year or so back even the auditors’ fees weren’t
charged up with the ordinary expenses ; they were voted
subsequently as an honorarium. And the directors’ fees
are still treated in that way. The capital also isn’t fixed ;
the shareholders can pull out when they like. The other
things mentioned don’t matter much; but this
last does matter. You can’t build up a business
very far when your shareholders are liable to drop
in any moment and want all their money back. One by-
product of this was that for very many years the man-
agement set its face against reserves ; as shareholders
could draw out at any moment, it was held that it wasn’t
right to put by money which they would forfeit if they
did go out. The society has got over that amiable eccen-
tricity more or less, and it needs to get over some others.
At present, having to be prepared for demands to
the full extent of its capital, it has to keep in hand
large amounts in liquid assets; and it is quite
conceivable that, even then, it could not do what it has
undertaken to do. The basis wants bringing up to date.
If the capital were fixed —say, at £50,000 —the co.’s
position would be much safer, a better return might be
got for the money, and, given good management, the
shares would be just as easily converted into cash. And
it might also be possible to give the customers something
more than the very modest 5 per cent. The Sydney
society pays its shareholders 10 per cent, and gives a
bonus or rebate of Hi per cent.

The cheeky little marine gamecocks of S. Aus. are prac-
tically all in the pen of this old-established co., which
also does a good deal in the way of lightering—a busi-
ness of which there isn’t as much now as there was be-

hole at the end of a creek. How much revenue the co.
writes off against depreciation isn’t clear; but it is evi-
dently considerable. Four years ago “12 steam tugs and
launches ” stood in the books at £30,060 ; now “ 13 steam
tugs and launches" stand in the same place at£26,927.
And, in the same period, “19 hulks and barges as
lighters” have been written down from £21,934 to
£18,228. Or the results can be looked at this way ;
June, 1906. June, 1910.
Liquid assets .. ..£10,973 £26,651
Liabilities .. .. 2,431 1,558
Surplus .. £8,542 £25,093
So, though a dividend running up to 20 per cent, has
been paid, and though a trifle has been put into tug-plant
and another trifle into slip property, £16,551 has been
dropped into the stocking in four years. That isn’t
necessarily net surplus profit; some of it may represent
wear and tear of plant, and be money put by. to replace
partially worn-out arks. But, making even a very liberal
allowance on that score, the performance is wonderfully
good, and shows what a sweet time S. Aus. has been hav-
ing. There is a doubt, certainly, whether, under normal
conditions, the co. will do as well as it used to do, owing
to the construction of the outer harbor aforementioned ;
and in that connection the shrinkage of the profits dur-
ing the past three terms is worth noting.
*•*■**
The men in the Australian Bank of Commerce
probably feel like seamen who have been saved from a
wreck after running short of provisions. It looked, at
one time, as though the old A.J.S. would go down with
all hands; but general-manager A. P. Stew-art beached
the ship, and knocked up another vessel which is as good
as the A.J.S. ever was. Naturally the crew feel grateful
to him ; and one day last week they met and presented
him with various valuable goods. There was a’ tinle
when A. P. Stewart was, reckoned a hard man, because
he didn’t hand out many favors ; but the staff believes
now that he didn’t do it, not because he hadn’t a mind
to, but because there weren’t any favors 6n the premises
to hand out.
* * * *
The Metropolitan Accident, Fire and General
Insurance Co. (Sydney) is going to join in the scramble
for more capital. The present co. is a float-off from the
Australian Metropolitan Life Assurance Co., which took
£15,000 in fully-paid £1 shares for the goodwill; and
whether the connection was woith the money can be
gathered from the fact that the net premium income
last year was £7038. More capital is certainly desirable ;
but, before it is issued, some of the present capital,
represented by a purely paper asset, ought to be written
off. With that done, the prospects are very good.

General-Manager Delprat,'of the Broken Hill
Prop., has given a fillip to the agitation for an export
dutv on crude ore and concentrates, of which hundreds
of thousands of tbhs are being shipped to Getmah and
Belgian smelters. At once the Freetrade papers cry,
“You’ll ruin the poor miner! You’ll shut out the
foreign buyer, and the poor miner won’t be able to get
as nmch for his ore.” The same Freetraders had the
same solicitude for the poor miner when an hupo™
duty on coke was proposed 20 years ago. “ You’ll shut
out the foreigner, and the poor miner will have to pay
more for his smelting.” Still, the duty went on, an Aus-
tralian coke industry was established, and to-day the
poor miner can get coke at about one-fourth the price he
had to pay 22 years ago.
* * * *
The present agreement between men and cos.
at Broken Hill runs out at the end of the year. But, on
appearances so far, there will be no trouble. The cos.
won’t ask for a wage-reduction, and the men, not being
'this time misled by a Tom Mann, know that, if they asked
for more pay, several of the cos. would promptly follow
the B. H. Prop., and shut up shop till the metal markets
improve.
*•* * *
At the Broken Hill South Extended an east
crosscut put out at the 930 ft. level to intersect the
southern continuation of the South Blocks zinc lode
(the existence of which had previously been proved by
drill), has opened up a fair-sized body of solid ore ; and
now all the mine managers in Broken Hill are being
invited to go down and look at it. For many months
past reports have been published in which reference was
made in a large way to rich sulphides, of irreproach-
able assay value and limitless extent. During that
time the underground workings were guarded like a
two-up school. Shares bobbed up and down serenely
enough till the last card had to be played, and then
it was found that, instead of the rich silver-lead lode the
public had been led into believing was there, all the fuss
had been about nothing more extraordinary than the
southern continuation of the hard, forbidding zinc lode
which the South Blocks developed in their Block 5 shaft.
The level on which this zinc shoot was found in Block 5
shaft is 900 ft., but that is actually only about 10ft. above
the level on which the ore was cut in the South Extended
crosscut. Moreover, it was to intersect this lode that the
diamond-drill was used. As Block 5 shaft is only about
a couple of hundred yards away from the South Extended
shaft, it was a pretty sure thing that ore of some kind
would be met at a certain point east from the South Ex-
tended shaft. Had the public remembered this in the
first place, it would not have been so ready to bite at the
shares.
The South Extended has only another three-
pence to call up, representing approximately £BOOO. Its
main shaft is a smail one, totally inadequate for hauling
zinc ore from a thousand feet and more (because the east
crosscut has only touched the top of the ore shoot). And
before any ore can be treated a mill will have to be
erected. Another reconstruction may be avoided, but on
what terms? It must be remembered that mining zinc
ore (with hardly any lead or silver in it) from a great
depth will prove a much more expensive operation than
treating tailings by a flotation process.
■k- vr**
The B. H. Junction directors are between the
Devil and the deep sea. If they don’t resume mining
and milling operations they will have to face reconstruc-
tion, and if they do restart production they won’t have
anything left to reconstruct on. A spasmodic lift was given
to the shares the other day by the discovery in an old
crushed-in stope of a hatful of very rich carbonate ore.
The man who secured the assay sample took his life in
his hand when he did it, and naturally he didn’t stay
there long enough to see how much of the rich stuff was
left! There are thousands of tons of rich Sulphide ore in
these old broken levels, but how to get the stuff out at a
profit is a problem that has caused the manager sleepless
nights and much vexation of spirit. There are some old
stopes on these levels through which it wouldn’t be safe
for a bird to fly. #
Marsh’s shaft, through which the major portion
of the ore that is being milled by the British Broken Hill is
being hauled, would be a great comfort to the Junction
North just now did that co. only possess it. As things are,
it was very unfortunate for the Junction North that
when diamond-drilling for the lost lode was being carried
on from the 1134 ft. level, the only indication of ore was
got on the -foot-wall side, or on the British boundary,
near Marsh’s shaft. It maybe that the Junction North
lode has turned over and gone away on an easterly dip.
If so, the deepening of Marsh’s shaft is a project which it
might pay the British directors to carry out^
Main Ridge Deep Lead, Rocldey (N.S.W.),
holds its head high above the ordinary duffer because its
average of sdvvt. 22gr. from 169 tons is at least something
to go on with. But the co. is in 22,000 shares ;so it will
have to speed up a bit and treat wash at a faster pace
than one ton per hour if dividends are to be paid. Mount
David, in the same district, is doing a deal of prospecting
just now. An alluvial shaft is down 120 ft. ;itis a wet
hole, and sinking is slow work. Sometimes alluvial leads
tail off into very deep country which merges into carbon-
iferous sandstone. Lignite was cut in both shafts at
Mount David, so the next thing to turn up may be
a coal seam. As an instance of this kind of thing, the
Wild Oat lead at Clermont (Queensland) may be men-
tioned.
* * • * *
Occidental, Cobar (N.S.W.), issued its half-
yearly report, the other day, and, as usual, didn’t impart
much information. The official report says there is a
“ considerable” quantity of ore above No. 3 level and a
“huge” block of virgin ground between Nos. 3 and 4
levels, which lemarks are too indefinite to be of much
value. There are several things to consider : (1) Can the
co. treat the sulphides at a profit? (2) Does£7ooo reserve
fund allow of much experimenting (Oobar Gold Mines
had £70,000 and spent most of it, and couldn’t solve the
problem)? (3) The ore in the bottom levels is admittedly
very hard and the stamps light—can the output be main-
tained? Another item given prominence in the report is
that the extraction averaged as high as 76 per cent, for
the last two months. It is nothing prodigious; and it
would have been as well to mention where the ore came
from. If from the upper levels, it explains the compara-
tively good extraction at once. But it would give no
idea of what can be done in future.

Cobar Copper (old Aoung Australian and
Mount Pleasant) has nailed Sheets of iron ov'er the door
and window openings; so the co. is evidently after a sus-
pension. The elosing-dowh is ill-timed, as the men
were getting well in with the drive towards the Young
Australian shaft, their objective. This has been another
instance of weird management. The machinery was
pulled down and shifted from the Young Australian shaft
and put on the Mount Pleasant about 900 ft. away, and
then they drove back to the Young Australian shaft.
Just when it was possible to strike something the men
were knocked off. Any other crowd would have pawned
the door-mat to have keptgoing a little longer.

•rt * * *
In the midst of the prevailing gloom at Norse-
man over the collapse of the Waihi, the Princess Royal
and other mines, there is a glimmer of hope thrown out
in lastest reports from the Mararoa. Below the No. 7 level,
the danger-zone of Norseman reefs, a fair body of ore,
worth 335. a ton, has been reported. This is south of the
original winze, so Norseman is feeling good.
* * * *•
Shareholders of the Golden Mile will soon say
“ Rats !” when “ reserves ” are mentioned. The Horse-
shoe, after many assertions of lOdwt. ore, cannot get
much over 6dwt., and every month or so a breakdown in
plant makes an excuse for low tonnages. However, when
the shaft gets down where it ought to have been two
years ago—to the 2000 ft.—there may be plenty to feed the
new works. The Associated, not to be outdone by Horse-
shoe, boldly withdraws half a million tons of ore from
sight. A flood —in one of the driest seasons known on the
fields—and a fall of earth in one of the stopesare used to
cover the circumstance. Of course the only remedy for
loss of ore is to put up more plant, and this is now being
done.
* * * *
Charles Grant is in Sydney, giving the Flag
(W. A.) proposition a shove along. Grant probably knows
the Phillips River field better than any other man, and
has looked at the figures from both ends and all sides,
and he can’t see anything less than a 35 per cent, return
on capital if he can get the money to add a concentrating
plant to the present equipment.
* * * *
Bendigonians gasped on finding Clarence
United being bailed up by an outsider for not working
its 18 acres on the Paddy’s Gully line. They didn’t weep
over the Lansell loss of the Sheepshead lease, but to try to
.dock the Clarence in this way was too bad! Bendigonians
have been putting £lO,OOO or so a year into the Clarence
workings on the Moon line, and quite forgot that the
Paddy's Gully lease was not consolidated. The Warden
was sympathetic, so the co. is to go to work at once or—
well, that will rest with Peter Mcßride, who is to be
appealed to by the dissatisfied applicant for forfeiture.
The applicant’s idea is that he ought to get the ground ;
it will do him no good merely to awaken the Clarence to
a sense of duty. Much is to be said for him, as fully a
two-mile stretch of the Paddy’s Gully line has been per-
mitted to lie idle for years. “Shepherds, beware; the
wolves are about! ”is the notice placarded on one Ben-
digro office.
* * * *
The very latest Victorian alluvial co., the South
Berry, Creswick, brings out its report just when Joe
English, chairman of the Madame Berry, passes out.
W. B. Squire, an old hand on the field, fashioned the
South Berry into'financial shape, and it now flourishes as
a 30,000-share co. with a good market position.
Scrip is not often seen, for the little syndi-
‘ cate connected with Alec Peacock’s office sticks
well together, believing that Squire knows what he is
about when on alluvial. So far the little syndicate has
every reason to hold fast. The co. got on to good wash
at once; it also picked up cheap plant, and did
not have to rattle it to pieces before the first wash-
ing-up indicated that there was loz. ground
about. That known, the rest was easy. Shares in
reserve were used to buy a permanent plant. The ground
was so dry and easy that the bit won from gold and a
few light calls enabled Squire to write: “It only re-
quires an adequate plant to ensure a fair dividend-paying
mine for years from the alluvial workings.” That this
treasure should have been left neglected on the Berry lead
seems too good to be true. Still, no one ever thought
the Cathcart had a £5 rise in it, or the Glenfine South a
£IQ buck ; so why, not a possible gamble in South Berry ?
. Creswick badly wants a flutter.

Mills’ United, Charters Towers, reports a
crushing of 988 tons for £2626. It is intended to sink a

been acquired on behalf of Mills. The latter is also hold-

The Etheridge (Q.) Co., which J. S. Reid is
pushing off in London on behalf of the Chillagoe people,
will be in 135,000 shares, according to last advices, and

claims, etc. As in the case of the Mungana, the Chillagoe
Co. will also hang on to enough shares to make sure that
the ore will go to the right place for treatment.

McGregor, Cloncurry (Q.), is now whispering
into the ear of Kidston that he really ought to put up
half the money to build the 26 miles of railway necessary
to connect the mine with the Hampden-Cloncurry
branch. The total cost would be about £60,000. If the
co. can’t whisper loud enough, a road will be made and
either traction engines or a Renard road train bought.
* * * *
Manager Pemble, who has taken charge of
Kulamadau, on Woodlark Island (Papua), thinks a por-
tion of the workings will require careful handling to
avoid a smash, and he finds the stopes rather heavily
depleted. The profit for nine weeks’ work was £2312, so
the finances should he on the strong side; but on the out-
look the directors will he wise to stick to their cash, and
pass by the very tempting chance to pay a dividend.
Of the 300,000 shares in Mount Lyell Blocks,
124,549 were knocked down by an auctioneer in Mel-
bourne last week, after having been forfeited for a 6d.
call. Price, a shade above the call. Tom Harvey lifted
50,000 and Fred Keats took 30,000. So the gentle public
did not see any bargains at the sale as of yore. Tom
Harvey made a good rise out of Blocks on the
“buck” of 1906, when he brought manager Fergusson
from Berringa to treat the copper clays. A gold man,
Fergusson answered expectations, helped by the price to
which metals went. Twodivs. came in 1906—none since.
A crowd in London somehow got the idea that the Blocks
was a big mine, thinking, perhaps, that the North
Lyell shoot of ore was fluking in to the claim: so
they gathered in all the 300,000 shares, except an
odd 10,000 or 15,000. The crash in copper brought
down the house of cards; and Saturday’s sale
represents a bit of J. Bull’s loss on the deal. The Blocks
always had a value as a position claim. Frank Gee Duff
persuaded W. R. Wilson to buy into the co. practically
on its possibilities. Death ended “ W.R.’s ” earthly deal-
ings, and his executors got out of the co. at the psycho-
logical moment, giving Tom Harvey the opening to come
in later on to make his little bit. Now he is going for the
double, apparently on the off-chance of the pitch of the
North Lyell shoot of ore being so much towards the
Blocks’ boundary at depth.
* * * *
For its latest four weeks Mount Lyell (Tas.)
has to make excuses for ah unusually poor run. Only
492 tons of blister were produced, containing 486 tons
copper, 44,4620 z. silver and 9050 z. gold. The first month
of the half-year gave 718 tons of blister, and the present
492 tons is a big slump from that standard. The ex-
planation, that some of the principal working faces are
supplying poorer material, doesn’t give much comfort.
During the last month’s run it must have required some-
thing like 57 tons in place of the usual 47 tons of ore to
produce a ton of blister, and the extra 10 tons, at the
moderate working cost of 16s. per ton, is equivalent to £8
per ton on blister. Even Mount Lyell cannot afford that
kind of thing. There are 1,200,000 shares in the co., so if
the output averages 8000 tons a year, the profit on each
ton of blister has to satisfy 150 shares and has to be £3O
per ton to give 4s. a share. Even if the output could
swell to 10,000 tons of copper, the profit on blister would
have to be £24 per ton to give a moderate 4s. on shares,
and shares are worth over 30s. in the market.
* * * *
The old Thames field (M.L.) is in a bad way.
That old reliable stayer, the Waiotaki, after some 25
years of steady dividends and one or two gorgeous
bursts, is about on its last legs in the present workable
ground. Everything depends on the results from the
deep-levels crosscut now being driven from the 1000 ft.
plat of the Mav Queen shaft. So far the mines have been
almost untouched below 600 ft. This level will unwater
another 400 ft., and there are great hopes of some more
big patches. The crosscut has lately been held up
through an inflow of dangerous gas, the curse of the
Thames field. It had previously cut two makes of quartz,
one carrying gold. The latter is in the ground reserved
round the shaft by the Government, which subsidises the
drive. There will be some high gambling later on, if the
continuation of the crosscut lays bare more reefs.
-*■***
Ross Goldfields, Ltd. (M.L.), which started
with a great flourish of bottles not many months ago,
is already up against old Anxiety. The co. was formed
to work deep ground on the alluvial flats at Ross, on the
west coast of the South Island. It had a £5OOO subsidy
from the Government, and the assistance, as managing
director, of J. Grirnmond, who made a fortune out of the
ground next door ; but the capital and directors were
mostly from Christchurch, which knows mighty little
about practical gold working. Against expert advice
pumps were put in which were soon found unequal to the
work. Then the electric power failed, and the pumps
were swamped. Next a shareholders’ meeting changed
the old directors, and there seems nothing in sight but
the necessity for more capital and reconstruction. Shares
have consequently flopped. * *
Waihi Grand Junction (M.L.) is excavating for
an additional 60 head of stampers. This will make a
total of 100 head. Some new tube mills are also being
put in, and it is estimated by the manager that his
monthly output will be increased to between 9000 and
10,000 tons.

wanted him to make a huge railway splash, he has
plucked up courage to say: “No; we are borrowing
quite fast enough now.”

Fiji has its rubber plantation with a capital of
£50,000 in 2s. shares. Bull got it, or rather Bull’s Mary
Ann' got it, for the 2s.' rubber stock belongs to the domes-
tic servant’s savings by natural rights. In 1908 the plan-
tation yielded a ton of cocoa, and there are 160 acres under
bananas, 100 acres under rubber, cocoa and bananas, and
150 acres unde? rubber and bananas. It'looks nearly all
bananas and little rubber. Banana revenue is
estimated at £2625; butin 1917-18 rubbershould, according
£o the prospectus, yield a profit of £11,200 and cocoa
£2500, bringing with the banana that faileth not a ne,t
£15,200. The vendors got 200,000 shares for all these
anticipations, and if all the estimates are as reliable as
the banana figures and the figures relating to cocoanut,
then the British domestic will probably be disap-
pointed. There are said to be 100 cocoanut plants,
“some in bearing,” but the palms of Viti Eevu arq
plagued by a local parasite which could destroy even a
rubber prospectus'. “Estates’’are a mountain of stong
with good flats below. Sold for £1 pey acre five years
ago, the co. pays over £lO an acre. Yet anyone who
wants the, pick of rubber land can get it in Papua for the
asking. Why, then,, pay so much for a stone ridge in
Fiji? ,

The most modern-, simple and effective Vactiutoi
Slimes Table is the “ Wilfley.” ft is continuous, and Con-
sists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged in a
tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The drum is
Covered with a filter cloth supported on grooves on the
(drum, which are connected by means of holes to the
interior to allow the liquid to be withdrawn. Through
the trunnions at the ends of the drum the vacuum pipe
passes to the interior. This pipe connects with the
interior of the drum, through which the. liquid and air
are withdrawn by means of a vacuum pump. The filtered
slime is removed by a simple apparatus placed at or
below the level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly
the cake is removed the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp ready to
receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the sucking in
of air, and consequent loss of vacuum between the time
the cake is removed and a fresh one is forming. The
• residual liquid in the slime cake is removed by distribu-
ting water over the surface of the semi-dried cake. Owihg
to the fact that the filter is circular in form, it will be
seen that it is continuous in operation, and has no com-
plication of valves to get out of order; furthermore;
work is being done continuously during the whole revolu-
tion of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manufactu-
ring rights of this filter.* I 2

EXPERIENCED TRAVELLERS.
Among commercial men and experienced travel-
lers it has become a habit to carry a bottle of
Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea
Remedy in their travelling bag, for they know
that change of water and food often brings on
colic and diarrhoea, which gives a great deal pf
pain and suffering. These ailments are of HO con-
sequence when this remedy is at hand, but very
often resuhrin serious disorders when it is not. It
is always better to buy a bottle of Chamberlain’s
Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy before leav-
ing home, for it cannot be obtained on board train
or steamer.* . -

Between washin’ and tunnin’

down clothes all day long, 1
gets pretty tired l can tell yer
by th’ time as my day’s work
is done, and when 1 gets ’ome
th’ very first thing 1 does is
put th’ kettle on th’ fire and
makes a cup o’ tea f’r myself—
it’s about th’ only thing as 1
knows that’ll keep yer pecker up.
1 uses Robur No. J Grade
■— yer needn’t look surprised,
surely a woman can spend ar
extra thrupence a week on
herself when she works ’ard
like 1 does —do you know, some
o’ th’ ’ouses 1 goes to they only
uses th’ No. 3 Grade, and it’s
not at all bad, but, as l says
before, a cup o’ th’ very best is
what 1 likes, an’ 1 thinks 1
deserves it too, don’t you?
The Washerwoman.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 27, 1910.
My Dear Arini,—
Three million tons of steel swinging at anchor
in our harbor, and over a thousand blue-eyed,
fair-haired sailor-men swinging in our streets !
(I’m not quite sure of the weight of steel; my
scales are out of repair.) Sydney welcomed the
Dutchmen as it does distinguished visitors—tried
to over-feed and over-tire them, all in the way of
hospitality of course. Massed on a lawn for pre-
sentation to the hostess of the hour, a batch of
the blond army is an interesting study. The
purity of the race is so evident that
they look more English than does a like
contingent from the Powerful and the
Challenger. Norman, Roman, Dane and Saxon
admixtures make our own men look rather a job
lot. There is the further circumstance that to
England have come all sorts and conditions of re-
fugeesforcentutiespast. The Briton hasneverbeen
particular with whom he allied him (or her) self in
wedlock. The Van de Weyers and Keppels are
good old English families now ; they were good old
Dutch families when William of Orange took
up the sceptre of the Stuarts. German, Polish
and French Jews have been intermarrying with
Scotch, Irish and English Christians since the
Dark Ages. A Jewish family need but have
cash and a certain elementary culture, and it may
contract alliances with the proudest families of
the United Kingdom, as witness the case of
Lord Dalmeny, who is half Primrose and half
Rothschild.
Actually the wealthiest and noblest born Brit-
ishers do not jib, seemingly, at marriage with
“cullud pussons,” provided the latter have some
claims to position in their own country. Lady
Anne Coventry, a daughter of the famous house
of Coventry, married one Dhuleep Singh of India
without rousingany particular comment. Circum-
stances have combined to preserve the Dutch
from this sort of calamity, and they have done a
good deal to preserve themselves from anything
of the sort.
At only one period in their history, when
the religious troubles of the early part of
the 17th century were raging, did visitors
flock to them in considerable numbers. The
visitors they got then were mostly religious
refugees, and of the same stock, broadly
speaking, as their tolerant hosts. For the rest,
the Dutchman, through all his wanderings
during all the centuries, has been concerned,
in a very dogged fashion indeed, to keep
white. And a better advertisement of the policy
could hardly be conceived than the clear,
skinned, clear-eyed young men that have been
thronging our streets for the past week.
A truly dreadful example of the opposite
policy was afforded by the American tars who
were here recently. Every race in Europe looked
out of their swart, square faces. And con-
stantly one glimpsed the high cheek-bones that
told of North American Indian descent, and with
horrible frequency one noted the sallow skin and
full lips, eloquent of a near ancestor named Caesar
or Uncle ’Rastus. A comparison between the
Dutchmen and the Yanks is, in its way, a whole
sermon on the vital urgency of a well-enforced
white Australia doctrine.
Hostesses are delighted at the punctiliousness
of their Dutch guests. The officer, senior or
junior, leaves a lovely young thing, who is obvi-
ously adoring him, to go across the lawn and renew
acquaintance with some solid, stolid person with
one long gold tooth and a hat like an old play-
ground, whom he met yesterday. It looks as if
Holland expected every man to do his duty, irre-
spective of his own feelings, and that every man
fulfils the expectation even though he does thereby
disenjoy himself enormously.
4t Rose Bay, on the Golf Club hill, where
the earth is nailed down to keep it from
blowing away, Mr. and Mrs. George Earp gave
an At Home (in the wind) to the Dutchmen. The
Earps came to Sydney some two years ago from
Newcastle, and settled at Wolseley-road, Double
Bay. He is of some importance in the coal
industry; she is a pretty young woman, white-
faced and dark-eyed, with a nice hand on the
piano, a good voice in song and considerable
taste in frocking. Her more important posses-
sion, a quiverful of attractive children, played
through the grey and windy pastoral. Gold braid,
and Dutch flag and anthem, and English spoken
academically in foreign accents—the trail of the
friendly invasion was over it all, and the new
hostess must have been gladdened by the success
of the toenadering—which is the nearest I can
get in Dutch to entente cordiale.
Somebody suggested that one might look 28
though really 41 by swallowing radium; and
some letters ago, when I wrote of the possibility
of the Queen Mother and one or two other dis-
tinguished ladies having done it, I might have
added Mrs. Willsallen, senior, to the small list.
With her slight, round, 20-year-old figure, her
unveiled, debutante hat and her pretty frock, she
was one of the wonders of the Earp At Home,
throwing her own daughters into the shade of the
back verandah. By tne way, the black eyes of
one of those daughters were simply shattering to
the defences of the invaders. Blue usually goes
down before black. Personally, I like a man or
woman as black as a pot. (Now, can’t you see, in
a vision, all the blond Dutch eating their hearts
out?)
Another pleasant recognition of our neighbors
from the north was the reception given on the grass
plot at Parliament House by the Premier and
Mrs. Wade. Somebody’s intimate knowledge of
Dutch was shown by the word “ Welkom ”
burning in waratahs over the gateway. As the
same guests had came along to all these “welkom-
lng ’ functions, they were quite chummy by the
time Mrs. Wade (in a beautiful grey hat, with a
pink rose twining up the wide brim, and a
white embroidery frock) got off the grass
and went inside the Hall of Wisdom for tea.
Only a few persons stayed outside in the golden,
grilling sun. That enclosed grass and flower
square, the one little lung of our Parliament
House, is the warmest spot in Sydney on a hot
day. Amongst the outsiders an officer was ex-
plaining to a girl that Dutch ladies wore the
engagement ring on the right hand, and when
they married changed it to the left, and that the
Holland husband also assumed a wedding ring
when he took to himself the white man’s burden.
Mrs. Trefle (I hope that’s the kind of accent
she wears on her last “e”), wife of the member
for Castlereagh in the last Parliament, who was
“minding” a delightful stranger from Moree,
had another brass-bound Viking almost in tears
because he would have to leave without seeing
Wilga trees. She held a brief for Wilga trees.
Australians who were present had also never seen
Wilga trees ; but they dissembled their grief.
Mrs. Paling, wife of the Dutch Vice-Consul,
caused a block at one of the tea-room doors. At
least the little bag in which she carried her purse
and handkerchief was the real cause of the
obstruction. Her friends, and several persons
whom she had never seen previously, but who
wished to be introduced to the bag, were asking,
“And you really made it yourself?” It was of
bead-and-gold embroidery, an exquisite thing,
with something like lotus-blue kid showing in the
groundwork. Under pressure of questioning, the
lady confessed that the crescent, forming the lower
part of the bag, was the toe of a slipper brought
from Java.
All the “ welkom ” festivities from the 16th to
the 26th of September have served, as well, to
better introduce to Sydney Mr. J. C. Stuart,
the new manager of the Dutch Packet Line of
steamers. He succeeded Mr. Coeheret some seven
months ago, and is a much-travelled Dutchman,
born in Java, despite his Mary Queen of Scots
surname.
Another —a flying Dutchman this one—is Mr.
J. de S. Lohman, who has made himself an im-
mense favorite with the Sydney girls. He is not
a man o’ war, but is doing some world-seeing after
a seven years’ stay in Sourabaya. His flight from
our country occurs by the Empire, leaving this
week.
I saw, by the way, that some well-meaning
folks took our visitors to Medlow, where “they
were entranced at the spectacle of the famous
Hydro. Picture Gallery.” I think, without in any
sense casting reflections on the Hydro, works of art,
that I would have kept the Dutchmen out of that
gallery. They came from a land which produced
Peter Paul .Rubens and Vandyck ; also Albert
Cuyp, Hobbema, Teniers and a thousand others
of almost equal genius. It is inconceivable that
they were entranced in the sense that the news-
paper man meant. Many a man has looked en-
tranced after swallowing a glass of ginger wine,
or what not, when he was only wondering
affrightedly whether it had prussic acid in it.
At Coolac, which is a hamlet over against
Tumut, the Dudley party were “tin-canned”
last week—the first time, i understand, that this
horror has ever befallen vice-regalities in Aus-
tralia. The natives had been tin-canning a
newly-married couple, after the cheerful manner
of those remote parts; but, hearing that the
Dudleys were in the vicinity, they immediately
abandoned their friends and removed themselves
and their mouth-organs, jews-harps and empty
dippers to the siding where the vice-regal
train reposed. The tin-canner’s idea is much
the same as that of the London street
organist—he expects to be paid in coin or beer to
go away. In this case the Dudleys misunder-
stood the position, and hearkened with affected
gratification (they imagined they were endur-
ing the best efforts of the local band) where
largess would have been more acceptable. Finally
the musicians were shooed off by a railway
official, and went home in a sober and conse-
quently morose frame of mind.
I« England decadent ? Certainly ! No country
that wasn’t would support “ Our Miss Gibbs ”
for a straight run of two years. Such good talent,
too, is vainly expended on the witless fooling of
the new musical comedy. Kirby makes
ten thousand bounces in the air singing the
while doggerel songs. The new girl, Blanche
Browne, is the sweetest thing that ever wore a
tea-cosy hat. This top-piece is made of lace, and
haspink baby-rosesround.it. The lady under-
neath it is as graceful as a kitten, and possesses a
fresh, small voice and the neatest pair of dancing
feet. But so far as her connection with the
sumptuously upholstered showroom, of which she
is “our Miss Gibbs,” is indicated, she might
almost as well be a typist.
The music-hall piece will “go,” nevertheless,
on the work of the scene-painters, the milliner
and the dressmakers. It is superbly staged, even
though many of the gowns are too much on the
sombre side as to coloring. Duchess Florence Vie
has an amber grey and electric-blue composi-
tion that is wholly satisfactory. Another
costume—one got on tick—is a string-
colored ninon, with emerald in sash form
shining from beneath. Miss Fitzmaurice Gill,
walking with thrush-like steps in her constricted
gown in the second act, is one of the minor trials
of the comedy. All the “gags” on the opening
right were of English significance. For instance,
the sponging duchess says that Someone or Some-
thing would be “ as much out of place as a Labor
member at a Royal garden party.” With the ex-
ception of a burst of applause from one spot in the
circle, probably inhabited by strangers, the epi-
gram fell flat. As our Labor members are often
writers, barristers, doctors or the like, a Royal
garden party would not be a note the lower for
their presence. ,
The beauty of the “ Arcadians," Miss Connie
Simpson—Corona was her alias— leaves for Lon-
don by one of the modest White Star steamers. At
the farewell dance given in her honor last week by
Mrs. George Sly,' of Strathfield, the daring young
party showed her originality by wearing, with
great success, an emerald-green chin-strap,
with a chou of chiffon above her left ear.
The rest of her was robed in a long black velvet
gown. But the green chin-strap was the text of
the talk the morning after, and the fair damsel’s
commentators are wondering if it was suggested
by the famous Queen Louise portrait. You know
the picture of the “Angel Queen” of Prussia
coming down stairs apparently clad in her nightie
(really “Empire”), and with’a wisp of drapery
over her head and under her chin.
Since returning from a French school, where
she had dwelt since the days of childhood. Miss
Simpson has been more or less in the suburban
eye, and when she joined the Clarke-Meynell co.
she came into the public optic also. Her mother,
one of the well-known Embledon family, was the
beauty of Sydney in her day, and a great-grand-
father was the remarkable old barrister, David
Buchanan, brother of the novelist George of the
same surname. Barrister Buchanan’s vitriolic
Replies to the late W. B. Dailey and the late
Archbishop Vaughan may still be found amongst
thecobwebbed and moth-eaten “littery” goods
in obscure second-hand bookshops.
Another stage beauty, who leaves here tr try
her luck in London, is Miss Maisie Le Soeur. She
can’t act, but she can look and dress so well that
the acting is condoned.
The Royal Art Soc. closed on Saturday with a
brilliant students’ night. About 50 pictures were
sold. Governor Chelmsford bought two and the
Edmund Bartons one. Attendances have been
better than last year, and, among some more,
Mrs. W. Lister Lister and Mrs. Tom Marshall
gave festive evenings. Now the rooms will go
back to the art classes, under the direction of
Norman Carter and the Rubbo.
By the death of squatter .T. K. Clark, father of
sharp little Robertson Clark, now private secre-
tary to the Governor of M.L., Sydney learns that
Clark, senior, had four daughters. Two of them
came on a visit to Sydney from their English
quarters some few years ago, and stopped at divers
Government Houses. Nice unassuming girls they
were. The elder married the manager of her
father’s station, and is simply Mrs. Browne.
Mrs. Falkiner, widow of the late F. S. Falkiner,
of Boonoke station, has hit on a peach of a plan
for preserving her woolly husband’s name. She
has handed over £2500 to help Riverina State
schoolboys through a university course. And the
Government has come along with a promise to
found a scholarship at the Hay High School; so
that now the poorest little Cocky, jun., in that
part of the country, may climb to the dizziest
heights of expensive scholarship.
Mrs. Ethel Turner Curlewis’s welcome-home,
given her at the Australia yesterday by a number
of friends, was as bright a success as sincere cor-
diality, music and nice cookies and tea could
make it.
The Matthews, of Wahroonga—the skin, hair
and hides people (there’s an awful lot of money in
that trinity)—are back from Europe with their
five ewe lambs. Their only son has been left
behind to study something. Mrs. Ure Smith and
others have been welcoming the Matthews at the
Australia with an extensive outlay of the shrub
that grows in China, Ceylon and Java.
Mr. Humphrey Stephen, a son of the Septimus
Stephen who died in England, has been furloughing
in Sydney, on a visit to the Edward Knoxes.
His return to his regiment in India was an excuse
for much dance and picnic frivolity.
Selections from Mr. Frederic Manning’s poems
made up Miss Rose Seaton’s programme for the
second reading of her series last week. “Brun-
hild” and “ Helgi of Lithend,” both narrative
poems, and some smaller sets of verse were read
to a good audience, many units of which did not
know that thepoetisan Australian. He is a younger
son of the W. P. Mannings, and abides in foreign
capitals. In one at a time, of course. A while
ago “ Scenes and Portraits,” prose studies, brought
the youngster considerable name and fame. Now
he is working on an eighteenth century French
romance.
The Sydney Muffs Dramatic Society is training
some promising talent for amateur and profes-
sional appearance. Mrs. Boult, a pupil of the
late Mrs. Majeroni, has a special class for stage
dancing, voice production and several etceteras
that make for success behind the footlights and
under the limelights.
Which reminds me that a clever and pretty
young Maorilander, of Dunedin by birth and of
Christchurch by residence, Miss Dorothy Gard-
ner, has been engaged by the Clarke-Meynell
Combine for the “ Gay Gordons” production,
opening in Melbourne near Christmas. The
novice sings and dances well, and her acting in
Arthur Adams’s playlet, “Pierrot in Australia,”
—she appeared in it with Mr. W. B. Beattie—got
honorable mention from the critics.
Miss Florence Young, who must be thanking
whatever gods there be that she is not in the cast
of that fool production, “ Our Miss Gibbs,” has
taken a cottage at Bondi. Surf is most likely the
lure.
Miss Nora Venables (isn’t that name one of the
kind Marion Crawford might have used for a
novel ?) changes her name this week to Rutledge.
The popular ex-hospital matron will vow in a
white crdpe-de-chine, and so long as it’s not a
pipe-stem gown, she ought to express that much
overworked word “charming.”
A departer for tropic parts by the Van Lin-
schoten will be Mr. St. Vincent Welch, ex-
manager of the big Commercial Union Insurance
Company. He is well known here as a pillar of
the Anglican Church and a good judge
of pictures. Mr. Welch goes as the repre-
sentative of a new company that has acquired
rubber and cocoanut lands in Sumatra, opposite
Penang. On his return journey he will sample
the idyllic island of Java.
Barrister Harry Manning’s election calico signs
are out in opposition to those of R. D. Meagher,
whom he opposes for Phillip Division. To retail
stale news, the new political aspirant is a son of
the W. P. Manning knight, and is married to a
daughter of the late Justice Martin. Mrs. Man-
ning and her little girl are at present visiting
kinsfolk in England.
With honorary conductor A. H. Norman hand-
ling the baton, the Mosman Musical Society last
week did itself justice at its final concert for the
season. Gounod’s “St. Cecilia ” Mass (Messe
Solennette) filled the first half of the programme,
and so well was it rendered that one felt the next
movement would be the passing round of the col- j
lection-plate. Miss Zara Woiinski (sister of
artist-singer Wolinski, whose “After Life’s Fit-
ful Fever” hangs on the walls of our National
Gallery), Harry Neil and W. B. Beattie were the
solo vocalists, while Mowat Carter soloed in a
romance for violin and orchestra, writ by Svens-
den. Sundry choruses and part songs, the work
of Schubert and Bishop, and an orchestral number
or two padded the programme, which closed with
a sestet and chorus from “Ernani,” sung in the
original Dago language.
Yours affectionately,
AkENEHI.
Extract from the wisdom of N.S.W.
Minister Hogue, uncorked at the Glebe :
When the present Government started to make
engines the Clyde Company was turniner out 10 ensrines a
year. Now the company was turning out 35 engines a
year. Look what an amount of additional work that
provided. (Uproar.)
A voice: Why don’t you let the State build the
engines?
Mr. Hogue: The State could not do what Private
Enterprise could do.
Quite true —so long as the State, for practical
purposes, consists of Wade and Hogue. But
why should Hogue thus advertise his own in-
capacity ?
“ 10-2-4.” : This is from the ready stylo
of Foster Fraser. (It occurred in Heakth and
Home, 9/7/’10): —
A little time back I was in Colombo. A dealer in
precious stones tried to do business with me. He had
not what I was seeking. “ Will you buy a cat’s eye?” he
asked —a curious stone which has the slit-light of a cat’s
eye in it. I was willing to look. With great care the
dealer produced a package from an inside pocket, undid
many wrappings, and at last unfolded a good cat’s eye.
How much ? Mysteriously he whispered he was in need
of money—he knew the great sahib was a mighty judge
—not for Mahomet would he let anybody else have it at
the price—l must keep it a dead secret —but I could have
it for £l7. “ I’ll give you £l," I said. He was shocked ;
he thought I was joking : with indignation he wrapped
up the stone and tucked it away and marched off. But
he came back and showed me other stones. Occasionally
he would revert to the cat’s eye. Well, £l2 1 Well, £lO,
though his heart cracked at parting with it. £ll offered.
He beseeched me nor. to be funny. He went away, and
came back in 10 minutes. At the end of two hours I qot
that cat’s eye for £l.
Who that has put in as much as four and a half
minutes on the verandah of the Grand Oriental
Hotel or the Galle Face, or the Bristol, has failed
to encounter that predatory nigger and his cat’s
eye. He is an uproarious jest in the mouths of
Cockney bagmen, and a byword among waybacks.
The gem which he offers first, and which he never
sells, has some appreciable value. The ultimate
exhibit is dear at sixpence, being {>ortion of a
consignment of inferior glass that is regularly
shipped to Ceylon from Brummagem, for the un-
doing of the “fly-flat ” tripper. To me there is a
flavor of pathos in the spectacle of Foster Fraser
chortling over his £1 “bargain”—in connection
with which the black hound who trafficked with
him probably took him down for 19s. lOd. I
acquired a similar “bargain” in the long-dead
days when I was a trusting ass of 18.

The Heart of The Bush,
By BRODA REYNOLDS.

“ The Heart of the Bush ” shows concl usi vely
that the people of the cities hold no monopoly
of the human passions, for the story teems with
incidents in which love and hate, jealousy and
revenge, weave in and out of the web of
Destiny in such a way as to enthral the reader
from cover to cover. That this gifted author
has an intimate knowledge of the affairs of the
Bush as well as the affairs of the heart must
be apparent to the most casual observer, and
those of us who have either memories of Bush-
land or just “ that touch of Nature that makes
the whole world kin,” will revel in the absorb-
ing atmosphere that pervades these pages.
The episodes that culminate in the duel in
Dead Man’s Gully are depicted in language
that leaves no doubt.
