THERE is no gift costing a like amount that is so impressive, so in keeping with the
whole spirit of giving, as a STECK PIANOLA PIANO. Its dignity, its beauty,
the splendid possibilities for musical enjoyment it offers to everyone, have made it a
favorite with those who wish to express more than the usual amount of esteem or affection
in their giving.
People do not buy fine paintings and then hang them where they cannot be seen.
Neither do people buy books printed in a language that they do not understand. Why,
then, should they buy pianos which they cannot play ?
A STECK PIANOLA PIANO means music of the kind that people enjoy most.
That is—music that is actually being played by some one, and not mechanical music in any
sense of the word.
IF there are half a dozen members of your household and you have a STECK
PIANOLA PIANO, you can soon tell from another room just who is playing it.
The perfection of the STECK PIANOLA PIANO’S construction, and its absolute
control of effects, make it as sensitive to the performer as any hand-played instrument, and
distinguish it from all its imitators.
If you are thinking of buying a piano, or making a present of one, surely the
piano that is always a real musical instrument, and never simply an ornament, is
the one to buy.
ANYONE, no matter how far removed his or her previous life may have been from
music, can play and enjoy the PIANOLA PIANO at once. Let us send you, free, a

RECIPE FOR THE COMPLEXION.
One of our readers, who writes that she was
formerly severely troubled during the winter and
spring with chapped hands and face, gives us the
following recipe for a simple home-made lotion,
which, she says, is superior to anything else she
has ever tried, and also that it restores the delicate
velvety complexion of youth to those whose skin
has become paralysed and leather-like from the
use of lotions containing arsenic, bismuth and
pearl white.
Procure from your chemist a four-ounce bottle
containing two ounces Rose Water, one drachm
Tincture Benzoin, simp., and two ounces Flowers
of Oxzoin. Mix together and apply night and
morning, with the hands, or use a soft cloth or
sponge. Always shake well before using. If you
desire it perfumed, add a spoonful of French Fon
Fleur Perfume, which is an unusually pleasing
scent, and combines perfectly with the other ingre-
dients. To secure perfect results , bathe hands and
face, in warm water and dry thoroughly before
applying.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
, ~ Melbourne, September 26, 1910.
Dear Yarrie,— , ,
,A Masc[ue —still another Masque ! The Found-
lings’ topnotch .affair, so far, was the hall on the
2Qtfyi yet it was but a rootling of the bazaar-
to;be, qr maybe only a bucketful of soil, since
thq money it is to be used in provisioning
stalls. Whatever stall it helps, the result
will be the same to the unclaimed little Austra-
lians who are howling for more room and endless
attention.
People like some show for their charity half-
guineas, and they dug much fun out of their
masquerade. The strong Anglo-Saxon strain
in the crowd killed zip, breeziness, devil; still
they frivolled, though in a decorous way. And
picturesque—the thing could not help being pic-
turesque with a Tallis (of J.C.W., Ltd.) backing
up his secretary-wife. George Allan placed an
all-seeing eye at the front of affairs, and affairs
consequently didn’t get into any collisions. All
the committee women worked like busy-bee
butterflies, and a muffled figure or so with mascu-
line outlines sported a committee badge.
Bachelor George Allan couldn’t, however, resist
the dance; though he masked the face he left his
noble forehead bare, so there was no doubt about
him. At most masquerades here the domino is a
laxity, shed at once to show a fancy dress,
and the mask is a mockery. Not so at this
ball. The eagle eye swept the floor, and no one
moved there without the muffling; and horrible
threats of untold terrors kept the masks on till
the gong crashed at 11. Di Gilio played a volup-
tuous waltz, with a frisky finale for the denouement;
then stodgy hundreds stood still, in the full glare
of a chandelier, and peeled their mufflings in the
ttlattet-of-fact manner of so many swimmers in a
batll-housC. A dainty, mysterious little purple
doiilirio strolled across the floor pulling off
hei' Velvet mask with languid boredom,
and Her escort’s whole anxious attention was
fixed oh' rolling his wrap into a masculine
bundle; Sdarlet faces, beaded With petspiration,
Caniß orit of the shrouds, arid stteaks of uncurled
damp hair glued themselves over eyes. Then
there was a rrish for dressing-rooms and quiet
corners—but only then.
Mrs; Oharlie Fairbairn had her crowd all black.
There was a fiery double set—mask and
domino all scarlet. The Athertons and Robbs
and attendant willy-boys wore dark-purple
shroudings ; the purple was thin ninon,
and looked like a country twilight; One
swankey set hound itself in well-cut black
crripe, with small gilt bells a-daUgling j and
a slim, fine-shouldered crew hung themselves in
white silkiness with black trappings. The
muffled Crowd made a theatrical ballet-scene for
the half-guinea Watchers of the balconies. The
unfolding made the next entertainment. Fancy
dress runs much to legs—especially feminine
fancy dress. The skirts climb rip to high-water
mark, unrecorded since far-back teens. Some-
times the silk stockings are worth seeing, but
often middle-aged ankles shriek for shelter.
Real, live mummers came along from their daily
bread shows and fixed up lancers. Twelve o’clock
sat up and began to take notice. Julius the
Superb One arrived in the white brocade of a
Beaucaire. He handed the Lewis Waller lady
round in the lancers. Two society gallants, who
haunt stage doors, dressed to back-up Julius ; and
Norma MacMeiken and a Robb and an Atherton
finished the Beaucaire set. Her Majesty’s sent
down a “ Whip ” set, a jolly hunting crowd with
cracking whips, who rollicked through the dance—
dashing moderns alongside the old-time frill and
furbelow. The Warwick lady did not know her
lancers too well. Julius, superbly bending, hid a
stage frown. But the Warwick forgot even
her French court train later when romping
through a two-step with Jackie McComas.
Beaucaire favored only one waltz—that was with
Mrs. Tallis, who emerged from her domino in a
dairymaid sort of dress, all butter-colored satin
and foamy cream frothings. Mere man was a
minor figure. Two vice-regal darlings escaped
from gentleman-help duties for a blissful hour or
two, but slipped (unofficially) away at the clang-
ing of the bell to unmask. It is so rarely they
can escape in pairs for an evening out. And,
besides, other matters of importance were hap-
pening within close range in the city, with a
Carmichael in command. One girl, with a sack
of black net tied round her ankles, danced so
much with her own husband that it almost ap-
proached a scandal. Otherwise the breath of
perfect decorum overspread the frivol.
Society people ignored the spirit of masquerade
hv snobbishly marking their dominoes with private
signs, so that sets danced with sets, and the awful
line of clique demarcation could not blur. “ You
never know who’s ivho, my dear.” Another
damper on that volatile spirit of masquerade was
the solid rows of watchers. Good for the Found-
lings, no doubt, for two balconies were half-
guinea crushes, and two were packed with three-
hob watchers. But the dancers felt watched ; and
sweet girleens say that it cast the chilliness of
a frost over them to have so much elevated
chaperone.
Dolores arrived in Melbourne on Thursday, and
on Friday afternoon society rushed with quite
obvious enthusiasm to a reception at the Grand.
A good round dozen of the specially-elect acted as
hostesses. They placed Dolores in the centre
and surrounded her valiantly, in the way ele-
phants are said to guard the most precious
small elephant of the herd. From this vantag;e
point the adorable one said “How-do?” She is
a dainty lady ; she was dressed in a little black
gown that breathed Paris, and there was some
divine coral round about her neck and in her ear-
rings. Dolores looks fit enough for anything ; she
drops years instead of adding them. Mrs. Viardot,
who has been with her since she was a baby, is
travelling with her again. The one has almost as
many old friends here as the other.
Mrs. Mayoral Burston, in her favorite blue
garbing, headed the row of attendant ladies.
Mrs. Robert Reid made a large part of tbie hos-
tessing. She is an old friend of the. singer; arici
travelled-all through Maoriland with her oh her
las); visit. Daughter Miss Reid was there; too.
She does not often play about at parties, for her
practice as a vet. calls loudly to her. Yet she
knows as.much about music as she does of dogs
and horses, which is a lot. Mrs. Surgeon
O’Hara helped the party along; also the Von
Pinschofs arid the YVertheims. Mrs. Albert
Miller and Mrs. Buchanan were also of the im-
posing row. Someone gave Dolores a dinky littjle
basket full of forget-me-nots—sucli a dainty de-,
parture from the cruelly-wired flowers of tlip usual
ungainly bouquet.
Five or six hundred women and five or six meh
thronged the reception in a stream. Bhey were
kept from the usual scramble by a line of chairs,
lashed together by a cute management. Women
at a reception mostly scramble like so many
sheep ; but after they had been tallied in this
narrow race, there was a fine stretch of paddock
in the whole space of the big dining-room, with
plentiful fodder spread on afternoon-tea tables.
From three till five the crowd did not cease
filing in.
Pianist Shrimplin only arrived after the Sydney
season. Boyd Wells (who has made an earlier
Australian tour with Dolores, and has been
slapping her piano all over South Africa and in
other lands) was packed and ready to come along.
A last-minute wire told him of the serious illness
of his mother, and the tour was abandoned. Mr.
Shrimplin was given just time to cram a cap, a
collar and perhaps some pyjamas into a valise,
and bolt for the boat. Clarence Newell, the pianist
so well known here in the earliest Australian days
of Dolores, rooted this fellow-Californian oUt and
sent him at the point of the bayonet. Newell,
by the way, is now—Shades of Ghopin ! —a real
estateagentin Berkeley, California. Mr. Shrimplin
did his duty manfully at the tea-party. As a
male he was sadly out-numbered ; but continuous
Australian experience of Dolores receptions has
turried him into a passably good imitation of an
afternoon-tea young man. He has not yet learned
to drink tea, though, and his American soul still
yearns for the impossible—a cup of real good
coffee.
Tuesday night was a whirl of dances. Mrs.
Staughton gave a big, bounding ball at her house,
and the Germans were rolling in an ecstatic masque
at the Turn Verein. I asked a girl who “ came
on” to the Foundlings’ Mask Brill how she liked
the Sauerkraut pirouetting. “ Just loved, it,” she
gushed, “We sat on the floor and drank beer
out of mugs.” That’s all I heard of the German
ball. The Scandinavians revolve with thqir
annual ferVof in the same Turn Verein just as this
copy is despatched to its pressing engagement.
On Tuesday, also, the Masonic Hall was going
its hardest with a Seamen’s Mission entertainment,
With the rescued Carnarvon sailors as a particular
draw. The second boat had not been heard of at
the time, and the rescued mariners, who made up
the first boatload, were smiling round to the
backs of their heads at their own luck. All along
that boatload dripped with calm assurance that
the others would “be along soon,” though a
fearsome night followed the morning that the
captain and his crowd made Port Phillip harbor.
Of all the Tuesday frivols, Carmichael favored
this Carnarvon men’s welcome. Some of his
brawny men-at-arms slipped away for a blissful
hour of masquerade ; but they were back in time
to see their Gov. safely home.
Henlev-on-Yarra is drawing nigh—October 22
is the gala day—and arrangements for the feast of
fireworks and the flow of houseboat are well
advanced. Also, a single sculler and a champion
four are to be imported from Tassy by payment of
their saloon return fares; for Henley is incidentally
a, regatta as well as a crush. The rowing races, of
course, make no difference to the strolling multi-
tudes on the reserved bank of the river, because
they can’t see the water for houseboats ; all the
same, if nothing were understood to be happen-
ing in the afternoon, the multitudes wouldn’t
know why they were paying a half-crown to be pre-
sent. In the evening the lighted boats and fire-
works offer a full explanation of the gathering.
There are to be first and second prizes
for the best decorated.., and best illuminated
crafts, but the wily advertiser who would make
an artistic mention of whisky or boots will not be
eligible to compete for a prize. In fact, the wily
advertiser is frozen out of Henl(ey-on-Yarra nowa-
days.
“ The Whip,” at Her Majesty’s, cracks through
the same sensational kind of doings as “ The Win-
ning Ticket ” at the King’s. In fact, “ The Whip ”
has more of the true Bilanderson flavor, with its
Lords and its pedigrees, arid even its Lady Di.
No self-respecting Young Ladies’ Journal story
may be defrauded of its Lady Di. The Lady Di
in “The Whip” is a pretty filly, something in
looks between Olive Morelland Fan Dango. She
skims about in a natty racy-top, with ’cute motor
mufflings, but leaves the gorgeous frocks for the
lady villain, a beautiful Titian-haired creature
who is keen on swathings for gowns, and carries
a lorgnette for the confounding of her enemies.
Marie Illington, as the Hon. Mrs. Beamish—a
dairymaid who has married (and lost) a pedigree
—is really too pretty to convince folk. Rising so
in the world, she naturally clutches at Fashion
till she exaggerates it. Hers are the too-too-tight
skirts of the extremist. She patters painfully
about the stage, till one longs to suggest to her
Bert Bailey’s expedient when he got into tight
skirts. He took lessons from the bounding kan-
garoo. But what I was going to remark about
Marie Illington before her skirts got in the way
was her extraordinary likeness to Mrs. Brough.
She talks like Mrs. Brough, she walks like Mrs.
Brough, she looks like Mrs. Brough—the same
poise of head, the same gesture, the same man-
nerisms.
There are quite a dozen horses dashing on and
off throughout the three and a half hours of the
bellowdrama. If one does not miss its cue some
night and take a flying leap into the stalls, the
theatre will be robbed of the sensation of its life.
The orchestra is pretty safe if the horse shows
clean heels in a jump; but the first and second
row of the stalls will be just about right for the
excitement.
“ The Winning Ticket,” at the King’s, is just
as packed full of excitement and horses and
sports and villains and heroines. The Eugenie
Duggan heroine is a bride. She “goes away ”in
a hat exactly like a waste-paper basket wearing a
true lover’s knot in its ear. The “hidden secret”
she carries off is probably concealed in
the basket. If she confessed that secret in
the first act (as she is on the point of doing)
—’well, the whole beautiful fabric would fall
through, and Temple Harrison would be bereft
of his play. She doesn’t, however, and things
work out tempestuously. The crowning point of
excitement, jto my thinking, is in the last two
scenes. The semi-heroine sees the stolen winning
ticket in the villain’s hands. She stretches over
his shoulder and grabs the precious cloc-u-ment.
This happens in the Birdfoigri at Flfeniingtoh,

"The Gity Newsboys’ ftiatinrie, billed for Gctobeb
14 at the King’s, is being boosted along by the

Mayoress and ex-Mayoress are both certain of
victory in the race for the seat that goes along
with the gaud-mayor’s chair. Mrs. ex-Mayoress
Davey sweeps about in gorgeous frocks, and with
hair fairer than ever (dry your hair in the sun
near the sea—that’s the newest tip), with the
anticipatory smile of the Hostess-in-Ohief. Mrs.
Present-Mayoress Burston smiles quietly and con-
fidently, and sends out cards for a ball.
As so-many men won’t go to dances where they
are wanted, some girls are consoling themselves
with a dance to which men won’t be asked, no
matter how much they want to be. They are
getting a lot of anticipatory joy out of dressing
up half the number as men, while the other half
will remain just plain (or pretty) girl. A deadly
mystery shrouds this coming frivol, which is to be
held in the ballroom of one of the sweet dears ;
but men pals are allowed to hear just enough
whispers of the affair to rouse in them the spirit
of Peeping Tom.
Judge Johnstone has been having a giddy time.
No; I’m not speaking of his recent year’s health-
seeking jaunt abroad ; that is a closed hook. It
was at Ballarat. The just-returned Judge had
taken his seat on the bench, and a leak in the gas-
pipes did the rest. His Honor reeled and fainted.
But he has recovered ; so it’s not so serious, after
all.
A mummer beauty-man is taking his Collins-
street strolls in a limelight hitherto unexploited by
matinee idols. He and wifie take the pram
along, with baby gurgling among the fripperies.
It is usually papa who wheels the chariot, too.
The gallant Frenchmen who are going to estab-
lish a Melbourne school of aviation have not yet
decided which racecourse shall be the scene of
their first display. Flemington would give by
far the best crop of gate-money, but its numerous
telegraph wires are forbidding to the man who
will perform the dicky-bird trick. The possi-
bility of flying into a telegraph wire and getting
entangled with an urgent message is a very
serious consideration for Mr. Gaston Cugnet, the
boss expert of the party. All the same, it will
probably be at Flemington that he will take the
air in a week or two.
The first public demonstration of a Bleriot mono-
plane in action is to be by way of testing Mel-
bourne’s public interestinaviation, and opening up
the question of aflyingschool with a business agency
attached. The Frenchmen talk interestingly of
their hopes and expectations. Seeing that Aus-
tralia is a great country for le sport, and produces
all sorts of champion athletes, they reckon that it
should aspire to championship in aviation. Mr.
Gugnet anl the others have come all these 12,000
miles to teach the art, take orders for monoplanes
and shake up the Government to a sense of its
responsibility. They dream of a big aviation
tournament, with lots of valuable prizes to attract
competitors from Europe, and brilliant victories
for local flymen. They talk business in broken
English, and seem to have well-defined notions of
what pioneering work they are going to do.
The gods here have got back to normal pitch.
The boys who sell “ri ’apenny comic ”on the
stairs going aloft were beginning to lose heart.
All through the Grand Opera times supercilious
people spurned the luscious morsel of literature.
But business is getting right again—and for the
lolly-boys, too.’ Munch and melodrama go hand
in hand.
From “Rosna,” of Adelaide :
The Admiral Hendersons have been staying at the
Residence all the week. They have seen Parliament, the
navy, the train that runs through the main street, and
all the other marvels we keep for the amusement of
visitors. One day they were motored up to the summer
residence, Marble Hill, which keeps a champion view
attached to its front verandah. On the same night a
dinner and reception, both utterly official, broke the
Government House monotony. Latet, the visiting lord
of the sea got into his warship and scurried up the Gulf
to Wallaroo, whose smelters insisted on his staying to
dinner.
Meanwhile, our own duke of the wave was comman-
deered by the village of Quorn to open its Agricultural
Show. He performed the feat without mishap.
The other Sunday the army had a memorial service for
the warriors who fell in the Boer-extinguishing blunder.
Admiral Henderson was there; also the Excel-
lencies, with a pair of black-robed daughters in
three-cornered hats. The general public likewise at-
tended, and the collection plate went round to
keep the soldiers’ graves green. Afterwards the army
put several wreaths on its bayonet and hung them round
the memorial statue on North Terrace. This, you may
remember, represents a warrior on horseback, and con-
tains the names of some men who fell in the S’African
war,- and some who didn’t, but happened to have lnttu-
entiai parents. •

At moment of writing a procession of new spring mil-
linery is wending to that frockiest occasion, the Hunt
Club races. Golden sunlight, a landscape speckled with
dandelions and a breeze that needs Keats to do it justice
are combining to knock every other Hunt Club day into
a cocked hat for pure bliss.
Two nights, last week, the annual celebration known as
the Thousand Voices packed the Exhibition to the last
inch. The voices are drawn from the State schools, and
the young Melbas and Carusos make a goodly noise
among them, some of the chorus work being no dis-
credit to anybody. Talking of State schools reminds me
that some good Australian has started a Bird Protection
Club among the youngsters for the laudable purpose of
preserving our native birds. Already the club is
flourishing in no less than 50 schools.

JOHANNA.

Denmark, even as she can snap her fingers at the sun, the great enemy

How much smoother things would go generally if people stuck to facts, and what a lot of expense the Co-operative Coupon Company would be spared if
it were not so much engaged battling with misrepresentations ! But those persons who talk in flippant fashion about “ the bit of glassware collectors receive
in exchange for their coupons must have a poor opinion of the intelligence of the ladies of Sydney and suburbs. For thirteen years this Company has been
established at its present address, and throughout that time these ladies have been flocking to its showrooms—in one month 13,821 came—and we imagine they
would have got tired of the system long ago if they could not have got what they wanted, and what we undertook to give them. This is no tinpot concern, we
buy heavily in the English, Continental and local markets,-(just think of the quantity we get through every year), and keep a large and valuable ever-changing stock
on hand. We append a list of some of the goods we have stocked during the past six months, and we desire to say that these lines have been stocked in large,
quantity and variety too. We invite anyone to challenge the accuracy of this list, and if he cannot do it, we hope he will be honest and for ever hold his peace.

Simple Regime to Ensure a Shapely Figure.

the grace of an alert and upright bearing! But
cdrpulbhey even the first signs of the lurking dis-

forth. Nothing could be more unwise. Fat or lean,
the system is not to be trifled with; ample food,
fresh air, moderate exercise, soothing restare what
Nature inexorably needs. As for the prompt
suppression of any excess of fat and the recovery
of perfect proportions, here is a simple and harm-
less prescription which has proved lastingly

Marinola, one ounce of Fluid Extract of Glyeyr-
i hiza 8.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine 8.P., and

s x ounces in all. Of this splendid fat-reducing

tion, or you can do so yourself) take two teaspoon-
fuls after each meal. Perfect proportions will

PREVENTS PNEUMONIA.
A great many people have learned that pneu-
monia may be prevented by taking Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy whenever they have a cold.
Among the tens of thousands who have used this
remedy for colds and influenza duringthe epidemics
of the past few years, we have yet to learn of a
single one who had pneumonia, which should cer-
tainly convince the most sceptical of the value of
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.*
MORE BLOOD FOR
PALE, WEAK PEOPLE.
♦
It Means Strength and Good
Health*
This Weak, Languid Girl Cured.
People who haven’t sufficient good, red blood
are said to be anaemic. Vigorous health and
strength are impossible if the blood supply be
deficient or impure. The whole health of the
body depends on the blood. That is why there
are so many distressing symptoms in anaemia—
lack of strength, paleness and listlessness, indi-
gestion, heart palpitation, backache and headache
are only a few.
The only cure for anaemia is an increased blood
supply. When the blood is built up all these
symptoms disappear.
To increase the blood supply, Dr. Williams!
Pink Pills for Pale People can lie recommended
with confidence. Their one great mission is tc*
make new blood. It is an absolute scientific fact
that they combine with food and air to increase
the bjopd supply. Further, they have cured
hundreds of people of anaemia. While so promptly
effective these pills contain no harmful
or opiates. They are perfectly safe, and create ncj
drug habit. They are carefully compounded in;
the most scientific manner with the finest ingre*
dients for the blood known to medical science, 1
Over 12 years record in Australia shows how good
they are.
“I was always rather delicate, and fell intq
anaemia as I grew up,” said Mrs. William Brown,
Ocean View, Bay Road, North Fremantle. “It
attacked me when I was 17, and I had doctor’s
treatment for fully 18 months. My blood slowly
turned to water. Mother tried everything she
could think of. I got so bad that 1 fully expected
to pass away. Thedoctor himself gave me up and
said he could do no more. My skin was transpair
ent, my lips and gums bloodless, and my face quit 4
yellow. My feet and hands were cold and clauimjf
even in summer. Sometimes I could not put oi)
my boots, my feet and ankles were so puffed up.
They all thought that I was in a decline. I went
to doctor after doctor here and in Queensland, bu}l
they could not get up my strength. I was ordered
to the hospital, but I refused, as I thought J ,
would never come out alive. I grew weaker every
day. Some days I would be ravenous with
hunger, and another day I would not touch a
morsel. I would faint off anywhere, sometimes}
quite suddenly in church or in the street, and
have to be helped home. I was not allowed td
hurry ; if I did, my heart would palpitate and 1
would think I was dying. I was very despondent.
As time went on I grew pitiably thin, my clothes
merely hung on me. I was tired of doctors, to say
nothing of the expense. Then through a friend of
mother I started Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I
owe my health entirely to them ; but for them I
am convinced that I would not be alive now. I
gradually got my appetite and strength back, and
filled out. I could feel my blood getting richer

and now my health is simply splendid.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Wade, speaking at Dubbo
tN.S.W.), let loose this astounding remark :
Nationalisation of the Sydney ferries meant that those
who used the ferries would travel more cheaply, and the
nationalisation of gas meant that those who used gas
would get it at a lower rate ; but how would that benefit
the people of Dubbo?
On the same principle, Wade, as Crown Prose-
cutor, used no doubt to address his jury this
way: “Gentlemen, the man in the dock com-
mitted murder. But as it wasn’t vou he mur-
dered, why find him guilty 1 ”
MORE PIRACY FROM THE LABOR
PARTY.
The new Wilson Government, in Westralia,
is having its nose pushed into the Lands Depart-
ment, and is being asked if it can smell anything.
One Henry Moore, in particular, is pushing hard.
He says he came out with a fair amount of capital
and a large amount of family, and he declares
that the land he and his son applied for was
allotted to a Chinese (European) official who hap-
pened to be visiting the State with his Chinese
wifq and children on a holiday, and that the
Ghipese (European) official presently passed it on
at jj, profit of £5OO and went on to his job in
China. Whether H. Moore is talking the truth
or not isn’t yet clear; but quite a lot of people
are joining in and telling of the vast difficulty they
have had to get on to anything better than a sand-
patch or a vigorous plantation of poison-weed.
“San Remo,” from the North :
One reason why I, a Queenslander, am prepared to lay
a shade of odds that the Wade Government is going to
be thrown down on October It is the gloom that has
settled upon the Phidston Government. A Labor victory
in the Ma State will have an overwhelming effect on the
next Queensland elections, and nobody knows it better
than the Phidstons. I watched them coming away from
a Cabinet meeting, a few days ago, like hopeless men try-
ing to steal a march on Death. I asked a bookie what
odds the Labor horse for the N.S.W. Nationalin October.
“ Odds 1” he shouted. “ Blime, do you want me to get
work ? ”
Some ancient and fish-like language by
Colonel Onslow, the Wadeite candidate for
Waverley (N.S.W.): —
IN AUSTRALIA WE HAVE NO MONEY OF OUR
OWN, and therefore we have to have recourse to the
money markets of the world to provide for our railways
and other national undertakings. But the Labor party
say they will not sanction any borrowing unless the
undertaking will return an interest of 4 per cent., and
provide a sinking fund to pay off the loan by degrees.
If the. Government of this country had waited until this
could have been done, we would never have had a railway
line in Australia.
Think of that; “in Australia we have no money
of our own ” —none at all ! The banks, it seems,
have no money ; the A.M.P. Society has none;
Colonel Onslow has none; Samuel Hordern and
Mark Foy and Rupert Clarke and
squatter McCaughey and Albert
Gould and the Manifolds and the
Fairfaxes and the Dixsons and all
the rest of the gilded folk we
know have none. The Dan-
gars are penniless, and
“Wingen” Abbott is a
swagman, and the big
pastoral companies and
Syd Kidman are really
sundowners. There
were never any
£500,000,000 of gold
dug out of tiie
Commonwealth—in
fact, there never
was anything. Yet
this wild political
blighter, who says
that all the six
States haven’t a
copper to bash
down on a tomb-
stone, if caught in
another mood, will
declare that N.S.W.
is a tremendously
affluent place which
pours out money
like water to support
the other members
of the Common-
wealth, and that be-
cause of its affluence
and generosity it ought
to have the Federal
capital and the transcontinental railway and
the naval base, and every other old thing’
that is going. And if he is told then thatN.S.W.
doesn’t own a sixpence in the wide world, his roar
will wake the dead and make the defunct sit up.
The N.S.VV. “Liberal” press has been
starring this pronouncement of A. G. Huie
I notice that Alderman Watkins is appealing to the
electors of Waverley for support as a “ Liberal ” candi-
date. Bob Watkins, as 1 believe some of his pals
familiarly call him, is the same Mr. Watkins who over
two years ago was a rank Conservative. I want to re-
mind Waverley electors of the essentially anti-Liberal
record of Alderman Watkins. What guarantee have they
that Alderman Watkins will not be up to his old tricks
again if his professed “ Liberalism ” enables him to attain
a position of greater power and influence.
The bizarre humor of this resides in the fact that
the “Liberal” alternative to Watkins is J.
Macarthur Onslow. Onslow is tin illiberal beside
whom B. Smith looks cheap. As a hysterical
shudderer at change of any description, he gives
W. Elliot Johnson points and a decisive beating.
There is only one fleshly thing as immovably con-
servative as Onslow, and that is a corpse ; and no
rational constituency wants to be represented by
a corpse.
The Tories in Australia are anxious for
an Osborne ruling in this country to prevent
trades unions voting money for political pur-
poses. As strikes are now illegal in several
States, and the arena of industrial warfare has
shifted from the strike area to the electoral ring,
to deprive trades unions of the right to spend
money in political warfare is to cripple them.
And that, of course, is what is aimed at; it is
not anxiety about the workers’ funds that is
troubling the Fat papers.
Labor in the Senate was fearfully out-
raged at Senator Clemons's yell to the effect that,
“ by a system of bartering and traffic, the Govern-
ment proposed to carry the transcontinental rail-
way and Northern Territory acceptance pro-
posals.” Supposing Clemons was wrong, why
should the Government be so angry about it?
Even while the Senators were protesting against
the slur, four at least of them were eating their
own words uttered last session in condemnation of
the Yassberra capital site, and voting to fix Aus-
tralia’s capital in a place which they had said
wasn’t fit for the purpose. If they were angry
with Clemons, how can they think calmly about
themselves? And if they are not angry with
themselves, why be mad with Clemons?
Thus Premier Wade at Annandale
(N.S.W.), according to last Saturday’s Tele-
gkaph :
There had been remissions throughout the whole range
of taxation, so that to-day there was no body of people
on the face of God’s earth more lightly taxed than were
the people of New South Wales. (Loud cheering.) The
Liberal Government had furnished strong and sufficient
reasons for a renewal of confidence—(applause)—they had
made life much easier for the workingmen and women
of the State.
Then the paper’s financial editor arose in the very
same issue and declared that “ taxation in Aus-
tralia since Federation has increased 75 per
cent..” and he also said that the average is £3
18s. 3d. per head and that it is 40 per cent,
higher than the figure in the United Kingdom.
It is awkward to have one “Liberal ” authority
clamoring that taxation is down almost to nothing
and that the great and noble State Premier did it
all, and another shouting that it is up sky-high
and that the wicked Commonwealth is the cause
of it; but these things will happen. One of the
incurable crazes of the Tory mind is the idea that
the very lowest scale of taxation is necessarily a
proof of the very best government. Yet the
minimum was reached by the aborigines, who had
no taxes at all, and absolute Ereetrade to boot,
and no Socialistic theories; and they were not a
very bright or successful lot.
Another story of this cursed State Social-
ism has just been told to the Yic. Department of
Agriculture by a man from Carina, near the S.
Aus. border. Last March the man owned just
what he stood upright in and a knowledge of
Mallee farming. To-day he has 400 acres of wheat,
which should bring in at least £lOOO in nine
months from the day he started. When he went
up to Carina, in March, the State, having sized him
up, took him in hand. It first rolled down the
mallee for him, taking bills on the crop as pay-
ment ; then, on the same terms, it found horses
and implements for him and supplied seed. He
hasn’t yet got his holding fenced ; but if his crop
does half as well as he expects, he will be able to
meet his bills, make his payments on his block,
and start next season well found and with a good,
round sum in hand to throw at the wolf when it
comes round with the first drought.

SYMPATHISER: “Cheer up, youngster! What's

I fear he must be starving somewhere. For

Anyone who wants a few lessons in the art
of lobbying, as practised by experts, should
attend the Vic. State Parliament, especially the
House of Fat, when the Government brings in
the Bill authorising a Wages Board for tramway
employees and another for revising the fares. The
recent municipal movement towards taking over
the metropolitan transport business has forced
Murray’s hand ; but unless he gives over being
John Weary for a while, something "will happen
to his Bills. He is up against the best under-
ground engineers in Victoria, with a sympathetic
House of Fat behind them. For Fat has ever a
tender regard for a monopoly—when it isn’t a
State monopoly.
THE EYE OF THE NEEDLE.—MODERN
VERSION.
“ At a meeting of the commercial interests acommittee
was formed to approach the Federal' Government, pro-
tesing against the land tax. The meeting wasarranged to
take place in the rooms of the Employers’ Federation,
hut they were found too small, and it was held in the
King’s Hall.”
It is easier for an elephant to pass through
the eye of a mosquito than for a rich landlord
to enter an ordinary building.
H. E. Manning, a barrister, has come out
against It. D. Meagher in the Phillip Divi-
sion of Sydney, whereat the S. M. Herald
remarks naively that “present indications
seem to point to this contest being one of
the most interesting in the whole campaign.”
“Jock” Garland, the most effective platform
speaker in the “Liberal ” party, snared 1735T0ry
votes in Phillip at last election, while Meagher
(Independent) and Sullivan (Labor) were
amassing 4313 votes between them. The
Meagher-Manning contest will certainly be inter-
esting—as interesting as, say, an encounter
between a mastodon and a Skye terrier.
Two lots of people had the loud guffaw
thrown at them from different ends of N.S.W.
last week. The Wade lot says that, thanks to its
statesmanship, the man who wants land m
N.S.W. can get it; and the anti-land tax
lot—which is mostly the same lot—says that
the tax isn’t wanted because big estates
are being cut up as fast as they can be absorbed.
Yet on one day last week there were 74 hungry
applicants struggling after one poor little home-
stead selection block near Forbes, and 36 other
hungry applicants were struggling after another
poor little block near Bellingen. If he saw 110
hungry barristers struggling for two small slices of
corned cabbage, even Wade might suspect that
there was hardly enough corned cabbage to meet
the case.
The nominations are in for the N.S.W
State elections. Three members, all Labor men,
are returned unopposed, and 198 candidates are
hunting after the remaining 67 seats. There are
six applicants in the King Division of Sydney ;
four at Waverley, and four at Marrickville. One
candidate is named Smith and three are described
as Jones—John Jones, Robert Jones and James
Jones. The last is said to have already contested
seven elections without any luck. The Bulletin
hasn’t traced his record right from the beginning,
hut certainly there was a James Jones who got
three votes at Darlington in 1898; nine at King
in 1904; three at Surry Hills in 1906, and 15 at
Darlinghurst in 1907. This may or may not be
the same Jones. No less than i 4 candidates des-
cribe themselves as journalists. Two are parsons.
There are seven solicitors, six barristers and one
Tomkins. Wood describes himself as “member
of Parliament,” which should knock him out on
the ground of wrongful description ; he can’t well
be a member of Parliament when there is no
Parliament.
PEACE, PERFECT PEACE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We require a period of rest and tranquillity. We want
nv more restrictive legislation in the near future.—
David Storey, M.L.A. (N.S. W.).
Peace, perfect peace ! To rest I fain would win 1
These legislative ways are far too rough.
We, like the lilies, would not toil nor spin.
Forbear! The laws we have are good enough.
Peace, perfect peace ! Reformers yell and curse,
We only want a shady place of rest.
The gentle handling of the public purse
In quietness of office suits us best.
Peace, perfect peace ! Break not the sleep of Fat,
Where prone he lies across the people’s land.
Bid Labor slumber. ’Tis a useful mat
Whereon our Private Enterprise may stand.
Peace, perfect peace ! Do not kick up a row ;
Let Bowling rest and wear his clanking chains.
Remember, he is used to them by now,
; l listen to the thing that Wade explains.
Peace, perfect peace ! Shut up ; give it a rest.
Be sure it is the country’s good we seek.
One measure yet is needed. It were best
To keep seven holy Sundays in the week.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Sydney sporting event of the week was the
great race between Challenger and Fairbanks for the
speed pennant of the N.S.W. Motor Boat Club. Enor-
mous public interest was evinced in the struggle ; around
the shores of the harbor great crowds watched the
race, and the harbor itself was alive with craft.
One reason for this unusual interest was that a
challenger’s appearance was the second coming of
that sturdy Australian, Charles llelph, the man
who tried to create an Australian motor-engine
industry ; and thousands of people who had never seen
the man prayed for his success on that account alone.
Years ago he installed the Magic with engines of his own
make. But the Magic was not a speed boat, and when
the Motor Boat Club sprang into existence with .£70,000
worth of motor craft, it was foreign-made engines that
competed for the championship. Then, one day on the
Parramatta River, the green Invincible flashed through
sunlit spray of its own making at 19 miles an
hour—the Invincible, the first Australian-made speed
boat from stem to stern, fitted with engines
designed by the Australian Relph, and made at Balmain
(Sydney) by Broomhead, the Australian foundry-man.
There was nothing like that beautiful green boat in
Australia, and Relph—owner and creator—was practic-
ally the champion of Australasia. Then Arthur Davies
appeared, with a boat built in Australia to a Yankee de-
sign, and fitted with a Fairbanks engine of nearly 40 h.p.
He attacked Relph, but was beaten with comparative
ease. He challenged again, and at the last minute
installed an extra engine, which, coupled-up with
the first, tandem fashion, developed 7S h.p. After
a splendid struggle, Relph and the Invincible
were beaten through the propeller of the Aus-
tralian boat carrying away at a time when it was leading
by about seven lengths. So, for three years, the Fair-
banks held the Speed Pennant, none daring to attack
Davies’s beautiful flier. Recently, however, Relph, find-
ing he had a little more time on his hands, through
having given up the effort to establish an Australian
motor-engine industry, determined to come again. As he
had not the conveniences for building an engine of his
own design, and as his object in making the Invincible
engine had failed, he imported a Daimler, and, has-
tily installing it in a hull made for a much lower-powered
engine, challenged Davies to defend his pennant. This
was the race that happened on Saturday.
It was an afternoon of golden sunshine and
glassy water except for the reach that lies between the
Heads, and even that was unusually sriiootb. Condi-
tions specified for three heats unless the first two were
won by the same boat, in which case the third would be
unnecessary. A splendid start was made ; the Chal-
lenger bounded to the front, and at Clark Island was
leading by fully 100yds. Relph slowed down to
take the turn, and then streaked away with a
flash which placed him a good quarter of a mile ahead.
Running across the Heads Challenger shipped several big
seas, and Relph had again to slow down. Fairbanks
made much the better weather crossing the rip. But the
champion was fully a mile behind rounding Manly buoy,
and when Challenger was streaking past Bradley’s Head,
Fairbanks was making George’s promontory. Relph won
easily, with a mile and a quarter to spare.
In the second heat Challenger again started
with a bound, and cleared Clark Island with 150 yards
to spare. Davies had tuned Fairbanks up a lot, but the
champion was clearly outclassed. And no one knew it
better than Arthur Davies; but he shut his teeth and
made a brave fight. Anything might happen ; and, any-
how, like a good sportsman, he was not going under till
he was outed. He tuned his boat up and forced it for
all he knew’; but it was a hopeless case, and again Relph
won, by three-quarters of a mile this time.
Relph’s only trouble is that his temporary hull
is not suitable for driving his boat at its full speed. Con-
sidering it was made for a much lower-powered engine,
it speaks volumes for the quality of builder Pelquest’s
work that it stood the strain so well. At present, Relph
and Griffith dare not drive more than 34 miles an hour,
whereas, with a suitable hull, they believe they
can cut out 40 miles per hour. The average
speed for Saturday’s racing was about 25J
miles, with occasional bursts of 30 miles by the new
flier. An amusing feature of the struggle was the an-
nouncement of the daily press that Fairbanks is 30-40
h.p., whereas the ow'ner told writer himself that the
engines with which the boat last raced—and presumably
they are still the same—developed anything from 70 to
80 h.p.
* * * *
The Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. is getting its
club-house under rather unusual conditions. A number
of the club members formed themselves into a co., and
bought a parcel of land at Rose Bay. On this-they’ are
erectinga club house, which will be leased to the club at
6 per cent, on the total expended, which, however, must
not exceed £2200. The club hopes to be at home by the
eiid of next month.
* * * *
Germany has decided to go in for no more
Zeppelins for military purposes, and the great fleet of
dirigibles that has haunted the sleep of other nations is
to be relegated to the scrap heap. The short lives of the
Zeppelin monsters, the astounding feats of the recent
soarers, and the amazing success of the aeroplane in the
French army manoeuvres on the plains of Picardy have
convinced the German war-bosses that the big gas-bag is
not a fighting machine in any sense of the word. So now
the Teuton is setting to work with feverish haste to create
a fleet of aeroplanes. The old idea of soaring over an
army and smashing it to pieces by dropping bombs of
high explosives has gone overboard ; the aeroplane may
beused toassaultpositions which cannot be stormed ; but
even that is doubtful. It is as a scouting machine that
the flying man will be utilised —till someone comes along
with the heavier-than-air flying battleship.
* * * »
One of the devices for promoting a better feel-
ing between Britain and Germany is the “Prince Henry
Cup Tonring Contest,” to be run off early in 1911 be-
tween 50 members of the Royal Automobile Club of Eng-
land and 50 members of the imperial Automobile Club of
Germany. The competition is confined to amateur
drivers on their own cars, and will begin in Germany and
end in Britain. King George is to revise the conditions
before they are published. This mixing up of sport and
diplomacy is quite a new idea.
The members of the Automobile Club of Aus-
tralia, as the N.S.W. motorists call themselves, have
been going 1 down heavily for damages, and they are
getting tired of it. Certainly, on the evidence, the
motorists have not been getting a fair deal of, late, and
there is a feeling abroad that the man in the car is under
a cloud. As a matter of solemn truth, the N.S.W. club
consists of very decent fellows who do not scorch or take
risks, and the bulk of their accidents have been sheer
misadventures, to which the injured people have con-
tributed the biggest share of carelessness. So the
motorists are starting a defence fund, and intend to fight
cases in which the club adjudges the car-owner innocent,
and the fight will be to the bitter end. The trouble is
that members of the club are prejudiced by the vagaries
of carmen who are not members, and over whom the club
has no control. For the club itself has gone to a lot of
trouble to impress members with the advisability of
avoiding anything like road-hogging.
* * * *
Motors are compelled by law to carry a tail
light, and presumably all other vehicles are supposed in
a vague way to do the same. But they don’t, and why
any exception should be made passeth understanding.
One morning, recently, two motorists were runningalong
a suburban road about 2 a.m., and, rounding a dark
corner, went smash into the tail of a cart on its way to
market. The occupant of the cart was not injured, but
his vehicle was. Also, the front of a valuable car was
wrecked, two motorists were nearly killed, and'’much
good garden produce was busted. Now the man in the
cart wants damages. If he had only had some sort of
light on the tail-end of his vehicle the smash would not
have occurred. It seems to the writer that the people
entitled to damages are the men in the car.
* * * *
The great Wurrnambool-to-Melbourne road
cycle race—the 14th of the series—eventuates on this
coming Saturday, October 1. Early last week the com-
petitors began to drift into Melbourne and take trial
spins along the road, with a view to discovering its tricks
and peculiarities. Last year no less than 312 riders faced
the starter, and it is understood that this year’s little
lot will he greater. The race will start from Warrnam-
hool at 6 a.m., and T. Larcombe, I. R. Munro and T.
Gascoyne (the scratch trio) will leave at 7 a.m. Gascoyne
is favorite, and if he gets home it will be his first “ Warr-
namhool.” Larcombe won in 1904 and 1907, and Munro
waltzed in first in 1909 after travelling the 165 miles in
7hr. 12min. 51see.
* * * *
This year’s Warrnambool attracted 525 entrants
—a record—and of these no less than 409 have accepted,
easily beating the 373 of 1909. This in spite of the strin-
gent regulations laid down by the Dunlop Co., which
sundry people reckoned would kill the race. No less than
32 inter-State riders are competing ; of these 10 hail from
Maoriland, and they are a formidable-looking lot. There
are seven feeding stations on the road, and four checking
stations. On arrival in Melbourne the riders will find
their own clothes and a hot bath awaiting them. Alto-
gether, the preparations are most elaborate, and the huge
and gruelling ride is shorn of all its after-terrors.
I. It. Munro, one of the scratch men in the
Warrnambool, made a great effort last week to annex the
Dunlop Rubber Co.’s £2O to the man who can eclipse Leon
Meredith’s unpaced 100-mile ride record of 4hr. 52min. 51
4-ssee. Munro chose z. nice run from St. Kilda through Mel-
bourne city and along the Sydney-road ; and to be clear
of traffic made his attempt at 1 a.m., when Melbourne
was practically asleep. The Dunlop Co. provided a car,
and made full arrangements for the checking, timing,
and so forth. The night was clear, and the conditions
all very favorable. But the best Munro could do was
shr. 25min.
* * * *
The Barnet Glass 100-mile road race from Scone
to Newcastle (N.S.W.) on Saturday added more grist to
the wheel of Victorian-rider D. Kirkham. A week ago,
Kirkham gathered in theGoulburn to Sydney event; and
his win from scratch on Saturday was a fine perform-
ance. F. D. Walcott and J. J. Wilson (Vic.) —also scratch
men—were second and third, and the backmarkers
mopped up all the glory and boodle between them.
So John Bull’s lawn tennis authorities have de-
cided to withdraw their challenge for the Davis Cup this
year. The alleged reason is the refusal of the Austral-
asian Association to guarantee £BOO. The English would,
however, have received half the proceeds of the Davis
Cup match, and also of games against two of the States,
while, in addition, the Victorian Ass. had guaranteed ex-
penses for a three weeks’stay in Melbourne, conditional
on their playing in the Victorian championships in No-
vember. There would thus be no danger of the tour
failing to pay expenses. The real reason for the with-
drawal is probably the knowledge that Dixon, Lowe and
Mavrogordato (fine English name !) would not have the
slightest chance of victory against Brookes and Wilding.
America held the Cup three years in succession (1901-3);
then came England’s successes (1904-6), due to the
Doherty brothers ; since then the bauble has for four
years been Australia’s.
* * * ■»
The advance copies of what promises to be a
most useful work of reference are to hand from William
Dymock, of Sydney. “ The Encyclopaedia of Sport ”is to
run to 30 volumes, and include everything, from narwhal
hunting to butterfl}' catching. The first number deals
with aeronautics and motors, and is good business from
cover to cover, written by men who knew their subject,
and illustrated with first-class snap photographs of the
highest grade.
* * * *
Fame comes in various ways. One English
novelist of last century said he never knew what it really
was till he was sent a case of brandy by an unknown
admirer. There is a cricketer who now knows what it
really is. Two events at the Adelaide Show were won
by “ hr. g. Clem Hill,” described as a Maiden Hunter.
w - *
“Whaffor” : E. Manifold, of the Victorian broad
acres, isthe Jonah of the Australian turf just now. Week
after week his mokes just-miss it. Coir, the erratic £5OO
leaper, made things lively for two miles in the Hurdles at
Caulfield lately, then came a cropper at the last fence.
Saddled up for the Steeple, the following Saturday, he
broke his neck. Squirm, another of them, falls meeting
after meeting, just when backers feel like collecting.
Wyangerie, at Caulfield on August 3, was winning by the
proverbial street when he fell at the second last hurdle.
This mad beast just missed winning at Flemington and
Williamstown. Saltare, still another, was left at the
post when heavily backed two weeks ago in a six-furlong
flutter. Mortoa, an Epsom candidate, has a habit of run-
ning second or third or being left at the harrier. And
there are others. It must be an expensive game for
E. Manyacres.
* * * *
Jeremiah Allman, horse-breeder and turfite,died
at Warwick (Q.) lately, aged 67. He was an Irishman,
and let the world know it when he named his nags—
Dan O’Connell, Dillon, Parnell, Kate Kearney, Kitty
O’Shea, Galtee More and Barney O’Hea, for instance.
Also, his stud farm was called the Curragh. The best
horse he ever owned was Narelle, which won the Nor-
thern Derby and St. Leger. But if Narelle was not the
best, then Barney O’Hea certainly was.
* * * *
The totalisator gave itself a fine advertisement
at the last Port Adelaide meeting. Unbacked horses mostly
ran into second place, and their few supporters took one-
fourth of the money in the machine. In one race the
two dividends were £6 Ss. and £8 7s. ; in another, £3 3s.
and £32 lls. In the. Hurdle race, with a field of 19, the
winner paid £3O 15s. and the second £35185. Meanwhile,
the 71 per cent, commission to the Club, which nobody
missed, far more than paid the whole stakes for the day,
and these are to he increased next time accordingly.
As a rule, when the parson rises to denounce
sport, he manufactures gaiety for the more rickety
members of his flock. But on Sunday last a new
type of critic got loose, when the Anglican Dean of New-
castle arose and spoke solemn truth and sound horse-
sense. Defining the difference between legitimate and
illegitimate sport, he hit the nail square on the
head. When a sport becomes a money-making
business, instead of a healthy recreation, it is
an illegitimate sport. Geegee racing he denounced as a
gambling device pure and simple. Which is true. Horse
racing is no longer a sport. It is a gamble in which no
man has much faith in his chances of getting a straight
run for his money, for (the Lord knows why) once a man
begins to make his living out of a horse, that man starts
to degenerate.
* * * *
A crowd of 43,000 thronged Melbourne Cricket
Ground on Saturday to see the preliminary final between
South and ’Wood. In five minutes it was obvious that
South had either to go harder or be passed out; so the
throng settled down to a strenuous game. Briefly, it
resulted as the season’s form indicated it should, Colling-
wood leading up to lemon-time, but only by four points,
which a determined rush by South might easily annul.
Both teams played strong but clean ’ball, the Magpies
showing just a trifle better system, a shade cleaner
handling, an ounce more determination. At the last
change it was still anybody’s game, but they had
no sooner got going than the Magpies buried all doubts.
In 10 minutes they had put on three more goals, and
for a moment South was rattled. But the oldsters
pulled themselves together and scored a goal. Another
attempt took longer, but the six points were gained.
The minutes were running out, and two more goals were
needed. The Red-and-Whites tried another rush, but the
’Wood’s backs held the attackers off till the bell rang—
leaving them winners by 8 goal:: 7 behinds to South’s
6 goals 9 behinds.
* * * *
At North Melbourne the locals were meeting
Brunswick in the semi-final. North, as leader in the
first round, has still another chance, but there was no
suspicion of any “ approachments,” and an 18,000 crowd,
worth £345, got its fill of joy. ’Wick had to win or go out,
so it lost no opportunities. A lead of but a few points lay
alternately with either side until just before lemon-time,
when, like the other Magpies, the ’Wicks put in 10
minutes of paralysing play and piled on three goals.
North came on in the last quarter and equalised the
scores, and an all-out finish ensued ; but, even as the
time-keepers handled their watches, ’Wick spent its last
ounce of energy in an attack that ended in a goal, and
the game was won. Scores : Brunswick, 7 goals 8 be-
hinds ; North, 6 goals 8 behinds.
* •* * *
Prior to the ’Wood-South collision, the Metro-
politan Ass. “ Challengers,” Carlton district, encountered
the Premiers, Leopold, but went down even worse than
the previous week, Leo gaining the pennant with 69
points to Carlton District’s 30. This concluded the
bogging in of the Mets.
* * * ■»
In connection with the recent South and Carl-
ton scrimmage, it is now known that at least four
Carltons were “ approached ” by a “ man in the street,”
whose name and address they omitted to get. Lang
was offered £lO not to do his best, and took
it. He says he never intended not to do his
best, and that he used the cash to back Carlton. The
yarn may be true; but Carlton’s committee suspended
Lang till further orders. Two others were similarly
approached, but the evidence was given privately, and
remains private. The committee acquitted Gillespie,
but hung Fraser up. Another, Marchbank, says he was
offered £lOO if he and three others would play stiff. To
anyone who knows the game, Marchbank is the
last man a sane squarer would approach; and he
naturally told the wouW-be briber to clear. Previously,
Holden, of Fitzroy, had been visited by some
“ unknown,” and offered coin to play dead in ’Roy’s
game against South. He says he refused the offer, and,
significantly, he hadn’t been playing 10 minutes in that
game before he was laid out for the rest of the afternoon
with a punch on the point. Roy’s captain, “Boxer”
Milne, also admits having been insulted by offers of coin.
Milne is like Marchbank—none but a born fool would
waste time trying to deaden him. Brereton and
Pearce, of Melbourne, are alleged to have been “ap-
proached.” The point is that South Melbourne is con-
cerned in all these charges of squaring ; and the rascality
is nothing new, though it appears so to simple-minded
press writers. The League, in making an inquiry, won’t,
therefore, have to cover as much ground with its rake as
it would otherwise have to do.
# * * *
The football troubles in Maoriland over the
Ranfurly Shield this season were very strenuous, but
Auckland slaved off all onslaughts and retained the
jewellery. The recent match with Canterbury was the
star game of the year ; amid the efforts of the ambulance
and the unwholesome hooting; of players, Auckland
just crawled out on top by six points to four. The
casualties were the principal item of the match, and the
crowd of 10,000 wandered away bulging with the belief
that Maoriland football is a good game— a magnificent
game, in fact, to look at.
* * * *
Theregular football season having ended, as far as
Sydney is concerned, the Rugby League put on a benefit
match between Eastern Suburbs and Newtown, the pro-
ceeds to go to the incomparable Messenger. A match
between Newtown (the champions) and Eastern Suburbs
(which everybody believed to be better than the cham-
pions) was interesting, naturally. When the teams met,
Newtown was slightly weakened by the absence of some
of the veterans, but its barrackers reckoned it was still
strong enough to hold its own against “Dailey ” Messenger’s
team. It wasn’t—not by a jugful; and Eastern Suburbs
got it down and danced on it till the whistle blew, with
the score at 33 points to six. “ Spare me days ! ” was
all the Newtown barracker could murmur as he wended
his weary way homeward.
* •» ’ * . ■»
What time the lower reaches of the Brisbane
river were crowded with white wings,'and eights and
fours were struggling for supremacy in the upper basin,
the Rugby Union played the final for the Queensland
premiership. Christian Brothers and Valley bucked in
with enthusiasm, but the game was at no time sensational,
and the altered rules didn’t tend a great deal towards
brightness. Valley hurled itself at the enemy before the
crush started, and within a few minutes, 14 stone of
humanity, named Stanley, blundered over six opponents
and the line. Just before half-time a goal was kicked
and the score of eight points to nil was never altered. In
the second half, Bro.thers got exceedingly busy ; but the
final artistic touch was wanting and Valley holds the
premiership for one year, despite all the power of Rome.
IK* * * *
Maoriland closed its football season with a great
match between the twoislands. These two parties hate
one another, and inter-island engagements are always
bitter and strenuous, so the prophecy that the affair
would be “ unusually fast and exciting ” was fulfilled. It
was very woolly, and South, getting North down, just
managed to rub its nose comfortably in the gutter to the
tune of 14 points to 10.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association has decided to
retain Alec Bannerman as “coach’’for another season,
and to lift his screw from £3 to £3 10s. per week. How-
ever, the employment of a “ coach ” is of little use unless
the Association selection committee Ban-
nerman’s efforts by giving colts a chance in “b'g ”
cricket; and the Association would act reasonably if it
recommended that the selection committee should con-
sult with “ the coach” in choosing representative teams
for the season.
Touching cricket: E. L. Waddy made a good
start by slashing up 107 against Marriekville XV. on
Saturday. Last season this sniiter was a prolific run-
getter, and, judging by his form on Saturday, thescoreis
are likely to be kept extra busy this season also. isut
one can never tell; which is just as well, as there is a lot
of charm in uncertainty. E. F. Waddv, a brother, and
Noble also patted up 40 and 28 respectively ; while Triun-
per, Cotter and Warren Bardsleyall made ducks. It was
ever thus.

Saturday with 13 first-grade clubs, and 18 in both secona
and third grades. The threatened visit of the*?outti
Africans is looked to with some interest, and should gi\
the game a considerable lift.

A novel race helped to fill up a programme at
Svdney Exhibition Building, last week, when champion
skater J. Burleigh undertook to skate eight ™lies, while
Canadian professional ped. J. Fitzgerald padded»
five. It was a good match for a while ; but the s P e ®“-'
Canadian completed his fifth mile when Burleig
onlv traversed his seventh, and thus won comparatlv j
easily. Fitzgerald, who has a good athletic record, is on
the look-out for matches against Australian professions
distance-runners; but, owing to dearth of professiona
ism, the outlook isn’t very promising.
♦ * * *
Died recently at Scone (N.S.W.), Dr. H. J. H-
Scott, much better known as “ Twopenny ” Scott, a nick-
name which he earned through his fondness for patron-
ising the 2d. ’bus during his two visits to London with
the Australian Eleven in 1884 and 1886. He was captain
of the latter team. In Scott’s day four balls were de-
livered to the over, and “ Twopenny ” was the hero of an
oft-told incident at a Yorkshire match, when the crowd
howled for one Wade to be put on to bowl. Scott pasted
him in his first over to the tune of 6,6, 4, 6—22 off four
halls. “Twopenny” was one of a famous triangle <t
Melbourne C. C. amateurs, Billy Bruce and J. W.Trumble
being the other sides.
* # * *
East Torrens district (Adelaide) remarked with
modest pride at its annual cricket meeting last week that
it is the premier club of the premier State of the world s
premier country. Also that, being the home of Mayne,
Crawford, Whitty, the Chamberlains, and all the Hills
except Clem, it would not mind—if it could have the
loan of Clem himself, Gehrs and another bowler repre-
senting the State. Then someone suggested that, if it
could have the loan of Bardslev, Armstrong, Ransford,
and perhaps another bowler, S. Australia would not mind
providing Australia’s XI. all on its own.

Sydney Marine Court last week was asked
to gaze on a hole in the steam-tug Stormcock and
ascertain what did it. The tug was lashed along-
side a barque off Bradley’s Head, and another
tug, towing a punt astern attached to about a
quarter of a mile of rope, was ambling along on
its own side of fairway. Suddenly the punt,
which was the receptacle for about a million tons
of silt, shot off on a voyage of discovery at right
angles to its tug’s course, but still on the end of the
towline. The Stormcock people saw the drunken
craft coming, and, not being able to prevent it,
let her come. After deeply brooding on the
possibilities the Court found—
1. The punt hit the Stormcock.
2. The Stormcock was hit by the punt.
3. The towline of the punt was miles too long.
4. The punt didn’t have a rudder, arid therefore the
men on it couldn’t be expected to keep clear of shipping.
5. The Court thanked its stars that the punt didn’t
select a crowded ferry-boat instead of an old steam-tug
to bump a cave in, but it may next time unless rudders
are made obligatory.
6. Nobody was to blame.
7. Not even the punt.
8. Adjourn the Court.
Customs Minister Tudor is still wrestling
with the undressed doll question. Lately the
Department demanded 25 per cent, duty on a
consignment of dolls dressed in a hair ribbon. The
importers indignantly forwarded samples of the
young ladies to Parliament House, and wrathful
legislators shook these nude persons in the face of
the blushing Minister, and asked him did he call
that dressed. They further dared Tudor to march
down Collins-street, robed in a hair-ribbon and p,
painted face, and explain to the police that he
was dressed within the meaning of Tariff item
327. Tudor quailed before this form of attack and
went and hid. Yet the trouble was not his, but a
legacy from Best, his predecessor, who became
enraged at the usual methods of the im-
porters. Formally, dolls wearing the first
thing ladies put on were deemed to be un-
dressed ; but the importers began to add
a hair-ribbon here, a stay-lace there, then
frills, then boots and stockings,, sometimes gar-
ment No. 2, till Best wrathfully announced that
no doll'girl could come in as undressed unless she
was undressed, and that undressed meant the
word in its most naked and hussy form.

IN THE WILL of JAMES ARMSTRONG, late of Mut-
tama in the State of New South Wales grazier
deceased.
TO ROBERT ARMSTRONG brother of the above-
named deceased formerly of Ardnatinney Omagh
County of Tyrone Ireland, last heard of at Muttama
aforesaid in the year 1892.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that in accordance with
the directions of the Honorable PHILIP WHISTLER
STREET Judge in Probate made herein on the twelfth
day of September thousand nine hundred and ten the
said Robert Armstrong one of the legatees under the Will
of above-named deceased is required to communicate with
SAMUEL HIGHFIELD DICKSON and WILLIAM LITTLE
the executors of the Will of the above-named James Arm-
strong care of the undersigned on or before the Fourteenth
day Qf November next and NOTICE IS FURTHER GIVEN
that in the event of the said Robert Armstrong failing to
communicate with the said executors in the manner and
at the time aforesaid the assets now remaining in the
hands of the said executors will be distributed on the
assumption that the said Robert Armstrong predeceased
the said James Armstrong AND NOTICE ISFURTHER
GIVEN that all persons having any knowledge of or
having been in communication with the said Robert Arm-
strong since the year one thousaad eight hundred and
ninety-two or having any knowledge of the date or place
of his death are hereby requested to communicate such
information to the said executors care of the undersigned
forthwith.
MORGAN and MORGAN, Solicitors for the Executors
of the said James Armstrong, Central Chambers, Parker
Street, Cootamundra.
By SLY and RUSSELL, 369 George Street, Sydney.

CAUTION.
Beeckm’s Pills
Chemists, Crocers and the public generally are
warned that unscrupulous persons have lately
been selling imitations of BEECHAM’S PILLS.
" TO WHOM IT MAY CONCERN.”
We are instructed by the Attorney under power of
Mr. Thomas Beecham, the sole proprietor of
Beecham’s Pills, to publicly notify that considerable
parcels of imitations of Beecham’s Pills have recently
been sold to grocers, dealers and other persons in the
Commonwealth of Australia. These imitations are
put up in a larger box than that sold by Mr.
Beecham, the pills are darker in color, and the
distinctive labels and wrappers are markedly inferior
copies of the genuine ones.
YVe shall be glad if anyone upon whom these imita-
tions have been passed off will communicate with us,
as our instructions are to immediately take legal
proceedings against anyone found selling or endeaVot-
ing to sell or otherwise pass off on the public as
Beecham’s Pills the imitations referred to.
HEDDERWICK, FOOKES & ALSTON,
Solicitors,
BATMAN HOUSE, 103-105 WILLIAM STREET,
MELBOURNE.

WHENEVER you see a bear with a Con-
tinent on his back, think of THE LONE
HAND. It’s swag is a Continent.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Henry Horsecollar”: Be this clamor about
the alleged handiness of the half-crown, it seems to me
that correspondents are either barking up the wrong
tree, or else, in their haste to bark up one tree, they have
quite overlooked other trees which badly require to be
barked up. Many things are wanted in the way of coin-
age, and one of them is a coin worth Is. 4£d. Assuming
that you have that, then when you are paying Is. 4jd.
you will only require one coin instead of four as at pre-
sent; and when you are paying 4s. lid. you will only
want three coins instead of four; and you could pay
(Is. 10id. with five coins instead of seven ; and 11s. 4id.
with two coins instead of five ; and £1 Is. 4id. with two
coins instead of five. And think of the blessing and ad-
vantage of having a coin worth 7s. 7jjd. Then you could
pay 15s. 2Jd. with two coins, whereas now you have to
use at least seven. And man—that dry, pronged animal
that the fish contemned because he went horribly on the
land perpendicular and without fins—seems to spend
most of his life paying 15s. 2|d., or else dodging payment
ofit - *
Brother Jonathan doesn’t trust to luck or even
to patriotism to man his navy. He advertises like this
“When you are 17
you will either be going to college or to work. If at that
age you cannot afford a four years’ college education,
think about a four years’ education in the U. S. Navy.
“ You can enter the Navy at the age of 17. You can
enlist for four years. During those four years you can
learn seamanship, electrical engineering, mechanical
engineering, etc.
“ During your spare time, and you have plenty, you
can study correspondence school courses on any subject
you like.
“You will receive the finest physical training in the
world ; see some of the United States, perhaps some of
the world, though the amount of travel depends on where
you happen to be stationed.' You will have a chance to
save some money, as nearly all your expenses are paid
and you get regular wages.
“ If you like the Navy and wish to stick to it there are
o-ood chances for promotion and increased pay.
“ The Navy Department doesn’t urge any young man
to enlist. It merely suggests that you investigate the
subject and decide for yourself whether or not you care to
try four years as a bluejacket. Our booklet, ‘ The Making'
of' a Man-o’-Warsman,’will tell you everything you-want
to know about work, play, pay, promotion, etc., in the
Navy. You can have it free if you address
“ BUREAU OF NAVIGATION
“ Box 48, Navy Department, Washington, D.C.”
Exactly what the British Lords of the Admiralty think
when they read things like that this paper doesn’t know ;
but it fancies it hears an apoplectic gurgle or two.
* *■ * *
The Renmark irrigation township, in S. Aus-
tralia, proposes to pave its streets with beer. It hasonly
one pub, owned by the community, and managed by a
committee elected by the whole people ; and one of the
planks of the successful party in a recent fight was the
earmarking of 25 per cent, of the profits for road-making
purposes. * ■ * # *
The South Aus. Chamber of Manufactures has
made “Show Week” coincide with its own show.
“ Manufacturers’ Day ” in Adelaide is now firmly estab-
I fished ; on that day (which is often a week) over 250
shops in the city, and as many more in the suburbs, show
nothing at all in their windows but Australian goods.
| Governor Bosanquet and other leading men form parties
to go round and inspect, and the thousands of country
visitors down for the Agricultural Outbreak do the rest.
At the inevitable luncheon this year Premier Verran
mentioned that his Government had passed a minute
that nothing which can he got in the Common-
wealth is to be got outside. Acting-mayor Simpson
claimed for little S. Aus. the largest furniture
factory, the largest saddlery factory, and the
largest clothing works in Australia, as well as the
largest salt-refining works and wineries. Vardon, presi_
dent of the Chamber, hoped boldly that soon there would
be no “Manufacturers’ Day” at all, because “on every
day in the year the shops would be filled with Australian-
made goods.” (Loud applause, some of the noise being
make by the Very Naicest people stamping the floor with
imported boots.)

“ Platman ” : The latest feat of the shipping
cos. which trade to North Queensland is rather humor-
ous. When I applied for a ticket the clerk said curtly,
“Sign, please, accepting the conditions.” “But I
haven’t seen any conditions,” I replied; “ where are
they?” “On the back of the ticket.” “Oh, then I can
study them on the way down.” And that ended it. I
remember, in a case in which plaintiff secured heavy
damages for injuries caused by falling through an open
hatch, Judge Chubb made some pointed remarks re cer-
tain shipping conditions. The average traveller is really
and truly at the mercy of the cos. Against a private
railway monopoly he can do something; he can walk,
even on the co.’s sleepers. But the track of a shipping
co. is protected by sharks, saltwater and other unpleasant
substances. The Fisher Government might devote a few
minutes to a study of the conditions on the hinder part
of these remarkable tickets.
GLASS WINDOWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Britain will contribute four stained-glass windows to
the Palace of Peace at' the Hague. —Daily paper.
With thunder-stroke and rending rattle,
And bar and plate of furnaced steel,
Bull girds him ready for the battle
With twelve-inch gun and Dreadnought keel.
By giant forges redly leaping
The hammers on his harness play,
And happy England, wake or sleeping,
Is wrapt in cordite night and day.
About his outer paddock fences
A line of corpses may be found, ,
Dead niggers who, on false pretences,
Would trespass on Bull’s private ground.
If niggers will not quit their capers,
The frontier graveyard still is near,
And wars that don’t get in the papers
Sprinkle with blood the changing year.
Small Indian matters, mostly tribal,
Some Pathan rising “ firmly met,”
Bull hands his savage foe a Bible,
And backs it with a bayonet.
Now universal love is mooted,
And warfare is a thing accurst ;
But, though his faith is firmly rooted
In peace, he’ll see the rest go first.
Still, just to show his good intention,
Four large, fair windows will he stain
With emblematic signs that mention
How war and bloodshed give him pain.
Frail glass, of peace a fitting token,
To stand an hour in light and sun,
An emblem of a hope soon broken
By one far echo of a gun.
But giant forges redly leaping,
And strong steam-hammers night and day
In blackened temples, never sleeping,
For England’s peace and honor pray.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.


Beale
Grand Prix
(London)
Pianos.
TO INTENDING PIANO BUYERS
Having decided that you will only have a piano with the best tonal qualities, and the
BEALE PIANO answering all requirements in that respect, as attested by the award of
THE GRAND PRIX,
you next consider the question of the permanence of the instrument, i.e ., its lasting musical
qualities. Here the BEALE PIANO meets requirements to a greater degree than any
other. By the nature of its construction it is the most durable piano manufactured. In
foreign instruments the wrest-plank, or block which holds the pins to which the wires are
strung, is made of wood, and fitted into a space in the iron frame. In the BEALE
PIANO the pin-block is cast solidly with the iron frame, and is part and parcel of it. The
strength of the BEALE PIANO, and therefore its durability, is obvious.
The difference in the pin-blocks naturally means a superiority in the tuning system. In
the wooden pin-block the pins are driven in from the front. They depend entirely upon the
pinch of the wood to hold them against the stress of the wires, whose total strain is about
twenty-five tons. In the Beale metal pin-block the pins are fitted from behind, and controlled
by tension studs on strict mechanical principle. Our tuning system is the only positive and
truly scientific method yet invented. Tuning is the perfection of smoothness and simplicity.
It was for this frame, with the patent pin-block and tuning system, that the
DIPLOMA OF HONOR
*
(next to the Grand Prix) was awarded at the Franco-British Exhibition. In the 13,000
Beale Pianos now in use there are 2,800,000 pins, and every one acts with precision.

A FEW DONTS.
IF you wish to preserve your hair and keep it healthy—
DON'T wash or apply water to it every day, as these have a most exhausting effect
on the Scalp and Circulation of the Hair.
DON’T forget to use the brush, as brushing the hair promotes the circulation, and a good
circulation is conducive to a healthy growth of hair.
If your Scalp is dry and covered with Scurf.
DON'T think for a moment that washing it with Soap is going to cure it—AS IT
WON’T. The cause lies deeper than the surface, and wants skilful treatment to remove it.
And Remove It You Must, or you will lose your hair, as a congested scalp always means a
weak circulation of the hair.
If your hair is falling out, DON’T
use Quack Nostrums. When a person fails
at other callings, he invariably turns to
making up preparations for the hair, which
he knows Absolutely Nothing About. Treat-
ing the hair is a fine field for exploitation for
these people who cannot make a living at
other work. They make a general cure-all
of a pinch of Borax, a drop of Glycerine
and two drops of Ammonia—for which they
charge three or four shillings. —See Govern-
ment Report.
Treating the Scalp and Hair is my
Life’s Work. I have given 33 years of
study to the Hair and its Treatment, and
the information acquired is at your disposal
in treating your hair.
Wonderful Testimonials
can be seen at my consulting rooms, proving
that my treatment is successful if used for a reasonable time.
Scalps which have been nearly bald for years are now growing a good, healthy crop of
hair.

and my hair is growing real good.”

my head ; now, thanks for your wonderful remedies, I have a splendid crop of hair.”
