Is the Australian Disliked?
V
DISLIKED -a nasty word. People don’t mind being hated, but disliked I
Yet that is the charge which Ernest Low brings against the Australian
type in the October “ Lone Hand.” He gives his reasons, too. Foster
Fraser never went quite so far as this new critic. The article is worth reading,
even if only to disagree with it. The main charges are “skite” and “unreliability,”
with an echo of the South African notice : “No Australians Need Apply.”
But the article is answered by a sturdy believer in the Australian
character, J. H. M. Abbott. A hard-hitting, yet humorous reply. Read the two,
and judge. The'subject concerns YOU. .

A SIMPLE PRESCRIPTION.
Said to Cure Rheumatism, Kidney Trouble and
Bladder Troubles.
We know that the readers of this paper appre-
ciate good advice, and we are glad, therefore, to
be able to publish the following from a prominent
physician.
It is astonishing to know the prevalence of
kidney trouble and other diseases, such as rheu-
matism, bladder and liver troubles, which are
almost invariably caused by weak kidneys. It is
said on good authority that fully one-third of the
people in Australia who have reached the age of
maturity are more or less afflicted with kidney
trouble in some form. The most dangerous feature
of this disease is the fact that many people have it
without their knowledge, and consequently neglect
i t until it has reached a dangerous stage.
The least symptom of kidney trouble, such as
backache, must be given prompt attention, and a
good reliable treatment should be taken, until
every trace of the disease has been eliminated.
We are glad to be able to give our readers a
prescription which we have seldom know to fail
in any case. It contains no harmful ingredients,
is pleasant to take, not expensive, and can be
supplied by any good chemist. It is as follows :
Tincture Gentian Compound, oneounce; Syrup
Rhubarb, one ounce; Liquid Barkola Compound,
one ounce ; Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
Take a teaspoonful after each meal, and one at
bedtime.
We are glad to publish this prescription, as we
know it will help our afflicted readers who give it
a fair trial. If taken continuously for a few
weeks, it will be found to be an almost absolutely
certain cure for rheumatism, kidney and bladder
trouble, backache and all allied complaints.
Note.—Other papers are privileged to copy the
above. —Advt.

IN
BANANA LAND
The last of Toolburra station, the first run
occupied on the Darling Downs, was sold the
other day. Toolburra was the first property
bought by the Government under the Repurchase
Act of 1894 ; but the owner retained a scrap of it,
and this was the piece that was sold recently.
Some people who evidently don’t believe
that the white man can’t live up North, are going
to give the trout, essentially a cold-water fish, a
chance to try the climate there and help to
further explode an old myth. The Queensland
Government has sent some ova to Herberton, and
the Sportsman’s Club, of Charters Towers, has got
5000 rainbow trout ova from Maoriland.
Of the mighty firm of John Collins and
Sons, land-absorbers and cattle-owners, only R.M.
is left. John, the founder of the firm, went first;
then son William, who died early last year; and
now J.G. The third son, R.M., the sole survivor,
represented Albert in the Queensland Assembly
for a while, but couldn’t spare the time from the
more fascinating job of counting his bullocks.
A special breed of lantana is credited, at
Cairns (N.Q.), with producing in local stock that
mysterious disease known as “ pink nose.” This
vegetable devil, which was originally imported to
Australia by a Port Macquarie (N.S.W.) parson
as'a hedge plant, has hoofed it right up the coast
to North Queensland, and founded a dynasty or
board of control over practically 2000 miles of
country. Some areas have become “lantana
sick,” just as others become “ banana sick,” and
others, again, “Chow sick,” though the latter
trouble is one of the very few that doesn’t spread
as quickly as it should.
Governor Macgregor orated recently at
the annual meeting of the Queensland Charity
Organisation Society in this fashion: “The
poor have always been with us, are always with
us f and always will be.” Why this everlasting
assumption that poverty always has been and
always will be? As a matter of history, there
have been many races that have known neither
poverty nor riches; thus, if Prescott is to be
believed, more than one powerful nation in South
America existed happily under a system of State
Socialism, and knew absolutely nothing of desti-
tution. But Fat loves the statement that poverty
will always be with us because it implies that
Wealth must be an eternal institution.
“ San Remo ” rings in some parsons :
There are a few parsons in the north of Queensland
who occasionally chase the sweater with an irreverent
brickbat, and I am reminded of some of them by the
proposal in New South Wales to make Saturday the
general half-holiday. Years before the Queensland Shops
and Factories Act was passed the Rockhampton shop-
assistants put up a fight for the Saturday half-holiday,
and most of the clergy took off their coats and waded in
A voluntary agreement was come to, but
was broken in three weeks by blacklegging and sweating
shopkeepers. Bishop Dawes (Anglican), just dead,
was one of the most strenuous battlers for the agree-
ment. He wasn’t satisfied with praying for the sweater
in the pulpit, but mounted the public platform to
deal with him from a. secular point of view. One
night the Bishop was holding forth on the evils of
late shopping to a large audience, and painted a lurid
picture of the wearied shop-assistant struggling to satisfy
Mrs. Upper Crust at 9.45 p.m., Saturday. In his best
Oxfordian drawl he described Mrs. Ruck (or Mrs. Snag
or Mrs. Nark) ordering down bale after bale, box after
box, packet after packet, until the counter and the floor
looked as if an earthquake had upper-cut the establish-
ment ; and then languidly remarking that she would
prefer to see the goods by daylight, as the colors were
so deceptive under an artificial light. “With which re-
marks ” said the Bishop, “ Mrs. Ruck glides away
to the tea-rooms, where she probably keeps the
unluckj- refreshment girl attending her until 11
p.m.” The sally was greeted with uproarious ap-
plause, becaule it was well known that Mrs. Bishop
was a Ruck in Class A, and the large number of shop
assistants present thought the Bishop was rubbing it in
to his worse half. Unfortunately, a good, kind cat told
the lady all about it next day, and the story goes that
the Bishop got the thick end of what followed. But
clerics, like whisky brands, vary. I remember a parson
whose wife was a notorious late shopper on Saturday
nights, and an almighty Nark at that, and her parcels
had to be delivered if it was 1 a.m. on Sunday when they
arrived. Next door to the parson lived a man who pro-
fessed agnosticism, and these two wheeled into the
middle of a scrap that was raging as to “ Why People
Don’t Go to Church.” Between them they threatened
to flood out the local press with their letters on the
subject. The man of sin was getting a bit the worse of
the'deal when his luck was turned by a new-chum carter
knocking him up about half-past one on a Sunday morn-
ing to ask if Rev. —— lived there. Next day the en-
raged unbeliever tore into the evening paper with a
letter headed: “One Reason Why Some People Don’t
Go to Church—Because They’re Kept Out All Night De-
livering Sunday Hats for Parsons’ Wives.” The dis-
cussion suddenly dropped dead, and the assumption was
that the parson had taken his 10 seconds on the floor.
The members of the counter jumping pro-
fession of Queensland will keep a kind
thought for the departed Gaiters, Bishop
Dawes. It was at Rockhampton that he
put up his best fight for them and their
Saturday half-holiday, and won. One evening he
broke a meeting right up by depicting the woes
of a worried shop-assistant handling a “ruck”
(tough female customer) at 9.50 on a Saturday
night. The Bishop drew such a vivid picture of
the “ ruck ” and her ways that all Rockhampton
believed it recognised the lady at the first look.
She was—or Rockhampton believed she was—the
lady the Bishop had the best right to rebuke
either in public or in private.
Brisbane Courier is having no luck.
Lately it opposed the suggestion to found a co-op-
erative hog factory because, it said, private con-
cerns were paying ever so much more for pigs than
the Byron Bay (N.S.W.) concern, the biggest dis-
play of co-operative hog effort in Australia.
Then along came Byron Bay with figures to show
that it paid £d. more per hog-pound than any
private factory. Here the Courier dropped pigs
and deplored the Commonwealth Land Tax, and
predicted—as the Tory sheet has been predicating
for years—that Capital would take his shekels
and fly away forthwith. Simultaneously, and
evidently to prove its words, it announced that a
half-dozen big wool firms had decided to erect
very large stores in Brisbane.
HER ADDED RESPONSI-
BILITIES.
EMPLOYED: “ Want a rise in salary ?
Why, your work hasn't increased .”
LADY CLERK: “No, sir; but I've a
husband to support now."
Warwick gave Arthur Morgan, who has
left there to live in Brisbane, a great send-off the
other night. The pretty Downs town is very
proud of Morgan, and credits him with much of
its progress. He was born there, was mayor five
times, owned and edited its paper, and with the
exception of one Parliament—he didn’t go up for
election that time—represented it from 1887 till
1906, when he gave up his seat and the Premier-
ship to become president of the House of Fossils.
“Wonga,” regarding “The Leak in the
Big Tank ” (B. 22/9/TO)
There’s another side to the matter of the leak in the
big tank, noted by “ Atacamite.” While it is true that
the present method of handling our artesian water-supply
is more wasteful than a “Liberal” Administration, the
big question is, Can the flow be regulated? Sundry
artesian water experts say NO in a ver3 r decided tone.
Some years ago the Bananaland Government instructed
one of its engineers to put a tap on a newly-opened bore
for purposes of regulation. Said officer did it, though he
felt very chary about it. Orders were to “turn her off ”
for 24 hours and note the effect; but after the flow had
been stopped for about half an hour, the water started
coming up the sides so strongly that orders were broken
and the tap removed. It’s a risky business playing the
goat with bores running down hundreds and thousands
of feet, and experimenting with a force whose power
isn’t too well understood. There are other warnings to
go slow. Every bore has w'ater coming out on the surface
outside the casing. The fact is that the best-sunk bores
leak. It is a practical impossibility to make the joints so
that they won’t, unless a beetle or a State insect is
trained to go down the casing inside, with a soldering
iron.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 24, 1910.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Macgregors are at Bundaberg, or were
until this evening. There has been a carnival in
the town that alleges it is the only decently laid-
out one in the State, and there have been high
revels in and around Bourbon-street. How Bun-
daberg came to get wide streets and a public
spirit is yet a mystery which his Ex. may be able
to explain when he returns, for he has a way of
getting to the bottom of things. Next week, he
will convey his women-folk to Southport, where a
salt water carnival will be in progress,
It’s really dreadful the way women’s minds will
run to clothes. They are not even content to deck
themselves out. but are turning their attention to
the beautification of man (and Heaven knows he
needs it, I say to myself). The latest local outbreak
is a cope for Archbishop Donaldson to open the
red-headed cathedral in. If you don’t know what
a cope is, it’s very like a bath gown of white bro-
cade with wide revers of red, worked in roses and
seven-pointed, each of which take half a decent
working day to embroider. It’s also rather like
a nightie. However, this one, along with
a case to pack it in, and an address (touched up
with gorgeous poinsettia flowers by the clever
fingers of Miss Macauley Turner) was handed
over to the churchman by the Archdeacon on
Thursday, at the new Deanery. Here, Mrs. Dean
Pattinson fed the multitude on the verandah and
in her pretty rooms full of old china and silver.
The new deanery, by the way, is the old Adelaide
House, from which Sir George Bowen read the
proclamation declaring Queensland a colony. The
ancient relic, now quite rejuvenated, stands on
the cliff next the Cathedral, and has one of the
most lovely views in the city. The clever fingers
which worked the previously hinted-at embroi-
dery belong to Mrs. Hugh Simmons and the
Misses Bertha Wassell and M. Bolton, while the
jewels—amethysts, of course—were given by
Major San key. The garment was mainly an
offering from wives of clergy and a few well-
dowered countrywomen.
Dave Bowman, the honest leader of the State
Labor party, is an inmate of the Mater Hospital,
and things didn’t look too bright. He has hoed a
hard row, full of big fights and abuse, and not all
of it from his traditional enemies. Quite recently
he lost his daughter, and now, after much suffer-
ing from a throat complaint, he has had a slight
hemorrhage on the brain.
Everyone is busy telling everyone else in stage
whispers that a third party has discovered a gold
mine on the very spot where the new Government
House was to be ; and now, instead of a few men
levelling a foundation, there may be millions of
hungry prospectors spoiling the place with holes. I
wonder what they will call the field. Kidston’s
Folly, perhaps, because the Premier rejected it as
a site for the University. Just think how con-
venient it would have been, with its own School
of Mines in its own back yard, digging up its own
revenue, “and no further worry,” as the advt. of
the Perfection washing machine puts it.
The ways of Anti-Sosh are weird. That un-
certain object which calls itself the P.P.L., and
is in reality Kidston and Co.’s canvassing com-
mittee, promoted a lecture one night lately, when
the evils of unions and wages boards were dis-
cussed. The chairman apologised for the fact
that there were only about 15 people present,
though they had sent out 140 invitations, and put
it down to other attractions—a picture show and
a church meeting. At first, the excuse seemed
feeble; but, after listening to the mourners, I
came to the conclusion that there were 125 sen-
sible folk in South Brisbane. If I had to choose
between that meeting and listening to 64 junior
Eisteddfodders singing “My Mother Bids me
Bind my Hair,” I’d choose the hair.
De Burgh Persse, as ancient a squatter as the
Albert River holds, had a birthday party at Tab-
ragalba this week. Whatever else De Burgh may
lack, it isn’t grandchildren, for there are six little
Joyces, one little Charlie, the Burgo twins, and
one little Fitz-Joyce, as well as a round dozen of
cousins and their lawful possessions in the way of
wives. I hear that D. T. Seymour, Queensland’s
first Police Commissioner, was also in the party,
his qualification being a friendship of something
like 47 years. He came out a gay young officer
of the 12th Regiment of Foot somewhere about 50
years ago, and has been on the pension-list 15
years.
A concert in London, given by Melba, Frederick
Brandon and an orchestra under Landon Ronald,
served as a debut for the Brisbane contralto, Evie
Doyle, a young person of prepossessing appear-
ance and golden notes, whom Melba took under
her wing on her last Australian trip. Evie^ chose
two Tosti compositions, “Love’s Way and
“ Once More,” and she seems to have done well.
Postcards drop along from Mrs. Bob Philp, who
with sister Ellie and brother Bill or John or
Charlie, is putting in time amid “Akenehi’s” green-
robed princes and brown Siamese or Javanese
boys. Latest news is that they have struck
Chinatown, and, if pictures go for anything, it
isn’t as yellow as it’s painted.
To-day saw the official 'unfurling of the
white wings upon the river, when the Brisbane
Dingey Club held its first outing, with crowded
flagships and no end of enthusiasm.
John Frances Buckland, one-time Parliamen-
tary rep. of Bulimba, where “Bishop” Barnes
now holds sway, passed on this week. He has
resided hereabouts since 1862, and was twice
elected M.L.A. The financial troubles of the
early ’9o’s affected him seriously, and he retired
from Parliament. He was followed in the House
by James Dickson, afterwards knighted. A band
of old friends, headed by Mr. Kelso, are receiving
subs, to ensure a more comfortable living for the
widow.
The she-leaguers held a floating Cabinet on
Monday, when about two miles of report and 15
ditto of speeches were rolled off at the multitude.
There was quite a crop of politicians—Barnes,
Swayne and Petrie; and the Home Secretary
only escaped by the hair of his teeth.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
“ Opened by his Excellency,” being a few
remarks by F. Born :
His Excellency the State Governor of Barataria was
fond of travelling', and saw a great deal of his subject
Baratarians. Also, he gathered quite a miscellaneous
stock of observations —good, bad and indifferent.
While on a visit to a country town, he had occa-
sion to walk up one morning to the railway-station-
Here his attention was attracted by an odd-
looking individual, locally known as “ Billy the
Loony,” who walked up and down the platform,
tapped the wheels of the train now and again, cast
a critical eye over the baggage, and finally crowned his
performances when the station-master gave the signal
for departure by imitating that worthy, and waving his
hand to the engine-driver.
“ Got her off safe again,” he murmured to himself, with
immense satisfaction, as the train puffed away in the
distance.
“Couldn’t manage to start her without you, Billy,”
said a loafer standing near.
“Of course not. You see it’s the practice I’ve had,”
said the loony with a smirk.
His Excellency smiled a cynical smile. “ Let me see.
Doesn’t Parliament meet to-morrow, and am I not sup-
posed to start the machine ? Does anybody dare to think
or insinuate that the thing could possibly start without
me? What did Billy say? Look at the practice I’ve
had i "
The earth-eating disease, which has become
such a widespread horror at Cairns and other
parts of North Queensland, is probably the legacy
of the imported nigger to whom the North used to
be so much addicted. It is caused by worms of
great tonnage, and it makes the patient a
stunted, bloodless, feeble, staring wreck
the “hookworm stare” is a conspicuous symp-
tom. A boy of 16 who has had ankylos-
tomiasis from childhood usually looks as if he
were aged about 10. In the southern section —
the old slave section—of the U.S., millions of
people are afflicted with the complaint—in fact, the
backwardness, weariness and futility, the slack-
ness, incompetence and leaning-up habits
of the mean whites: “down south” are largely
ascribed to it. The nigger, of course, has it, too
—in fact, he brought it to the country; but he
apparently had it for thousands of years before
he shifted to the U.S., and long practice has
made him proof against its effects. It is a
strange, dingy complaint, aggravated by a dreary
ache in the waistband, which ache is somewhat
relieved by the eating of earth and miscellaneous
rubbish. In one street of Cairns—the name of
the street is given as Sandy Gallows —there is said
to be one or more patients in nearly every house.
1 Ankylostomiasis isn’t hard to cure nowadays, but
until recently it was set down on the world’s list
of hopeless diseases. The trouble is that very
few doctors are acquainted with it, and in those
parts of Australia where it is most plentiful doc-
tors are scarce. Also, a disease which is chiefly a
tired feeling is often set down as something that
doesn’t require a doctor.

The Inky Way.
The proprietors of Cooper’s sheep-dip have forwarded
an intimation that they are offering three prizes (one of
£5O, one of £25, and one of £3O) “for the three best
papers on the blowfly trouble.” Tiie Bulletin can t
think for the moment which are the best papers on the
blowfly trouble. But, if anybody is offering prizes for
the three best papers on the stinking-fish trouble, it will
guarantee to supply the list by return mail.
■¥■ * * *
Melbourne is to have a quarterly magazine, the Aus-
tralian Onlookkr. It is to be under the auspices of Mel-
bourne’s struggling literary societies, and aims at reveal-
ing new talent of the lighter academic order and widening
tiie circle of “ culchaw.” It will have a first subscription
list of 750 at 2s. 6d„ and an issue of 1000 copies in all.
The scheme is modest enough. The Australian On-
looker will be a 20-page sheet, one inside page only
beinj** reserved for advertisements of a harmonious char-
acter. All services are to lie honorary, and an office—at
1!) Queen-street, Melbourne —is provided gratis by Mr.
Frank Russell, the business manager. All going well, the
infant, will come to light before Christmas.
* * * *
“ O. N. Lines ” : Salaries for press reporting in Sydney
are going down, and instead of every senior staffman
receiving at least £6 as formerly, the wages have been
carved into until very few men, indeed, are on that mark.
This is due entirely to the lack of competition. To all
intents and purposes the Herald and Telegraph (the
two big morning papers) are a replica of one another.
Occasionally one or other gets a scoop,,but as a general
thing the news in one paper is the news in another. The
papers are understaffed, and if Blank on the Telegraph
finds himself on the duty book for twice as
many engagements as he can cover, he hunts up Hash on
the Herald who is up for at least one or two of
the same jobs, and who, you can bet your life, is
looking hard for Blank. Supposing it is civil court
work, and Blank has four or five or six courts.
Naturally he cannot do them all. Dash is in a similar
predicament; so probably is Blast ou the News and Hang
on the Sun. Therefore an arrangement is come to,
whereby all the jobs are split up and the “copy ” pooled.
It is a dangerous proceeding, and each man in the pool
carries his job in his hand —for a libel geneialli
means Out, and it doesn’t look vyell for four papers
to have the same libel. A leading paper will send
to an important set of jobs a youngster who could
no more do them than he could swing the Deni
round by the tail. But his boss knows very well that lie’ll
get his copy from the pool; and so he does. In the
circuit there is bound to be one or two competent men,
and they take on the difficult work while the incom-
petents'take work that is of less consequence, and
that can be thrown overboard if time doesn’t per-
mit of verification. But the pool system, like all
other dodges, has a limit, and the limit is just
about due. Youngsters are being shoved into the places
of senior men as vacancies occur, and are being paid £2,
£2 10s. or less, and gradually the remaining strong men
who have to carry them are bending to breaking point
under the weight. Presently there will not be enough
stron”' men to go round, and then the pool system will
suddenly crack right down the middle, and the subs on
the big dailies will howl in vain for the day’s “copy.”
The N.S.W. Institute of Journalists has been blamed for
not rising on its hoofs and fighting the cheap-ieporter
trouble, but personally I don’t think the Institute can do
anvgood. The only sure and decisive way to end it is
for the competent men to come to an agreement not to
swap copy with a living writer on another paper. That
will simultaneously kill stone-dead the cheap, incom-
petent reporter and the under-staffing in the various
offices.
« * * *
“ Yarriambiak ”: Some time ago, the Institute of
Journalists in Adelaide set to work to try to improve
the conditions of its members—perhaps the most over-
worked and underpaid lot of pressmen in the Common-
wealth. A committee to draw up a wages log was
appointed, and the old foolish idea that it was infra dig.
for a journalist to have anything to do with trades
unionism or collective action seemed firmly and finally
dead. Unfortunately, it was merely snoozing. In due
time the committee presented a suggested log, and a
special meetinginstructed thesecretary tofonvarditto the
proprietors of the three daily papers. Then the trouble
broke loose. The secretary didn’t forward the suggested
lo”, on theground that,subsequent to theaetion taken by
the’lnstitute, the proprietors of one paper had voluntarily
increased the salaries of their employees. This being so,
are'ued the secretary, there was need to reconsider the
matter. Incidentally, he added that he had received the
resignati >n of numerous members who objected to the
proposed action ; and, to clinch the argument, he pointed
out that the meeting which ordered the log to be
issued was only a little one. Later, a special meet-
ing was called, and theinaction of the secretary endorsed.
The probable result will be the formation of a journalists’
union, which will seek affiliation with the Typographical
Society, and already a preliminary meeting has been
held. 'The old Institute is in a parlous state. There
have been two sections ; but from now onward there will
be only one, the members of which will be able to meet
*in solemn conclave for the purpose of admiring the
manner in which they preserved the “dignity of the pro-
fession.”
* * * *
E. S. Emerson, well known to Bulletin readers as a
writer of verse and' stories, goes west to edit the
Westralian Worker, being the successful applicant
among a scoye or more aspirants. Emerson has a firm grip
of things Australian, and should do good work in clearing
the stumps in'tiie Bigjobn Forrest State. He recently
completed an up-to-date opera, which, from latest ad vices,
promiseswell for acceptance in London ; and he has hopes
of later on singing praises of the Australian bush in
England for a spell.
B * -*- * * *
The arrival of'the Adelaide Labor daily has shaken up
the other papers. Twelve months ago an important
union meeting got three or four lines of nonpareil, and it
didn’t matter where the stuff was dropped; but now
each daily has its special industrial expert, who prac-
ticallv lives at the Trades Hall, and no item is so small
or swift that it can dodge the printer. Nothing exceeds
like excess.
THE VERSE-MOULDER'S REQUEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
110 ! for ihe bold, brine-crusted tar !
Give him a stave of cheer. —Grant Ilervey.
Here’s to the iron-welder stark !
A verse to hearten him on.—Grant Ilervey.
Grant, I would take it kindly if, next time
You feel inspired to swell the mighty list
Of toilers whom you’ve dowered with a rhyme,
You would, so far as I’m concerned, desist.
Far be it from the writer to be rude
Or callously indifferent to Art ;
Yet, Grant, you strike me as a trifle crude,
For all your vast, warm, throbbing human heart.
I’ve toiled behind your bounding Pegasus,
Past Clapham, Coogee and the Celebes ;
I’ve chased him through the sparkling Ilissus
(Near Athens, Grant), and scaled the Pyrenees
(I mean, of course, in spirit) in his tracks.
Mosman I’ve visited, and Samarcand.
(This class of verse all difficulty lacks
Provided one’s an atlas close at hand.)
And ever, as I tore from place to place,
Amid a ceaseless orash of proper names
And wild, loose talk of Home and early Thrace
And Babylon and Hell and Smithfield's flames,
I asked : “ Why all this verbiage ? Why say
In twenty weird, recondite words what we,
Who are not Grants, can set forth any day,
Without the slightest strain, in two or three?”
Answered a voice at last: “ Old man, it’s hard
To realise you have so little sense ;
He’s paid by space —so many coins a yard,
And rhymes expansively in consequence.”
And then I knew. . . . Yet still I make my plea :
Do not accord me, Grant, a stave of cheer.
Immunity from that is worth, to me,
More than the price of breweries of beer.
N.S.W.
SINGEMOINK THE VERSE-MOULDER.

THE LONE HAND offers you the Glad
Hand. It’s the Right Hand, and a week
after publication there are none left.
Private Enterprise, in the form of “ a deputation
representing the interests of the Korumburra,
Jninbunna and Outtrim coalfields,” came right
out into the open, last week, and asked Premier
Murray to charge the public more for the coal
won at the Powlett State coal-mine, or to go slow
and give the public less coal. It was represented
variously that the Private Enterprise coal busi-
ness was being prejudiced horribly by the State
concern ; that the State had a right, by charging
higher prices, to make bigger profits than at
present; that it was the Government’s duty to
conserve its coal and refrain from tearing it
out with indecent haste; that, as a matter of
common justice to the vested coal interests, the
furthest the Government should go in the coal
business was to supply the needs of the State rail-
ways ; that the Powlett mine wasn’t going to last
for ever ; that when it started down hill, ruin and
desolation would smite Victoria—and a lot more
to the same effect. Verbiage apart, it was obvi-
ously the fixing of the price of Powlett coal at
14s. a ton that agitated the coal-owners. The vast
disproportion between that figure and the price
charged by Private Enterprise was whathurried the
deputation to the feet of Murray. And what stu-
pendous impudence its point of view betrays !
There would be considerable uproar if a deputation
of bullockies suggested to Murray that the train
freights should be levelled up to their charges,
and the train-speeds levelled down to the gradual
gait of their conveyances. Yet such a suggestion
would be no more unreasonable than that the
State of Victoria should enter into a sort of com-
bine or vend with a number of private mine-
owners to keep the price of coal high and the
output low. Murray gave scant encouragement
to the deputation. No Premier who didn’t want
to be identified with the views of the Back Ages
could have done anything else.

Laura Merry Wurm was a party in a law-
suit in Adelaide, last week. And Merry was a
widow as well as a Wurm.
Many people in Britain desire a new title
to describe their King and Emperor, George V.
It is objected that the present designation is too
long, uncertain and cumbersome; so something
with the virtues of brevity and compnehensive-
ness is loudly called for. Suggestions aro already
tumbling in, and George has been variously ad-
vised to style hiniself “ His Majesty the
Monarch,” “Arch-Emperor,” “Emperor of the
British,” etc., etc. This last title is noisily sup-
ported by various patriots; but it doesn’t go far
enough. It is too insular and exclusive for s\
ruler of an Empire of so many tints. Why not
“King of the British and Emperor of the
Shades”?
J. R. Y. Goldstein, a Melbourne person-
age, has just resigned.the flesh. The deceased,
who had passed his seventieth year, was known
as an amateur soldier and a professor of charity.
He joined Victoria’s army in the dying ’6o’s
and retired when the ’9o’s were getting
wrinkled. As a charity expert, he founded in
Victoria the Leongatha Labor Colony and the
Convalescent Aid Society for Men. Also, he
dipped a finger in the control of the Women’s
Hospital and the Hospital of the League of
Mercy, Melbourne. But those labors for society
seem paltry beside his deeds in a parental
capacity; for he was the father of the. Goldstein
family, which includes Miss Vida, the political
climber, and Mrs. H. H. Champion, the wife of
the journalist and social evolutionary.
“Phil Harum”: Be “Gil” and the
bugles blown by the coachdrivers of the Never-
Never (B. 8/9/TO). May I rise, to remark that
there was precious little romance about the
brass with which the coachmen of the bad old
days woke • the echoes? They simply tooted
their “horns” because a paternal Postal Depart-
ment put it in the bond. Why, only some
departed postal autocrat ■ could tell, and he’s got
something else to think about. But bugles were
not always forthcoming. On one occasion com-
plaint was made that the driver of the Thargo-
mimjah-Oontoo mail did not carry the necessary
brass implement. _lh.e._ Department insisted.
The contractor-owra-driver wired the Brisbane
head office : “ Bugle not procurable ; will any
other musical instrument do?” Apparently
under the impression that a brass instrument was
meant, apd know’ing that bugles didn’t grow on
every wayside mulga, the Department replied
“Yes.” When the coach reached Oontoo next trip
the crowd around Costello’s came forward to help
themselves as usual; but the driver waved them
back with a flourish : “ Wait a minit, boys, till I
give ye a bit of the bugle.” Then producing a
jew’s-harp, he solemnly thrummed out “ Pop
Goes the Weasel ” before handing the bags over.
“ A.H.” : Concerning the value of King
Sound in the Far Nor’-West, as a naval base,
“G.R.F.” (B. 15/9/TO) admits the difficulty ships
might have to get into it, but declares that should
be one of its recommendations. Why ? Apart
from other troubles there is a tidal rise of from 30ft.
to 40ft. up that part of the coast. To make the
harbor at all vessels must take the tide fairly full
—even vessels of medium and light draught.
Taking it that a naval base means not only
graving docks but provision and coal stores, also
a centre from which strategic moves could be
made, or to which a safe retreat could be easily
effected, how would such a locality answer, say,
in case of hasty retreat, with hot pursuit, when
the tide didn’t happen to be full? Unless the
fugitives reached the Sound at the right moment
they would sLiply have to lie outside, say, for 15
or 20 hours, waiting their opportunity, or court
disaster by trying to cross reefs, shoals and sand
bars at-low water. It is all right to have a base
which is difficult for the other fellow to get into ;
but surely you want to be able to get into it your-
self without breaking your neck.
One of those cases in which your true
lawyer simply revels has come to a temporary end,
so to speak, in the Victorian Supreme. Court. In
1906 the Geelong Harbor Trust bought two
second-hand dredges—the Walrus and the Octopus
—from the Natal Government. The Octopus
speedily started on the road to D. Jones’s locker,
and went to pieces on the S’African coast. The
Walrus left Durban on Aug. 13, and was never
afterwards heard of. The glorified scoop was in-
sured for £12,000 with the Maritime Insurance Co.,
but the co. declined to pay up on the grounds
that a different route was taken from the one
specified, that the scoop was overloaded with
coal, and that it was generally unseawortliy.
The Trust went to law, and for 39 days various
expensive barristers argued, and expert witnesses
droned, and exhibits of models of dredges and
parts of dredges, hoppers, false and true bottoms,
and other abstruse things, were handed round.
Then the jury pondered for two days and con-
sidered the 13 questions put to it, and the 14 sub-
questions, and finally decided that the insurance
co. ought to part up the £12,000. The co. is
understood to be looking towards a higher court;
but it isn’t rushing the matter. It has noted that
the Costs have already reached £20,000, and is
probably trying to figure out what height they are
capable of reaching.

“E.8.D.” on the Nyngan (N.S.W.) im-
provement leases (B. 25/8/’10): —
I agree that a pale-blue odor invests the very name of
improvement lease ; but there are times when it is wise
to let lands under that tenure. The Nyngan blocks were
lands of the sort. This land has been open for selection
ever since there has been such a thing as selection ; but
neither Fat nor the land-starved men butted in to get it.
Further, the land has been offered on improvement lease
no less than four times without commanding a bid. The
fifth time the price was reduced, and then the compe-
tition was not between the landless, but between parties
already holding big areas. One block, however—the
nearest to the railway and the most suitable (or, rather,
the least unsuitable) for a small settler—is still open.
But, simply because I am a small-settlement advocate,
I shouldn’t advise any land-hungry little man to take it.
He might just as well commit suicide at once and save
himself a lot of details.
From an Australian in the innards of
Russia:—
To-day (August 2) I read in a Russian paper that it is
probable Westralia will give a large piece of land to settle
1,000,000 Russian Jews. If Westralia does any such
foolish thing the whole country will pay through the
nose for it. I have travelled in Russia now for four
years and eight months, largely in the Jewish parts. I
have lived with these people, worked with them—and
beep cheated by them. I have seen no Jews on the land,
and if they were given land in Australia they would
quickly go off it and drift into the towns, to ruin the
Australian shopkeepers. South Russian villages are
almost all owned by Jews. As soon as they drive out
the Christian shopkeepers, they form small trusts and
raise prices, and so the work of fleecing the country goes
on. With that comes wholesale corruption and bribery,
two of the pests of the country ; and patriotic Russians
blame the Russian brand of Jew for most of it.

Rugby Union Football Competition. Champions and Unbeaten, 1910.
Newtown Rugby Union Football Team.
ROYAL BLUE PENNANT FLIES HIGH.
The Rugby Union season ended Saturday, 17th inst., Newtown having gone through
unbeaten. They are accordingly unconquered premiers, an honor which has not fallen
to any other club since the district system was introduced 10 years ago. -
It will therefore be interesting to the general public, as it was gratifying to the Pro-
prietors of Rexona, to read the following testimonial given unanimously by every member
of the team. The letter speaks for itself, and shows in what high esteem this premier
team holds the new Rapid Healer Rexona. They write as follows :
The Manager, Rexona, Sydney.
Dear Sir,—The whole of our Football Team are so pleased with the
benefit we have derived from the use of Rexona, the Rapid Healer, that we
have unanimously decided to give you a testimonial regarding same. We
have used it for numerous purposes in our training and after our matches,
both as an embrocation and as a healer for the various cuts and sores
incidental to football.
This has been a most successful year with us, and we certainly feel to a
considerable extent that we owe our success to the great benefit which we
have derived from the use of Rexona, the Rapid Healer.
A number of the boys have also used it for Chilblains, Sore Feet and
Chapped Hands, and in every instance have found it to be an excellent
remedy.
With best wishes for Rexona.
We are, very truly yours,
(Signed) ERNEST E. BOOTH,
Captain Newtown R.U. Football Team.
Also signed by the other members of the team.
Football players everywhere will find Rexona of the greatest use in the training room. It is
invaluable as a rub-down for strained and tired' muscles, and will rapidly heal all the many cuts and
bruises acquired in the football field. If you have not already tried Rexona, send us your name and
address, and we will send by return post a sample pot gratis.

The Cable’s Requiem.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE served the Master surely, lying low
beneath the sea—
Lying deep, a thousand fathoms ’neath the
thunder of the screws ;
He hath set me here to labor where the lost
Armadas be,
And he knows that well I serve him in the depths
of silent ooze !
For the Master’s ways are secret, and his purpose
strideth far—
Very far beyond the knowing of the cable deep
and dim ;
Though he harness moon and planet, though he
drive the bitted star,
He shall hear the pulsing heart-beat in my ocean-
requiem !
In the long ago the Master set me here to serve
him well-
Ah, the years of faithful labor ’neath the booming
of the tide ! .
I have taken forth his orders—ships of battle on
the swell,
They have swung as I have whispered—see the
great steel monsters ride.
Now the Master’s brain hath fashioned servants
new to do his needs,
And I hear his orders flashing far and wide above
the sea;
I have been both true and loyal—o ye choirs of
jarring creeds,
Have ye served him half as truly as I serve with
bended knee?
Ye have heard the armies marching, heard the
crash of shotted guns—
’Twas the echo of the message that I flashed from
shore to shore;
Forth from Sitka steams the sealer, but my trail
of service runs
Out of Brisbane to Vancouver, from Madras to
Singapore !
I am trailing out of Sydney—through Magellan’s
straits I go,
And I murmur soft from Darien ’neath the sum-
; mer Caribbees ;
Where the coal-ships bring their cargoes I am
leaving Callao;
Valparaiso hears me calling as I sing beneath the
seas !
It is good, in days of passing, simply this to always
know—
How the hand was to the labor, how the soul had
strength to dare ;
Neath the wire-leashed North Atlantic I am
singing as I go—
-1 hough New York may never hear me, still my
song shall proudly fare !
Roar of streets and crowded cities, angry voices
' of the slum—
Ihese are hushed, and none shall hear them
where I croon beneath the sea ;
But when Man-the-Master’s Master beats his
mighty judgment-drum,
I shall raise my Song of Service—are ye singing,
souls, with me ?
Who hath served the Master surely ?—lying here
beneath the sea,
Lying deep a thousand fathoms, I have heard the
beating screws ;
One hath set ye here to labor—with the Lost Things
I shall be,
But the Master knows my service in the darkened
depths of ooze!
Aye, the Master's ways are secret, and he drives the
bitted star
Very far beyond our knowing as we touch His
garment's hem ;
Yet I know that Work is worthy, and when all
things silent are
Mill my deep-sea psalm shall echo—God shall hear
my requiem 1
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
In Vain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
UNDER the evening glare of the
tyrannous tropic sun—dipping huge and
crimson towards the western sea-line—
stood out in ruddy glow the piled-up flat-topped
houses of the island town. In the background, a
low green stretch of mainland was already clothing
itself with ghastly evening vapors ; and, away
to the north, Cape Verde—Africa’s farthest
reach-out into the Atlantic—lay like a faint
streak of indigo dividing sea from sky.
The parting sim
Beyond the earth’s green cape and verdant isles
Hespereati sets.
Milton’s lines about that actual locality came to
Halsbury’s mind as, leaning upon the yaght’s
bridge-rail, he swept the scene with a binocular.
“So, that’s Goree,” he said, presently ; “ the
little island that has by turns been Dutch, Eng-
lish, Dutch again, French, English again, French
a second time, English once more—and, Anally,
French.”
“That’s Goree right enough,” said the skipper
of the Dream ; “ though I didn’t know the place
had been chucked to an’ fro like that. But
it's French now, at all events; and, for the job
we’ve got in hand, I wish it was ’most anything
else.”
Halsbnry—a middle-aged, middle-statured man,
with keen blue eyes and a tawny moustache—put
down the glasses. “ Why is that ?” he said.
Captain Bunster tugged at his short, grizzled
beard. “ Well, sir,” lie said, “ it’s this way.
Wherever, you find the Parlyvoo you find a
sickenin' lot of reg’lation, an’ red-tape, an’ Jack-
in-office, an* askin’ of questions, an’ signin’ of
papers, an’ all that rigmarole.”
“Well?” asked Halsbury, finding that his
skipper paused awkwardly.
“ Well, sir, I take it that we don’t exac’ly want
to have any spyin’ an’ pryin’ into what we’re here
for. The game is square enough, I make no
doubt.”,
“ If you’ve any misgiving on that point,” Hals-
bury began hastily; but the skipper held up his
hand. “I haven’t, sir—not a doubt that every-
thing’s real reg’lar an’ shipshape, from your
point of view. But, you see, what we’ve got to
consider is what the, officials here will think we’re
after if we don’t go into harbor, an’ what we’re
to say of ourselves if we do go in.”
“Where is the harbor? I don’t see any.”
“ Round on the other side of the island—such
as it is.”
“Well, never mind that; I don’t intend to go
in.”
“ All the better, sir; because, if they didn’t
like che account we gave of ourselves, they’d clap
a party of soldiers aboard, right away.”
“ Soldiers !—in that footy little place ? ” Hals-
bury stared at the town, all grey and dim now,
save where the sun-glow still lingered on the
castle of St. Michael, that tops the southern end
of the island.
“Soldiers? Yes, sir—plenty of ’eiq. Goree’san
important military post, or the Frenchies think
it is, at all events. And if we rang-off our engines
now, we’d have a guard-boat out to us in a jiffy,
late as it is.”
“Well, what do you propose, Captain Buns-
ter?”
“ I was thinkin’, sir, that, as it’s only about 45
miles down the coast to Bathurst, at the mouth
of the Gambia, we’d better stand-on as if we were
maltin’ for there. About midnight you want to
be off here, you say ? ”
“ Yes ; midnight or after.”
“Very good ; I’ll time her accordin’ly.”
The Dream stood on to the south in the gather-
ing darkness, and presently Halsbury said : “If
you’ll come below now, captain, we’ll have another
look at those directions I showed you. Won’t do
to make a mistake in the darkness.”
Gently, through the gloom of the early middle-
watch, the Dream slipped over the oily swell
towards the dark and silent town. Not an air-
breath stirred, and only here and there a star
showed faintly through the cloudy canopy.
“Almost too dark,” the yacht’s owner said to
her skipper, as they stood together on the bridge.
“ If we miss the exact spot we may spoil all.”
“Oh, that’ll be all right,” Bunster said, cheer-
fully. “I’ve got the directions well hammered
into my noddle, and I’ve got night heyes like a
howl. I’ll engage to put you right on the mark.”
“Very good,” Halsbury said—“then we’ll
chance it. And, in case anything should happen
to either of us—for there may be some danger in
the matter—l’ll answer now the question you have
never asked. What lam sneaking ashore here
for, at dead of night, is to bring away my
brother.” The skipper gave a low whistle of
astonishment. “My younger brother, who has
been for years in the indigo and ivory trade in
Dakar, on the mainland over there. Ever hear
of Stephen Halsbury and Co. when you were a
mate in this African trade?”
“No, sir; buu you see I was mostly runnin’ to
Fernando Po an’ the Congo. This place was off
our beat.”
“Well, Steve was prospering here—prospering
too much, in fact; for it was business jealousy
that first made things uncomfortable for him in
Dakar. Then came that Fashoda affair which got
him so intensely disliked, as an Englishman, that
he made up his mind to sell out and leave for
Europe. But here he was blocked. He had hap-
pened to get wind of a very fishy contract-trans-
action that some of the principal local function-
aries were mixed up in, and they arranged things
So that, when Steve happened to be over here in
Goree, he was prevented from leaving the island ;
and he is still detained there, under surveillance
mililaire, on suspicion of Lord-knows-what. Only
a dodge, of course, to keep him from getting to
Europe for the present. He might find his way
to Paris, you see, and make inconvenient com-
munications to the Bureau des Affaires Coloniales.
But, through the medium of a friendly Portuguese
merchant, he contrived to cable the situation to
his London agents. And, as he’s too closely
watched to escape otherwise, it was arranged that
I should come down here in this swift little hooker,
and get away with him on the quiet. He was to
hold himself in readiness every night from the
beginning of the present month ; and here we are
only four days behind that.”
“ I see, sir. Rummy fix to be in ; but it fits in
well with what I said about the Parlyvoos. But
all this must have meant a lot of cablin’, an’ I
can’t make out how the Froggies didn’t guess
there was some such game on.”
“The cables weren’t to my brother, man .' They
were to his Portuguese friend—and in code, of
course.”
“Oh, I see. Well, sir, I’ll be gettin’ a boat
over the side, an’ havin’ muffles put on the oars.
Real old-fashioned, naval cuttin’-out smack about
that kind o’ thing.”
Ten minutes later the Dream’s boat, with four
oars gently paddling, had crept in close under the
sea-wall, about midway on its western frontage.
“ Handsomely, now, men—not the ghost of a
splash ! ” the skipper said in a loud whisper as he
kept the boat away to the right, along the wall,
peering intently upwards as they went. Hardly
200 yards had been noiselessly traversed when
through the darkness loomed up above the wall a
barely distinguishable lofty mass of masonry.
The skipper put his mouth to Halsbury’s ear.
Got our bearin’s easy enough,” he whispered.
“ That’s the tower we were to make first; the
openin’ won’t be far away.”
In a couple of minutes more they arrived at a
low arch in the wall, beneath which the dark
water passed into still greater obscurity. It was
fairly wide ; but, as they turned softly into it, the
heave of the swell nearly brought their heads
against the stonework above. Inside, even Cap-
tain B'unster’s boasted “howl’’-like night vision
was hard put to it to discern that they were in a
short waterway of about five yards wide, ending
in a flight of steps that mounted to a small door
in the wall that faced the entrance and closed the
alley. Slowly the boat was brought to the foot of
the steps, and Bunster, with a whispered caution
to the men,, got gently out. Halsbury followed
him, and like shadows the two stole cautiously
away up the steps.
For about 10 minutes, then, all was still, and
the boat’s crew began to get uneasy. The bow-
oar had remarked in a hoarse whisper to No. 2
that it was “divilish dhrary worrk waitin’ there
in the darrk widout as much as a dhraw of the
poipe ” ; and No. 2 was in the act of Teutonically
replying that “Py Gott, it vas all von tarn pig
biece of voolishness !”—when three figures came
quickly and quietly down the steps. Two stepped
noiselessly into the boat, but the third—Captain
Bunster—stumbled as he stepped over the gunnel,
and instantly upon the hollow sound came from
above the challenge of a sentry.
“Pull gently to the entrance, men,” snapped
out the skipper in a half-whisper, “and then
give way for all you’re worth !”
A second time caine the quick “ Qui va la?”
followed almost instantly by the sharp bark of a
rifle. The bullet “ clumped ” into the water a
few feet in front of the boat; then, as she moved
ahead, another smote the wall in her rear; but,
unscathed, the party reached the exit. “Now’
you sinners! ” hissed the skipper as the boat
cleared the archway, “ put your backs into it like
Britqns!” The “Britons”—a Paddy, two
“Dutchmen,” and a Dago—bent manfully to
their oars, and in a few sconds the boat was flash-
ing to seaward over the sullen water. Literally
“ flashing,” for a brilliant phosporescence splashed
out under the oar-strokes and trailed sparkling
away astern, making a fair mark, and bringing
the bullets “clip-clipping” all round the craft.
. “Magazine-rifles, by Jiminy!” said the skipper.
“We’ll be lucky if we get aboard without any
holes in us ! ”
They were lucky to that extent; for, though
the oars were several times struck, the only bullet
that came aboard passed between the forward
oarsmen and went out through the bow-planldng.

starboard-light, they were soon alongside, and the
moment they had scrambled on board the boat
was run up to the davits and the engines were
rung on “ Full speed ahead.” But, as the little
steamer gathered way, the newoomer had a word
to say.
“If I might advise, I’d put her head to the
southward, so as to make them think we’re making
for Bathurst, or somewhere down there. Then,
when we’ve run a couple of miles or so, we can
make a good offing and head away north again.”
“Why so?” asked the other Halsbury. “What
does it matter which way they think we’ve gone?”
“ Because, if they see by—what do you call the
right-hand side? —oh, starboard—if they see by
our starboard light that we’ve gone northward,
they’ll cable to St. Louis; and, as there’s a
cruiser there at present, she’d probably try to cut
us off.”
“Very good,” said the yacht’s owner. “ She’d
have to be a mighty speedy craft, certainly, to
overhaul the Dream ; but it’s as well to take no
chances. Put her about at once, captain, will
you ? Of course, we could dowse all the glims, but
it's better to let them see our port light moving
southward.”
Bound swept the vessel to her helm, leaving the
curve of her wake marked out in shining seethe
upon the dark water, and hardly was her sharp
nose pointed south again when a fresh burst of
firing from the walls told them that the red port
light was a much more conspicuous object than
had beeu the starboard glimmer of green. Some
of the bullets fell pretty close, and one spanked
hard against the plating of the quarter; then, as
the yaeht got fairly into her speed, Goree slipped
away astern like a dream.
The brothers and the skipper went below for a
refresher. “ Well, gentlemen,” Bunster said, as
he raised his tumbler, “we pulled that off all
right. I congratilate you both ” —and down
wont the libation.
Stephen Halsbury nodded a stiff acknowledg-
ment. He was a lean, brown, clean-shaven man,
lo iking older than the elder brother. “ What is
slie doing now ? ” he asked, cocking his ear to the
swift rush of water past the sides.
“A good 18,” the skipper said, complacently.
An’ I could screw another knot an’ a half out of
her on a pinch.”
“Hum! say 20 knots, then, at the outside. I
wonder how We’d stand for speed with the
Joanne d ? j|rc?”
“ The cruiser you spoke of ? ” asked his brother.
“Yes. She’s a new vessel, and she’s credited
with, 42 kilometres an hour—whatever that may
mean in knots.”
“Soon tell you that.” The elder Halsbury
took out notebook and pencil and sat down at
the cabin table. He figured for a minute or so
—then looked up with a shake of the head. “It
works out at nearly 24 knots,” he said. “Of
course, that may be only her measured trial
record ; but, even with a knot or so off, she’d
still have the heels of us. But, at the worst,
Steve, what can they do? You’re a British sub-
ject in a British ship. They can’t touch you
now.”
“It won’t do to count too much on that,”
Stephen Halsbury answered. “ The Prefect of
Goree-Sakar will be shaking in his shoes with
terror of iny getting to Europe with what I know
about his little financial games—quite enough to
lose him his post; if not to land him and others
in prison. So lie’s pretty sure to send over the
cable some charge, serious enough to justify the
cruiser in seizing me in the meantime. It could
be made out afterwards to be a mistake; and,
when the Prefect’s term of office is over, he
needn’t trouble about me.”
The other Halsbury turned to the skipper.
“What do you think, captain, about standing
well out tovyards the Cape Verde Islands as we
run up again ? That would slip this cruiser, if he
should happen to be on the prowl for us.”
It was thus agreed upon ; and wlion, half an
hour later, the yacht was once more put about;
the course given to the quarter-master at the
wheel was nor’-west by west. “That’s dead on
for Boavista,” the skipper said ; “and we’ll keep
it till we sight the island—a good 300 miles of an
offing—before we lay her head nor’-east for home.”
**' * *
The Prefect of Goree-Sakar didn’t take any
risks. “ This accursed Englishman,” he said to
himself, “will seek to be revenged. It is to
Europe—to Paris—he will go with his-informa-
tion. This heading southward at first was of
course but a blind ; it would be childish to put
any faith in that."
So the cable spoke to St. Louis, 120 miles up
the coast; with the result that at daybreak next
morning the Jeanne d’ Arc was steaming out of
the Senegal River, to look out for “a steam yacht,
about 200 tons ; white, blue stripe ; black-topped
white funnel; two pole-masts, very raking”—a
precision of information that showed what good
use the Goree observers had made of their eyes
during the passage southward, at sunset, of the
vessel suspected of having returned in the dark-
ness for the levanted Englishman. And the
cruiser’s captain, knowing what he himself would
have done had he been in charge of the fugitive
vessel, headed straight out to sea, in the hope of
intersecting the nor’-westerly course which he cor-
rectly judged the yacht would be taking.
And thus it was that next day, a little after
noon, the Dream’s skipper detected on the eastern
horizon a faint feather of smoke, which he watched
intently until he convinced himself that it was
nearing rapidly. He hastened to the engine-
room voice-tube. “Give her every pound of
Steam you can, Mac,” he called down. “She’ll
*»ave to put her best foot foremost now, or
I’m confoundedly mistook ! Yes, sir,” Bunster
answered then to Halsburv’s inquiring look ; “ I
Relieve it’s that cussed cruiser. From the rate
that smoke’s risin’ on us, there must be a tremen-
dously fast vessel under it. She’s coinin’ right
west-awav, too ; as if they’d dropped to our little
game. But we ought to rise Boavista in a couple
of hours ; an’, if we can once get into Portuguese
waters, we can laugh at him. I wouldn’t have
any doubt about that, if it was a dead-on s'tarn
chase; but you see were runnin’ on the slope,
like.”
One of the speediest things of her tonnage
afloat, the Dream was now slicing through it
little short of 20 knots,' so that from her knife-
like “entrance” a foamy swirl of water rose
high as the rail on either side, and trailed away
far out astern. Yet still the smoke in the east
rose higher and higher; and soon the glass re-
moved all doubt about the kind of vessel it came
from.
“He’s cornin’ up hand-over-fist,” the skipper
said. “It’s time we were gettin’ you stowed
away, Mr., HJalsbury. There’s Boavista only just
peepin’up now, so we’d be overhauled long be-
fore-we touched the three-mile limit. By Jiminy,
that chap’s a flier, and no mistake ! Now, sir,
come bjelow, an’ I’ll fix you up where no bloomin’
Johnny All-taut will be likely to search, what-
ever a Custom House weasel might do if he was
pokin’ around for smuggled cigars.”
The fugitive followed the skipper below, and
five minutes later Bunster came on deck again
just as a jet of snowy smoke sprang from the
ctuiser’s forecastle and a heavy thud shook the
air. Bunster put his hand on the engine-room
telegraph and looked inquiringly at Halsbury.
“We can’t possibly get away, I suppose? ” the
latter asked.
“No chance, sir. That gun was unshotted ;
but we’d better ring off before he takes to puttin’
iron pills in our prescription ! ”
Halsbury nodded ; the telegraph spoke, and
the Dream began slowly to lose her way. Pre-
sently, with engines reversed, she was checked to
a dead stop, while the cruiser came surging along
at actual railroad speed, and in ten minutes
had ranged up, about a cable’s length away, on
the yacht’s starboard beam. She was long, low
and black, with white upper-works; three yellow
funnels, a single “military” mast forward, and
the tricolor flaunting from a staff astern. With
naval celerity a quarter-boat was dropped and
manned, and a couple of minutes later a sous-
lieutenant and half a dozen seamen scrambled on
board the Dream.
Politely the officer saluted. “Vat sheep ees
dees 1 '? -’’-ffrejasked.
“The Dream,” Bunster answered, “of London,
and/or London, from a pleasure cruise.”
The Frenchman smiled knowingly'. “ Plaisure
cruise?” he said. “Zen you ’ave passenjair, eh ?
But, firrst, I mus’ see your papier.”
“ I must refer you to my howner, munsheer,”
the skipper said, with a wave of his cap towards
Halsbury. “He is the honly passenger we have
on board.”
“We decline to produce the ship’s papers to an
officer of a foreign navy, sir,” Halsbury said,
coming forward. “ And I wish to know by what
authority you stop, in time of peace, a vessel fly-
ing the British flag?”
The Frenchman smiled and nodded, as who
should say : “Quite right! Of course, you pro-
test —that was only to be expected.” But he
knew his business. “Sare,” he said, politely,
“ v is autorite I ’ave nossing to make—to do, 1
mean. Merely I obey ze orrdares ; an’ I ’ave not
no times to loss—so ’ave ze goodness to mustare
all your peoples immediatement.”
“ Not a man, sir ! ” Halsbury returned, firmly.
“ Zen I mus’ sairsh ye sheep.”
“ Under the guns of your vessel, sir, we cannot
prevent you from doing so. But this is an out-
rage of which more will be heard.”
Halsbury turned on his heel, and the officer
gave an order to his men, who immediately scat-
tered fore and aft to search the vessel.
For fully half an hour they rummaged below,
without finding anybody at all answering to the
description upon which the officer was acting.
Then the man left in the boat alongside swung
himself hastily on board to say that the cruiser
had signalled recall—“ Squall coming.”
Captain. Bunster had already noted that away
to the sou’-Vest there was rapidly rising a bank
of lead-colored cloud, shot with strange, greenish
reflections, and margined at the horizon by a
scarcely-seen white line.
“By Jiminy ! ” exclaimed the skipper, “ it’s a
chubasco bianco, sure enough. That big fellow
needn’t mind it much, but it’ll be a lot above our
weight if it catches us fair. I think I can dodge all
but the skirt of it, though, by keepin’ full-speed
ahead as we were goin’.” He had his hand on
the telegraph, and the moment the Frenchmen
were in their boat he rang on, and the yacht was
soon dashing ahead again towards Boavista,
showing now like a faint brown smudge on the
nor’-western horizon.
The chubasco was travelling nor’-easterly, but
so widely did it open out as it came that Bunster
had everything made fast and battened down.
“Lucky if we don’t get more than the edge of
it,” he said, as the fringe of the coming darkness
reached the yacht, and the racing foam-ridge
gleamed higher and whiter against the sullen
blackness behind. Then, suddenly, the deep
humming note that had filled the air rose to a
piercing, tearing shriek, and the Dream—racing
desperately to clear the western edge of the mad
wind-rush—was hove-down as sharply upon her
beam ends as if a giant had jerked her over by the
mast heads. Almost as quickly, however, she
righted ; and then to those on board of her was
presented the extraordinary spectacle of a furious
tumult of wind and rain and tortured sea sweep-
ing past hardly a couple of hundred yards astern,
while all further token of the disturbance that
reached the yacht was a thin spatter of giant
rajn drops.
“By Jiniiny 1” 'said the skipper, after about a
minute during which nobody spoke, “that was a
near thing!; That was a patilla —a ‘whisker,’
as the Spaniards call such puffs—that caught
us. If we had been a quarter of a mile
more to the east’ard, 10 to one Old Davy would
have been stowin’ us away in his locker by now.
I see nothin’of the Frenchman. I suppose he’s
Steamin’ away full-bat before it. Four-an’-twenty
knots will take a lot of the weight out of a
chubasco eveq.”
“Very remarkable phenomenon,” Halsbury
said, rubbing an elbow which had been badly
bruised when the sudden heel of the vessel flung
him against the starboard bulwarks. “Often
happen hereabout ? ”
“Pretty frequent about the beginnin’ of the
rains—as it is now ; though I can’t say I ever saw
one quite as clean-cut in the edge as this was.
But, God bless my soul, sir ! we’re forgettin’ your
brother.”
The skipper hurried away, and Halsbury fol-
lowed slowly, smiling at the thought that amongst
such strenuous happening he had, for the time,
quite forgotten his brother’s presence on board.
Then the smile disappeared as Bunster rushed up
on deck again, with a face as blanched as was
possible to its long-standing bronze.
“Good Lord, sir!” the skipper said-f breath-
lessly, “ don’t come down just yet. There’s been a
bit of a liaceident! That sudden heel to the
vessel”
“ Where is he?” Halsbury said, quietly. “Take
me there at once.”
Infected by the other’s calmness. Bunster
turned and led the way to the stokehold ; and
there, stretched on the deck, with the assistant-
engineer and a couple of firemen bending over
the figure, Halsbury saw it. For the moment
bis look fell upon the blankly staring eyes, the
drooping jaw, and the trickle of blood -from a
corner of the mouth, he knew that what lay
before him was it—not he.
Halsbury lifted one of the hands ; then let it
fall limply back. “How was it?” he said, briefly.
“I ’ad ’im planted in the starboard coal
bunker,” the skipper said, with what was almost
a sob; “planted beautiful, with a tarpaulin over
’im, an’ a tidy layer of coal over that, an’ a six-foot
length of hose to breathe through. But—oh
Lord, oh Lord! —when the craft heeled over
sudden she brought down a couple o’ ton
o’c&al qn top of’im. These chaps was only just
clearin’ the last of it off him when I come down.
But p’raps he ain’t dead after all, sir. I’ll run aft
an’ get ” ——
“He is dead beyond all doubt. But you are in
no way to blame, Bunster.” - Halsbury wrung the
skipper’s hand. “ You have done your best—as
I have done mine—but the Unforeseen has beaten
us.” . .
